UNRA 
Culvert Management System 4 Culvert Inventory Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 1.  DEFINITION OF INVENTORY DATA FIELDS 
1.1  CULVERT NUMBER (#) 
DESCRIPTION: This is a unique number assigned to each culvert. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 6 characters . 
 
This is a compulsory field.  It is very important t o use the correct culvert number.  This culvert 
number is the identification field in the Culvert M anagement System (CMS).  Prefix "TC" will 
be culverts which do not have an official registere d culvert number (#) yet. 
 
Examples: 
C 0 2 1 2  
C 0 5 6 2  
C 3 7 9 0 A 
C 0 0 7 8  
C 5 1 2 5  
1.2  LINK NAME OR LINK NUMBER 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the link identification of th e link name of 
the road crossing over the culvert. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 255 characte rs. 
 
Example: Abim – Kilak, Aduku – Lira, Agwata - Kachu , etc. 
 
Note: 1) Leave this field blank if the feature cros sing over the culvert is not a road. 
1.3  KM DISTANCE  
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the km position of the culver t. 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,00 and 999,99. 
 
Km distances must always refer to the permanent RMS  km reading.  This could differ from the 
km distances used during the construction phase.  F or this reason, the km distance should be 
verified during inspections. 
 
Note: If the principal feature is not a road, this item must be left blank. 
 If the km distance is unknown, it must be left bla nk. 
--- Page 9 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System 5 Culvert Inventory Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 1.4  CULVERT TYPE  
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the type of culvert. 
FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  The culvert type is described by  a two digit code 
 
01 Box culvert (in situ concrete) 
02 Box culvert (precast units) 
03 Concrete pipe culvert 
04 Corrugated metal pipe culvert 
05 Corrugated metal arch (Armco) culvert 
06 Concrete arch culvert 
98  Unknown 
 
1.5  NUMBER OF PIPES / CELLS  
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the number of spans / cells o r openings. 
FORMAT  :  Integer Field  :  Range between 1 and 99 
 
This is a compulsory field. 
 
1.6  SPAN / DIAMETER  
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the span length or diameter o f a specific 
culvert cell / opening 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,99 
 
For cell structures, the span length is measured pe rpendicular to the centre line of the culvert and 
should only be the clear width per opening. In case  of more than one type of opening, the largest 
opening should be recorded and mention should be ma de of the actual culvert size in the remarks 
table.  
 
1.7  HEIGHT  
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the height of wall or rise of  opening  
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9 
 
The height of wall is measured from the invert leve l to the soffit level, on the inlet side of the 
culvert. If the height varies significantly along t he culvert length, details thereof can be supplied 
in the remarks table.  
 
--- Page 10 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System 6 Culvert Inventory Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 1.8  GIS CO-ORDINATES (DECIMAL DEGREES) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the respective E and S co-ord inates of the 
centre point of the culvert in decimal degrees. 
FORMAT  :   Decimal Field  :  Range between 0.00000 and 99.99 999. 
 
1.9  MAINTENANCE STATION 
DESCRIPTION:  This field gives the Maintenance Station of the cu lvert.  
FORMAT  :  String  :  The Maintenance Station is a string: 
?   Unknown 
01 Kampala 
02 Mpigi 
03 Masaka 
04 Mubende 
05 Luwero 
06 Mbarara 
07 Kabale 
08 Kasese 
09 Fort Portal 
10 Hoima 
11 Masindi 
12 Lira 
13 Gulu 
14 Kitgum 
15 Moyo 
16 Arua 
17 Jinja 
18 Tororo 
19 Mbale 
20 Soroti 
21 Moroto 
22 Kotido 
 
1.10  INVENTORY PHOTOS 
A minimum of 9 inventory photos shall be taken, ie 
 
• Photo 1: General elevation photo from upstream 
• Photo 2: View from approach along centreline of dec k, in direction of increasing 
kilometres  
--- Page 11 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System 7 Culvert Inventory Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 • Photo 3: Photo of culvert number plate, or culvert number as provided on culvert, or 
alternatively of area on culvert where number is ty pically provided (even if no number is 
provided)  
• Photo 4: Underside of deck 
• Photo 5: Typical wing wall 
• Photo 6: Typical parapet / guardrail 
• Photo 7: Feature crossed 
 
Inventory photos shall have the following photo fil e names (YY refers to year, eg 15): 
 
• Photo 1: C............._YY_01.jpg   
• Photo 2: C............._YY_02.jpg 
• Photo 3: C............._YY_03.jpg 
• Photo 4: C............._YY_04.jpg 
• Photo 5: C............._YY_05.jpg 
• Photo 6: C............._YY_06.jpg 
• Photo 7: C............._YY_07.jpg 
1.11  DESIGN APPROVAL DATE 
DESCRIPTION: Use this field to indicate the year when the design  drawings 
were approved by the responsible authority.  
 
Note:  The Design Approval Date is to be given in t he format: YYYY, and can be found on the 
design drawings or ”As Built” drawings. 
1.12  DESIGN CODE 
DESCRIPTION: Use this field to indicate the Code of Procedure th at was used 
in the design of the culvert. 
FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  The Design Code is indicated by a two-digit code: 
 
01 SATCC (1988) 
02 BS5400 
03 CEB/FIP 
04 DIN 
05 CP114 
06 TMH7 (1988) 
07 BS153 
08  Uganda Bridge Code 
98 Other 
? Unknown 
--- Page 12 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System 8 Culvert Inventory Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 1.13  COMPLETION YEAR 
DESCRIPTION: Use this field to indicate the year when constructi on was 
completed.  This date can be obtained from the culv ert number (#) plates during 
inspection or from as-built drawings. 
 
Note: Use the "As Built" date on the drawings when the culvert number (#) plates are not 
available.  The completion date is to be given in t he format : YYYY. 
1.14  DATE MAJOR REHABILITATION 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the completion date of major rehabilitation 
work performed on the culvert. 
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  YYYY 
 
The date of major rehabilitation works is to be ind icated in the format YYYY.  Rehabilitation is 
defined as work aimed at upgrading the general cond ition of a culvert or the load carrying 
capacity of a culvert (e.g. widening).  Please give  a description of the rehabilitation in the block 
for additional remarks. 
1.15  DESIGN LIVE LOADING 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the characteristic live load,  as defined in 
various loading codes, for which the culvert was de signed. 
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  The type of design load is des cribed by a two digit code. 
 
01 Full M.O.T./B.M.T. - Not tested for NC 
02 Full M.O.T./B.M.T. - NC30 
03 Full M.O.T./B.M.T. - Less than NC30 
04 NA + NB24 
05 NA + NB36 (NC not tested) 
06 NA + NB36 + NC 
07 HA + HB25 08 HA + HB36 
09 HA + HB45 
10 Pedestrian only 
11 RU (rail) 
12 Uganda Bridge Code 
98  Other 
?    Unknown 
1.16  DESIGN RETURN PERIOD (YEARS) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the return period of the desi gn flood. 
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  Range between 0 and 999. 
--- Page 13 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System Page 9 Culvert Inventory Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 1.17  DESIGN FREEBOARD (M) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the freeboard corresponding to  the design 
flood for the culvert, measured from the shoulder b reak point of the road. 
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9. 
1.18  DESIGN FLOOD DISCHARGE (m 3/S) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the magnitude of the design f lood for the 
culvert. 
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  Range between 0 and 99999. 
 
1m 3/s = 1ft 3/s  x 0.3048 3 
1.19  CULVERT GRADIENT (%) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the longitudinal gradient alo ng the culvert 
invert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0 and 89,9. 
 
 
Figure 1:  Dimensions of a culvert 

--- Page 14 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System Page 10 Culvert Inventory  Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 1.20  OVERALL LENGTH (m) (‘OL’ IN FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the overall length of the cul vert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,9. 
 
For box/pipe/cell culverts the overall length is me asured parallel to the centreline of the road to 
the edges of the outer walls.  
1.21  OVERALL WIDTH (m) (‘OW’ IN FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the overall width of the culv ert 
perpendicular to the road.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,9. 
 
For box/pipe/cell culverts the overall width is mea sured perpendicular to the road.  
1.22  MINIMUM CLEAR WIDTH (m) (‘MCW’ IN FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the minimum clear width on th e road 
surface between barriers or kerbs.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,9. 
 
For box/pipe/cell culverts the minimum clear width is measured perpendicular to the road 
normally between barriers or kerbs.  If there are n o barriers or kerbs use the value equal to 
Minimum road width. 
1.23  OVERALL CELL LENGTH (m) (‘OCL’ IN FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the overall cell length of th e culvert along 
the centre line of the culvert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,9. 
 
For box/pipe/cell culverts the overall cell length is the length of the culvert measured in the 
direction of the centre line of the culvert.  It is  the length measured from inlet to outlet. 
1.24  MINIMUM ROAD WIDTH (m) (REFER FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the minimum road width perpen dicular to 
the road. 
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,9. 
 
--- Page 15 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System Page 11 Culvert Inventory  Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 For box/pipe/cell culverts the minimum road width i s measured perpendicular to the centre line 
of the road as follows: 
 
Surfaced roads: As constrained by edge of surfacing , kerbs, inner edges of guardrails or parapet 
faces. 
Gravel roads: As constrained by edge of shoulder.  
1.25  APPROACH WIDTH (m) (REFER FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the approach width.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,9. 
 
For box/pipe/cell culverts the approach road width is measured perpendicular to the centre line 
of the road as follows: 
 
Surfaced roads: As constrained between shoulder bre ak points (same as formation width). 
 
Gravel roads: As constrained between shoulder break  points (same as formation width). 
1.26  SKEW ANGLE (DEGREES) (REFER FIG 1) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the skew angle of the culvert  with respect 
to a line perpendicular to the road direction.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9. 
 
For a structure that is 90° to the centerline of th e road the angle of skew is 0°. 
1.27  MAX FILL HEIGHT (m) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the maximum height of road fi ll material 
on top of a culvert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9. 
 
The height of fill refers to the maximum fill heigh t above the culvert along the culvert length.   
1.28  MIN VERTICAL CLEARANCE (m) 
DESCRIPTION:  This is the vertical opening remaining between the  top of 
debris and the top slab of the culvert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9. 
 
--- Page 16 ---
UNRA 
Culvert Management System Page 12 Culvert Inventory  Manual 
 
Jan 2017 Revision 3  
 This is the vertical opening remaining between the top of debris and the top slab of the culvert.  
If there is no debris inside the culvert it is the same as the “MAXIMUM CELL SIZE - HEIGHT 
(m)”. 
1.29  MAXIMUM CELL SIZE - WIDTH (m) 
DESCRIPTION:  This is the width of a single cell measured perpen dicular to 
the walls of the culvert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between Range between 0, 0 and 99,9 
 
This is the width of a single cell measured perpend icular to the walls of the culvert. 
1.30  MAXIMUM CELL SIZE - HEIGHT (m) 
DESCRIPTION:  This is the height measured between the constructe d invert of 
the culvert and the top slab of the culvert.  
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9. 
This is the height measured between the constructed  invert of the culvert and the top slab of the 
culvert.  Generally, if there is no debris inside t he culvert the “MAXIMUM CELL SIZE - 
HEIGHT (m)” and the “MIN VERTICAL CLEARANCE (M)” ar e the same. 
1.31  MIN. DEPTH OF FILL OVER (m). 
DESCRIPTION:  This is minimum height of fill measured above the structure 
. 
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9. 
This is minimum height of fill measured above the s tructure.

 SECTION 1 
 
1.  DEFINITION OF INVENTORY DATA FIELDS 
1.1  BRIDGE # 
DESCRIPTION: This is a unique number assigned to each bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 6 characters . 
 
This is a compulsory field.  It is very important t o use the correct bridge number.  This bridge 
number is the identification field in the Bridge Ma nagement System (BMS).  Prefix "TB" will be 
bridges which do not have an official registered br idge number (#) yet. Prefix “X” has been 
reserved for Irish Crossings (drifts). 
 
Examples: 
B 0 0 1   
B 2 0 0   
X 1 0 0   
X 1 3 6   
1.2  BRIDGE NAME 
DESCRIPTION: This item gives the name of the bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 30 character s. 
 
This is a compulsory field.  In order to present th e captured data more friendly this field must be 
filled in. 
 
Examples: Lagwel 
Lakaye Bridge  
Kitgummatidi 
Nakironget Irish Crossing. 
 
Note: If bridge does not have an official name, a n ame must be compiled by describing the 
features over and under the bridge. 
 
 
 
 
 

--- Page 9 ---
UNRA Asset Management System   BMS Data Dictionary 
 
January 2017  2 
 1.3  TYPE CROSSING 
DESCRIPTION:  This field describes the intersecting features. 
FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  The crossing type is described b y a two digit code. 
 
 
01 Road over river 
02 Road over rail 
03 Road over road 
04 Road over canal 
05 Rail over road 
06 Canal/Pipe over road 
07 Pedestrian over road 08 Road over pedestrian 
09 Agricultural underpass 
10 Road over Water pipes 
11 Road over conveyer belt 
12 Conveyer belt over road 
13 Other 
? Unknown 
1.4  DESCRIPTION OF PRINCIPAL FEATURE 
DESCRIPTION:  This field describes the feature carried by the br idge. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 20 character s. 
 
This is a compulsory field.  If the feature carried  by the bridge does not have an official name, 
the Link Name, or a description of the feature must  be filled in. 
 
Examples: Chosan-Amudat Road 
Ariamoi Road 
Moroto-Soroti Road 
Nyakaita - Ibanda 
Kahenge-Rwabigangura. 
1.5  LINK NAME / NR OF PRINCIPAL FEATURE 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the link name / number of the  road carried 
by the bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 255 characte rs. 
 
Example: Abim – Kilak, Aduku – Lira, etc. A001_Link 08 etc 
 
Note: 1) Leave this field blank if the feature carr ied by the bridge is not a road. 
1.6  KM DISTANCE ON PRINCIPAL FEATURE 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the km distance of the link c arried by the 
bridge. 
--- Page 10 ---
UNRA Asset Management System   BMS Data Dictionary 
 
January 2017  3 
 FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,00 and 999,99. 
 
Km distances must always refer to the permanent RMS  km reading.  This could differ from the 
km distances used during the construction phase.  F or this reason, the km distance should be 
verified during inspections. 
 
Note: If the principal feature is not a road, this item must be left blank. 
 If the km distance is unknown, it must be indicate d with a question mark. 
1.7  RIVER NAME 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the name of the river flowing  under the 
bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 20 character s. 
 
Example: River Kibimba; Malaba River; Katinda River ; Mpanga River 
Note: If the river name is unknown, it must be indi cated with a question mark. 
 If the secondary feature is not a river/stream, th is field should be left blank.  
1.8  DESCRIPTION OF SECONDARY FEATURE 
DESCRIPTION:  This field describes of the feature passing under the bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 20 character s. 
 
This is a compulsory field.  If the feature passing  under the bridge does not have an official 
name, the Road Number, or a description of the feat ure must be filled in. 
 
Examples: Rail 
  Pedestrian subway 
  Agricultural underpass    
   
  . 
1.9  LINK NAME / NR OF SECONDARY FEATURE 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the link name / link nr of th e road passing 
under the  bridge.  
FORMAT  :  Character String  :  Maximum length of 255 characte rs. 
 
 
Note: 1)  If the feature passing under the bridge is not a road, this item must be left       
      blank.

 1.10  KM DISTANCE ON SECONDARY FEATURE 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the km distance of the featur e passing 
under the bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Decimal Field  :  Range between 0,00 and 999,99. 
 
Km distances must always refer to the permanent RMS  km reading.  This could differ from the 
km distances used during the construction phase.  F or this reason, the km distance should be 
verified during inspections. 
 
Note: If the secondary feature is not a road, this item must be left blank.

1.15  LAY-OUT PLAN 
THE FOLLOWING DEFINITION IS VERY IMPORTANT! 
Definition for Abutment (left) and Abutment (right)  
 
When looking along the principal feature in the dir ection of increasing km distance, abutment (Left) 
will be behind you at the lower km distance and abu tment (Right) will be in front of you at the higher  
km distance.  If only the secondary feature has km distance markings, then abutment (Left) is to the l eft 
when viewing in the direction of increasing km dist ance and abutment (Right) to the right.

 1.28  SPAN # 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the number of each of the spa ns. 
FORMAT  :  Integer Field  :  Range between 1 and 99

1.29  SPAN LENGTH (m) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the length of a bridge span. 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 999,99 
 
For bridge structures the span length is measured o n the centre line of the principal feature from 
centre line of support to centre line of support.  For cell structures the span length is measured 
perpendicular to the centre line of the culvert and  should only be the clear opening width.

1.32  NUMBER OF PIERS 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the number of piers of a brid ge. 
FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  Range from 0 to 99 
 
This is a compulsory field.  A pier can consist of a single column/wall or a row of columns that 
divides the deck it supports into two spans.  Each inner wall of a cell structure (in situ) must be 
considered a pier.  The contacting inner walls of c ell structures (precast units) must be 
considered as one pier 
. 
IMPORTANT  :  The number of piers is always one les s than the number of spans. A 
single span bridge has 0 (nil) piers

 1.36  PIER HEIGHT (m) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the average height of each pie r measured on 
the centre line of the principal feature.  Also see  figure 4. 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9 
 
The height of a pier column is measured from the to p of the footing to the top of the pier 
(bearings excluded).  
1.37  PIER THICKNESS (m) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the most prominent thickness o f each pier, 
measured perpendicular to the width of the pier.  A lso see figure 4. 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9 
 
For piers with tapered columns, the dimensions are taken at the top end. 
1.38  PIER WIDTH (m) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the width of each pier.  Also  see figure 4. 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,0 and 99,9 
 
Width refers to the dimension that is generally par allel to the secondary feature.  For culverts, the 
pier width is measured on the centre line of the cu lvert between the outer edges of the headwalls.

1.41  TYPE BEARINGS ON PIERS (ROW 1)/(ROW 2) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the type of bearings on which the bridge deck is carried 
at each pier. 
 
FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  The type of bearing is descri bed by a two digit code. 
 
If the bridge deck is continuous over a pier, there  will only be one-row bearings per pier (side 
elevation).  Bearings (row 2) will be coded 97 .  If there is a joint over a pier, there will gene rally 
be two rows of bearings per pier (side elevation).  Bearings (in row 1) is on the left abutment 
side and bearing (in row 2) on the right abutment s ide of the pier.

THE FOLLOWING DEFINITION IS VERY IMPORTANT! 
Definition for Abutment (left) and Abutment (right)  
 
When looking along the principal feature in the dir ection of increasing km distance, abutment (Left) 
will be at the lower km distance and abutment (Righ t) will be at the higher km distance.  If only the 
secondary feature has km distance markings, then ab utment (Left) is to the left when viewing in the 
direction of increasing km distance and abutment (R ight) to the right.  
 
 
 
01 Mass concrete gravity type 
02 Reinforced concrete 
03 R C wall with RC wingwalls

06 Frame (only bridges, not cell structures) 
07 Buttressed concrete wall 
08 Masonry wall

 1.52  DATE MAJOR REHABILITATION 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the completion date of major rehabilitation 
work performed on the bridge. 
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  YYYY 
 
The date of major rehabilitation works is to be ind icated in the format YYYY.  Rehabilitation is 
defined as work aimed at upgrading the general cond ition of a bridge or the load carrying 
capacity of a bridge (e.g. widening).  Please give a description of the rehabilitation in the block 
for additional remarks.

Y – Yes 
N – No 
U - Unknown

1.67  HORIZONTAL CLEARANCE PRINCIPAL (m) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the horizontal clearance measu rement  on 
the bridge.  (See Figure 13).  
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,00 and 99,99.  
 
The horizontal clearance is the limiting measuremen t for a vehicle to pass over the bridge.  The 
shape of kerbs, balustrades and traffic barriers wi ll determine where the measurement is to be 
taken.  For cell structures the horizontal clearanc e is the formation width, or the width between 
guard-rails.  If the feature passing over the bridg e is not a road, this field must be left blank.  
 

--- Page 38 ---
UNRA Asset Management 
System  BMS Data Dictionary 
 
January 2017  31 
 
 
 
Figure 13: Horizontal clearance 
1.68  VERTICAL CLEARANCE SECONDARY (m) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the vertical clearance of the road passing 
under the bridge. (usually at the tie point). 
FORMAT  :   Decimal String  :  Range between 0,00 and 99,99. 
 
When not obtained from the drawings, the vertical c learance must be measured at the critical 
point.  That is where the distance between the road  surface, and the deck soffit is the smallest.  
Vertical clearance for the secondary feature does n ot apply for river bridges and drainage 
structures. If the feature passing under the bridge  is not a road, this field must be left blank.  
1.69  HORIZONTAL CLEARANCE SECONDARY (m) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the horizontal clearance measu rement 
under the bridge.  (See Figure 14) . 
FORMAT  :  Decimal String  :  Range between 0,00 and 99,99.  
 
The horizontal clearance must be measured as the di stance between the supports/embankment 
perpendicular to the centre line of the road under the bridge.  Horizontal clearance for the 
secondary feature does not apply for river bridges and drainage structures. If the feature passing 
under the bridge is not a road, this field must be left blank. 
 
 
Figure 14: Horizontal clearance – secondary feature

 1.70  NUMBER OF LANES 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the number of traffic lanes o ver a bridge. 
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  Range between 0 and 9. 
 
A fully developed turning lane on a bridge is also considered as a lane.

DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates whether a bridge carries one- way or two-
way traffic.  
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  Either 1 or 2.

DESCRIPTION:  This field gives the class of road (Geometric stan dard

passing under the bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Character Field  :  Maximum length of 3 characte rs. 
1.73  NUMBER OF SIDEWALKS 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the number  of sidewalks over a bridge.  
FORMAT  :   Integer String  :  Range between 0 and 9. 
1.74  AVERAGE DAILY TRAFFIC (PRINCIPAL/SECONDARY) 
DESCRIPTION: This field indicates the average daily traffic (veh icles per 
day) passing over (principal) or under (secondary) the bridge. 
FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  Range between 0 and 9999999. 
 
Average daily traffic is measured in equivalent veh icle units per day.  A heavy* vehicle is 
counted as equivalent to three standard vehicles.  
 
*Axle load > 4000 kg, or gross vehicle mass > 7000 kg, or carrying, capacity > 3000 kg. 
1.75  TRUCK FACTOR (%) (PRINCIPAL/SECONDARY) 
DESCRIPTION:  This field indicates the percentage of the traffic  volume that is 
made up of heavy vehicles. 
--- Page 40 ---

System  BMS Data Dictionary

 FORMAT  :  Integer String  :  Range between 0 and 99% 
The following values can be used as a guide-line wh en information is unavailable: 
• High percentage (%) of heavy vehicles: 20%. 
• Average percentage (%) of heavy vehicles: 8%. 
• Low percentage (%) of heavy vehicles: 2%.

THE FOLLOWING DEFINITION IS VERY IMPORTANT! 
Definition for Abutment (left) and Abutment (right)  
 
When looking along the principal feature in the dir ection of increasing km distance, abutment 
(Left) will be at the lower km distance and abutmen t (Right) will be at the higher km distance.  If 
only the secondary feature has km distance markings , then abutment (Left) is to the left when 
viewing in the direction of increasing km distance and abutment (Right) to the right.

Appendix A:  Inspection Sheets for Bridges  
 
 
--- Page 6 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  1 1. INTRODUCTION 
 
1.1 BACKGROUND 
 
The basic objective of a bridge management system i s to ensure that existing and future bridges provid e the 
required level of service within the available budg etary constraints.  One of the fundamental requirements 
for such a management system is the routine, detail ed surveillance of the structures involved.   
 
Various phases in the life of a bridge can be ident ified, as presented in Figure 1.1.  This figure is also 
highlighting the important role of routine inspecti ons. 
 
 
FIGURE 1.1 : PHASES IN THE LIFE OF A BRIDGE 
 
 
 DECOMISSIONING NEED 
UPGRADING IMPOSE WEIGHT 
RESTRICTIONS MAINTENANCE AND 
REHABILITATION INSPECTION/
MONITORING CONSTRUCTION DESIGN PLANNING NEED 
--- Page 7 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  2 1.2 SCOPE 
 
This document is intended as a guide to the techniq ues, procedures and documentation of inspections of  bridges of a 
type commonly found in Uganda.  Its aim is to provi de information and recommendations, based on domest ic and 
overseas experience, with the hope that some degree  of standardisation may result. 
 
Much of the information and recommendations include d relate to the engineering aspects of bridge inspe ction.  
However, such topics as organisation, management, i nspection strategy and training of inspectors are a lso discussed. 
 
Finally, this document is concerned only with inspe ction to assess the current physical condition  which 
includes identification of defects  and maintenance  recommendations. 
 
1.3 WHY IS BRIDGE INSPECTION NECESSARY? 
 
Bridges are an intrinsic part of the overall road n etwork, and, as such, require surveillance, mainten ance and general 
management in co-ordination with the rest of the ro ad system.  Furthermore, bridges are key elements i n that system.  
They are often of strategic importance and represen t considerable capital investment.  The consequence s of the 
failure of a bridge can be disastrous, particularly  in terms of the disruption of traffic and the subs equent economic 
losses sustained.  It follows, therefore, that part icular attention must be given to management of the  bridges 
component of the road network, and systematic inspe ction of the structures is essential for efficient management. 
 
The main reason for the regular inspection of bridg es is to assure, or at least maximise, the safety o f the travelling 
public.  A road authority has a continuing obligati on to minimise the risk to the public using the bri dges in its 
jurisdiction, subject to the constraints of the res ources available to it.  Because the safety of a st ructure depends on a 
broad array of socio-economic as well as engineerin g factors it is difficult to assess the contributio n of systematic 
inspection to bridge safety by standard methods, su ch as cost/benefit analysis.  Nevertheless, even li mited resources 
spent on regular inspections linked to preventative  maintenance can only have a beneficial effect on t he overall 
safety of the road network. 
 
The second main reason for bridge inspection is to protect the capital invested in the structure with the minimum 
operational cost.  Economically, the advantages of preventative maintenance are axiomatic, and the opt imum 
planning of such repair work as is necessary is hig hly dependent on the information gained from system atic 
inspections. 
 
New technical developments in bridge engineering ta ke place continually, and have been particularly ra pid and 
numerous in the last two decades.  The direct or in direct consequences of these developments may only become 
apparent in the long-term.  Today’s structures, the refore, need careful monitoring to ensure that the greatest 
advantage is gained from new developments.  Again, this means regular, systematic inspections.  
 
Bridge inspection should also be seen as the final check in the design and construction process.  The structure as 
built is rarely identical to that designed, and imp erfections or errors are inevitable.  While it is c ommon practice to 
inspect a bridge at the time of completion, and aft er the end of the maintenance period (normally one year), the 
consequences of many design or construction errors may not become apparent for several years. 
 
1.4 AIMS OF BRIDGE INSPECTION 
 
 The aims of bridge inspection, not necessarily in order of priority, are: 
 
--- Page 8 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  3 • To provide assurance that the bridge is structurall y safe and fit for its intended use; this relates t o 
gradual deterioration of the bridge with time or to  an accidental occurrence such as impact, flood, fi re, 
or overloading by exceptional vehicles and convoys of vehicles. 
 
• To identify actual and potential sources of trouble  at the earliest possible stage. 
 
• To record systematically and periodically the state/condition of the structure, and 
maintenance/rehabilitation  recommendations for the repair of defects . 
 
• To provide the Bridge Management System with detail information  regarding defects , i.e. the extent 
of the defect, the stage of the defect, and the exa ct quantity of the required/recommended maintenance  
activity, in order for the BMS to prioritise mainte nance/rehabilitation activities.  
 
• To group defects/maintenance activities on a number  of bridges together so as to deploy maintenance 
resources more efficiently and reduce repair costs.  
 
• To enable the BMS to provide estimates of longer-te rm budgetary requirements to maintain the bridge 
system at the required level of service (i.e. strat egic planning). 
 
• To enable the BMS to provide future network conditi on predictions for budgetary constraints. 
 
• To provide feedback information to designers, contr actors and owners about those features which are 
likely to give maintenance problems and to which th e necessary 
attention is best given during the design and const ruction stage. 
 
• To assess new materials and methods used for constr uction and repair. 
 
• To check the effect of any changes in permitted loa ds on a bridge; this may be associated with a ratin g 
or assessment system, particularly for bridges whos e strength is considered sub-standard with respect 
to current design standards. 
 
1.5 DOCUMENT LAYOUT 
 
This document has been structured in such a way tha t it firstly provides specific guidelines for bridg e inspections as 
required for the Bridge Management System and secon dly provides information which could apply to more detailed 
investigations. 
 
In Chapter 2 a brief description of the bridge mana gement system is given and then the requirements fo r the BMS are 
defined in terms of categories of inspections.  Eac h of the inspection categories (superficial, princi pal and special) 
are dealt with in Chapters 3, 4 and 5 respectively.   Chapter 5 also covers topics related to special i nspections.

June 2012  4  
2. BRIDGE INSPECTIONS AND THE BMS 
 
2.1 THE BRIDGE MANAGEMENT SYSTEM 
 
2.1.1 BENEFITS OF A BMS 
 
In order to manage and maintain UNRA’s capital inve stment in bridges, large amounts of data needs to b e 
processed.  The BMS is first and foremost a managem ent facilitator.  In its computerised format it mak es this 
data easily accessible and manageable.  Enquiries t hat would otherwise be very cumbersome are made ver y 
easily.  Typical examples are: 
 
• State/Condition of the bridge network or portion th ere-of at any time, current or future projection? 
• What, where and how many bridges currently require routine and/or major maintenance or repair, and 
how much is this going to cost?  Prioritise require d maintenance activities? 
• Which bridges are “structurally deficient”?  The te rm “structurally deficient” is merely a euphemism f or 
“unsafe”?  Prioritise/rank these bridges? 
• Which bridges are “functionally obsolete”?  The ter m “functionally obsolete” is a term used, for 
example, when a bridge is no longer wide enough for  the class of road, or when a bridge soffit gets hi t 
frequently by under passing traffic, or when too ma ny accidents are occurring at the bridge.  Rank the se 
bridges? 
• What is the effect of less than optimal use of fund s for bridge maintenance, on the bridge network? 
 
2.1.2 OBJECTIVES OF A BRIDGE MANAGEMENT SYSTEM 
 
The ultimate objective of a Bridge Management Syste m is to assist officials in ensuring that all bridg es provide 
an acceptable and safe level of service to the publ ic, while the expenditure needed to provide this mu st remain 
within the budgetary constraints.  This objective c an be met by providing a system that incorporates t he 
following functions: 
 
• comprehensive record keeping of all relevant bridge  information; 
 
• ensure uniform reporting of Inventory and Inspectio n information; 
 
• identification of bridge defects that will require maintenance, rehabilitation or replacement activiti es at a 
bridge; 
 
• planning and prioritising of all maintenance and upgrading work on bridges; 
 
--- Page 10 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  5 • Recording historical expenditures for different mai ntenance and rehabilitation activities. 
 
• providing estimates of longer-term budgetary requir ements to maintain the bridge system at the require d 
level of service (i.e. strategic planning); and 
 
• Providing future network condition predictions for budgetary constraints. 
 
The level of service can be used as the primary foc al point in managing a public facility:  In this ca se the level 
of service gives an indication of the degree to whi ch a bridge provides an acceptable and safe facilit y to the 
general public.  Failure to meet this level of serv ice could result in costs to the user due to damage  to property, 
or cost associated with delays due to bridge closur e.  On the other hand, the authorities which have t o maintain 
these bridges are financed through taxation of the general public.  A bridge management system should 
therefore minimise the total cost to the general pu blic by finding an optimum balance between acceptab le levels 
of service and the cost of providing these levels o f service within the budgetary constraints. 
 
2.1.3 COMPONENTS OF THE BRIDGE MANAGEMENT SYSTEM 
 
(i) Database  
 
The database is the nucleus of the BMS.  All the in formation essential to the management of the 
bridges is contained in the database.  This include s bridge inventory data (describing each individual  
bridge), condition data, maintenance and rehabilita tion data, upgrading data and cost data. 
 
(ii) Ranking Module  
 
A primary objective of the BMS is to identify those  bridges that need attention .  Structural safety and 
traffic safety  should be considered.  Structural safety by way of  capacity and condition, and traffic 
safety by way of level of service provided by the p revailing roadway standards.  In order to decide 
about this, bridges are ranked according to a defic iency index (defect index).  The index is made up o f 
three basic components, as discussed below: 
 
a) The level of service provided to the public (Tra ffic safety) 
 
The degree to which a bridge meets users’ demands ( traffic safety) is based on an evaluation 
of the following physical properties of the bridge:  
 
• Vertical clearance 
• Horizontal clearance 
• Roadway alignment on the bridge 
 
b) Condition of the bridge (Structural safety) 
--- Page 11 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  6  
Evaluation in respect of structural condition is co nfined only to criteria which could render 
the structure critical from a strength or stability  point of view. 
 
These criteria are the bridge deck (superstructure) , the abutments, piers, bearing and 
foundations (substructure), the approach fill mater ial (approaches), the embankment 
protection works and riverbed (waterway), and the t raffic barriers and expansion joints 
(roadway). 
 
c) Importance of the bridge 
 
The relevant importance of each bridge can be measu red in terms of the traffic volumes that 
cross the bridge and the strategic importance of th e bridge.  Strategic importance is measured 
in terms of the potential delays in travelling time  if the bridge were to be closed. 
 
The contribution of each of the above aspects in th e calculation of the deficiency index is governed b y 
a weighting factor assigned to each aspect.  The different weighting factors can be changed to s uit 
the particular emphasis of an investigation. 
 
iii) Short Term Budgeting and Maintenance Module  
 
Bridge inspectors indicate the type, quantity and t he stage and extent of the defect to be repaired at  a 
bridge on standard reporting forms.  The BMS progra m then prioritises the selection of work using a 
priority index.  The priority index is also includi ng the effect of the relative importance and sensit ivity 
of the various components.  Unit rates for the stan dard maintenance items are used to calculate costs.   
Records are also kept of the actual costs of comple ted maintenance activities. 
 
iv) Long Term Budget Module  
 
The Markov chain method and dynamic programming is used to simulate the deterioration and 
performance of a bridge over a specified number of years, and the consequent budget requirements to 
maintain acceptable service levels are calculated.  Dynamic programming is used to establish the 
optimum maintenance alternative for a given age and  condition of a bridge and the Markov chain is 
used to simulate the consequent deterioration or im provement in bridge conditions due to the selected 
option.  The impact of budget restrictions on the c ondition of bridges can also be evaluated.  
Historical information about the condition ratings of the bridges over a period of time can be used to  
establish deterioration curves, which are used in t he simulation module.  This model is based upon 
extensive bridge data from a total of 6 500 concret e bridges that had been subjected to similar 
conditions as those bridges in the network.  These records include bridges of up to 70 years of age. 
 
v) Reporting module  
 
--- Page 12 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  7 The reporting module is the communication link betw een the BMS and the end user.  Report 
generation is quick and simple, yet very versatile.   Reports may vary from comprehensive network 
reports to specific enquiries, e.g. list all bridge s on road D180 with vertical clearance less than 4, 8 m 
and also give the actual vertical clearance at each  bridge. 
 
vi)  Inspection frequency module 
 
This report estimates the cycle length between insp ections, which normally ranges between 1 and 4 year s.  The 
following factors are used in the model: 
 
• Road class/category 
• Condition of bridge components 
• Bridge age 
• Average daily traffic carried by the bridge 
• Risk of scour. 
 
Above model is only a tool to help the bridge engin eer in making rational decisions regarding the sche duling of 
future bridge inspections.  The approach adopted by  an authority will depend on the available resource s and the 
nature of structures under its jurisdiction.  Final ly the scale and frequency of bridge inspections re mains a matter for 
engineering judgement. 
 
2.2 CATEGORIES OF INSPECTION 
 
It is important for the bridge inspector to know th e extent of detail that is required when inspecting  a bridge.  
Clearly the extent of detail required for general p lanning purposes that would typically be utilised i n a bridge 
management system is less than what would be requir ed for an investigation into a specific problem 
encountered at a bridge, for which the causes might  be uncertain. 
 
Inspection can be classified into three categories,  defining both the frequency and intensity of inspe ction. 
 
(i) Superficial inspection  
 
This is cursory, visual check carried out by road m aintenance personnel who have a good practical 
knowledge of road structures, but who are not neces sarily specialists in bridge inspection.  These 
inspections may or may not be regular and will usua lly occur as the opportunity arises, perhaps during  
road maintenance.  On lightly-trafficked roads in r ural areas, such an inspection may amount to little  
more than a reassurance that the structure still ex ists; on heavily-trafficked and vital routes, such as 
urban freeways, a cursory check may be carried out monthly or even weekly.  Any adverse condition 
should be reported, but the absence of defects need  not be recorded.  Because of their casual nature, 
no definite recommendations can be made on the freq uency of superficial inspections. 
 
Even when a programme of regular, detailed inspecti ons is operating, the importance of superficial 
inspections should not be underestimated.  If the g eneral road maintenance staff of an authority are 
--- Page 13 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  8 aware of the importance of these inspections and co -operate in performing cursory checks whenever 
possible, this can increase the level of surveillan ce considerably. 
 
(ii) Principal inspection  
 
This is a regular inspection carried out by a quali fied inspector (typically a professional engineer or 
professional technologist with at least 5 years’ ex perience in bridge design), under the general 
supervision  of a senior bridge engineer , or alternatively by a senior bridge engineer  depending on 
the strategy of the authority and forms the backbon e of an inspection programme required for the 
BMS.  It should consist of a thorough examination o f all the components of the structure and, although  
essentially a visual inspection, it may include suc h observations as the measurement of crack widths, 
level surveys, etc.  Standardised reporting forms a nd evaluation criteria should be used to aid in the  
computerisation of the inspection results.  The ide ntification of all defects, the stage and extent of  the 
defect, as well as maintenance recommendations are all part of the principal inspection. 
 
The frequency, and to some extent the intensity, of  a principal inspection depends on a number of 
factors such as the importance of the bridge in the  road network, its condition, and perhaps of greate st 
importance, the resources available.  With major st ructures on heavily trafficked routes, it is 
recommended that a principal inspection be conducte d every second year.  This may be reduced to 
once every three to five years on more lightly traf ficked routes.  The frequency of inspections is als o 
discussed in section 2.4, and the possible strategi es regarding resources in section 2.3. 
 
 
 (iii) Special inspection 
 
A special inspection will usually be made in connec tion with unusual circumstances, such as 
exceptional loading, with the occurrence of a major  weakness or with the reassessment of the structure  
against revised specifications and regulations.  Su ch inspections may require supplementary testing 
(e.g. load tests), structural analysis, and may eve n include research.  They will invariably require t he 
detailed involvement of a specialist bridge engineer .  When the capacity of a bridge has to be 
reassessed, as for example for the passage of a Sup erload, the parameters used in the structural 
analysis must obviously reflect accurately the cond ition of the structure at the time. 
 
A special inspection will be required in the follow ing circumstances: 
 
• A specific condition is discovered by casual observ ation or by a superficial or principal 
inspection. 
 
• Specific conditions are found for similar materials  or similar structures, e.g. the discovery of 
concrete containing high-alumina cement, or the app earance of alkali-aggregate reaction. 
 
--- Page 14 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  9 • Structures are subjected to critical stresses or ar e known to have weaknesses, e.g. a bridge 
before, during and after the passage of an exceptio nal load. 
 
• Subsidence occurs in areas of mineral or coal extra ction. 
 
• After an exceptional event, e.g. flood. 
 
• After the occurrence of an accident either on or un der a bridge; special attention should be paid 
to slender piers, balustrades and other structural members exposed to impact by vehicles. 
 
• Scouring takes place; this may require underwater i nspection. 
 
2.3 INSPECTION STRATEGY 
 
It is important to have a clearly defined inspectio n strategy.  The approach adopted by an authority w ill depend on 
the available resources and the nature of the struc tures under its jurisdiction. 
 
The following options can be considered: 
 
--- Page 15 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual

Selecting Inspectors: 
--- Page 16 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  11  
In selecting suitable personnel for training as bri dge inspectors, the senior bridge engineer should l ook 
for the following qualities: 
 
i) Reliability 
 
The engineer must be able to depend on his inspecto rs to carry out a task according to instructions gi ven, and to inform the engineer promptly of 
any unusual circumstances. 
 
ii) Technical Skills 
 
   The inspector must typically be a professional e ngineer with at least 5    
   years’ experience in bridge design, or a profess ional technologist with at least 5   
   years’ experience in bridge design. 
 
iii) Language skills 
 
Inspectors must be able to read the technical Engli sh, used on the inspection report; they must also b e able to write clearly in the same language. 
 
iv)  Health  
 
Inspectors should be physically fit, so they are ab le to inspect difficult places without taking 
unnecessary risks; it is advisable to check that th e inspector has good eyesight, as he will be 
required to detect small defects in conditions of v arying light and shade. 
 
v) Attitude 
 
Good inspectors are methodical in their work and mu st be willing to inspect items thoroughly and criti cally; they must also be continually aware 
of safety so that they do not take risks and do not  endanger the safety of others. 
 
Training Inspectors: 
 
i) Training of inspectors is crucial to the success ful operation of a bridge inspection  
 system and should be tailored to the ability and e xperience of the trainees.   
 Training should, ideally, be carried out initially  by a skilled instructor.  The Bridge  
 Inspection Manual can be used as a textbook for fo rmal classroom instruction of  inspectors. 
 
ii)  The next step in training an inspector is for the s enior/specialist bridge engineer or an 
instructor to go to several different bridges with the trainee inspector, or a group of trainee 
inspectors, and go through the inspection step by s tep.  As the inspector will probably be 
dealing with several different types of bridge, he should inspect at least one of each type with 
an engineer before working on his own. 
 
b)  A senior bridge engineer: 
 
--- Page 17 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  12 The role of the senior engineer will combine the fu nctions of an inspector and senior engineer as disc ussed in option 
(a).  This option will be definitely the most expen sive. 
 
Unless specific problems are anticipated on a certa in road or in a region, option (a) is almost always  the 
preferred option. 
 
2.4 INSPECTION FREQUENCY 
 
The scale and frequency of bridge inspections are a  matter for engineering judgement and must be decid ed for 
each situation.  They depend on many factors includ ing: 
 
• The amount of traffic using the structure, usually expressed in terms of either vehicles per day or 
equivalent vehicle units per day; certain types of deterioration, such as those associated with expans ion 
joints, are directly related to the amount of traff ic carried.  In addition, damage is common followin g 
overstressing by illegally loaded vehicles or by ab normal loads travelling too fast over the bridge, a nd 
the incidence of such overloading increases with th e traffic volume. 
 
• The classification or importance of the route on wh ich the structure is located; the sensible use of 
limited inspection resources demand that a greater priority be given to the surveillance of bridges on  
strategically or economically important routes than  to those routes of lower importance or classificat ion. 
 
• The type of structure; experience has shown that ce rtain types of bridges are more prone to deteriorat ion 
than others. 
 
• The age, condition and history of the structure; ev idently; if deteriorated has been observed which 
requires monitoring, or if a structure is suspect f or a reason, then closer surveillance of the at str ucture 
would be justified.  The age of the bridge is impor tant - very old structures often need to be inspect ed 
regularly. 
 
Because these factors interact, and because the req uirements of road authorities differ, it is extreme ly difficult - 
and perhaps unnecessary - to provide universal reco mmendations for the frequency and type of inspectio ns.  In 
effect, the inspection strategy for each bridge, or  group of similar bridges, has to be decided indivi dually, 
depending on the combined influence of the factors above. 
 
To assist inspecting authorities in formulating ins pection strategies, five examples, or case studies,  are given 
below.  These are intended to provide practical inf ormation on the scale and frequency of inspection t hat would 
be advisable in each case.  The examples refer to t he factors above and reinforce the suggestion that each 
structure requires inspection according to its indi vidual needs, rather than according to a set of uni versal 
recommendations. 
 
(i) Bridge 1  
 
--- Page 18 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  13 A series of short-span, reinforced concrete slab st ructures taking a rural road over minor rivers.  Th e 
bridges are isolated and carry less than 200 vehicl es per day. 
 
Strategy: 
 
Principal inspection every five years backed up by superficia l inspections by road maintenance staff 
at approximately yearly intervals. 
 
(ii) Bridge 2  
 
A series of medium-span bridges of prestressed beam -and-slab construction.  The structures are of 
similar, conventional design and have shown no prob lems to date.  They are situated on an important 
route carrying 3 000 vehicles per day. 
 
Strategy: 
 
A principal inspection every two to three years.  Because of th e conventional design and safe history 
of the bridges, it is sensible to check such compon ents as expansion joints and railings, which may 
deteriorate more rapidly than the structural elemen ts.  On a route of such importance and traffic 
volume, superficial inspections are likely to take place regularly. 
 
(iii) Bridge 3  
 
Cracks have appeared in the main beams of one of th e medium-span bridges on the above route.  
Because its design is slightly different from that of the others, there is no reason to suspect the re st. 
 
Strategy: 
 
A special inspection every six months with the main purpose o f monitoring the cracks.  An 
experienced structural engineer should be involved in these inspections. 
 
(iv) Bridge 4  
 
A short-span, reinforced concrete structure in an u rban area.  The traffic volume is low at 2 000 
vehicles per day but the bridge regularly carries a bnormal loads as it is situated close to a factory 
producing heavy industrial components. 
 
Strategy: 
 
A principal inspection once a year.  This relatively high frequ ency is justified, in spite of the small 
amount of traffic, by the frequent abnormal loads. 
 
--- Page 19 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  14 (v) Bridge 5  
 
A complex, multi-span prestressed concrete box gird er which forms part of the elevated section of an 
urban freeway.  The structure carries over 20 000 v ehicles per day.  Access to the bridge is difficult . 
 
Strategy 
 
A principal inspection once a year.  A high degree of surveilla nce is essential for such a structure.  
The amount of traffic carried and the importance of  the bridge from the point of view of both safety 
and economics remove any possible complacency.  Sup erficial inspections are likely to take place 
regularly. 
 
2.5 SAFETY MATTERS 
 
Safety is the first concern in the field.  Getting the job done is secondary.  The work is inherently dangerous 
and therefore requires continual vigilance on the p art of every member of the inspection team.  Two of  the most 
important factors are common sense and a safety con scious attitude.  Safety must be practised at all t imes to be 
effective.  Accidents are expensive, and can be fat al. 
 
While the work may be hazardous, the accident proba bility may be limited by proceeding cautiously.  Th e 
following rules should be observed: 
 
• Always be careful, use good judgement and prudence in conducting your activities, both for your own 
safety and that of others. 
 
• If you wear glasses, wear them when climbing.  The wearing of bifocals is an exception to this rule.  
Only regular single lens glasses should be worn whi le climbing.  Where work requiring close-up 
viewing is to be performed, a separate pair of glas ses with lenses ground for this purpose should be 
worn.  
 
• Do not drink alcoholic beverages before or during w orking hours.  They impair judgement reflexes, and 
co-ordination. 
 
2.5.1 BRIDGE SITE ORGANIZATION 
 
The safety of personnel and equipment and the effic iency of bridge inspection operations depend upon p roper 
site organisation. 
 
All individuals who are assigned to work aloft must  be thoroughly trained in the rigging and use of th eir 
equipment, i.e., scaffolds, working platforms, ladd ers and safety belts.  The plans for inspection sho uld give 
first consideration to safeguarding personnel from possible injuries. 
--- Page 20 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  15  
Inspection equipment, highway traffic barricades, a nd signs should be arranged according to the plant of 
inspection so as to accomplish as little handling, unnecessary movement, and repositioning as possible .  
Vehicles not directly involved in the inspection pr ocess should be parked so as to prevent congestion and avoid 
interference in the area of inspection. 
 
Individuals should develop orderly habits in workin g and housekeeping on the job. 
 
2.5.2 PERSONAL PROTECTION 
 
It is important to dress properly.  Keep clothing a nd shoes free of grease.  Always wear the following  required 
protective equipment. 
 
• Hard hat with a thin strap. 
• Goggles, face masks, shield, or helmets, when aroun d shot blasting, cutting, welding, etc. 
• Reflective vests or belts when working in traffic. 
• Life preservers or work vests when working over wat er. 
• Shoes with cork, rubber, or some other non-slip sol es. 
 
2.5.3 SPECIAL SAFETY EQUIPMENT 
 
A life line or belt must be worn when working: 
 
• at heights over 5 m; 
• above water; and 
• above traffic. 
 
A life-saving or safety skiff should be provided wh en working over large rivers or harbours; the skiff  should 
have life preservers and lifelines on board. 
 
Warning signals, barricades, or flagmen are necessa ry when the deck is to be inspected, or when scaffo lding or 
platform trucks are used. 
 
2.5.4 CLIMBING OF HIGH STEEL 
 
It is preferable to work from a traveller, catwalk or platform truck, if possible. 
 
NOTE : On ladders and catwalks, proceed with extrem e caution. 
 
(i) Scaffolding  
--- Page 21 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  16  
When using scaffolding, the following precautions s hould be observed: 
 
• Scaffolding and working platforms should be of ampl e strength and should be secure against 
slipping or overturning. 
 
• Hanging scaffolds and other light scaffolds support ed by ropes should be tested before using by 
hanging them a foot or so from the ground and loadi ng them with a weight at least four times as 
great as their working load. 
 
• Scaffolds should be inspected at least once each wo rking day. 
 
 (ii) Ladders  
 
Ladders should be used as platforms only when it is  absolutely necessary to do so.  When using 
ladders the following precautions should be observe d: 
 
• Make certain that the ladder to be used is soundly constructed.  If made of wood, the material 
should be straight-grained and clear. 
 
• Ladders should be tested to make sure they could ca rry the intended loads. 
 
• Ladders should be blocked at the foot or tied at th e top to prevent slipping. 
 
• Personnel should be cautioned frequently about the danger of trying to reach too far from a 
single setting of a ladder. 
 
Catwalks, scaffolds, and platforms should have hand  rails and toe boards to keep tools or other object s 
from being kicked off and becoming a hazard to anyo ne below. 
 
Always watch where you are stepping.  Do not run or  jump. 
 
Do not climb if you are tired or upset. 
 
2.5.5 CONFINED SPACES 
 
Confined spaces are present both in large truss bri dges and in box girder bridges with rectangular or trapezoidal 
box-sections.  In these types of members, the inter ior is often closed off at both ends, forming a clo sed box.  
This protected interior is high in corrosion resist ance even in the bare metal state, a great advantag e in the 
maintenance of the structure.  In some closed secti ons a closable, watertight, and vaportight access m anhole is 
provided to permit inspection of the interior.  Whi le the box-section offers both structural and maint enance 
--- Page 22 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  17 advantages over other types of sections, there are certain health hazards of which the maintenance ins pectors, 
and other who may be involved with these types of s ections, should be aware of. 
 
No health hazard exists in confined space if there is proper ventilation.  However, a hazardous atmosp here can 
develop because of a lack of sufficient oxygen or b ecause of a concentration of toxic gasses.  Oxygen 
deficiency can be caused by the slow oxidant of org anic matter which has become moistened.  Toxic gase s may 
seep into the confined space or may be generated by  such work processes as painting, burning or weldin g.  The 
confined space may be of such small volume that air  contaminants are produced more quickly than the li mited 
ventilation of the space can overcome.  Persons sho uld not be allowed to work in confined spaces conta ining 
less than 19 % oxygen unless provided with air-brea thing apparatus.  However, as noted above, a space with 
sufficient oxygen content can become unfit for huma n occupancy if the work conducted therein produces toxic 
fumes or gases.  The inspector should occupy such s pace only after adequate ventilation. 
 
(i) Safety Procedures  
 
(a) Pre-entry air tests 
 
• Tests for oxygen content should be conducted with a n approved oxygen-detecting 
device.  A minimum of two tests should be conducted . 
 
• Where the presence of other gases is suspected, tes t for such gases should be 
conducted using approved gas-detecting devices.  Th e following gases should be 
considered: 
 
- Carbon Dioxide 
- Carbon Monoxide 
- Hydrogen Sulphide 
- Methane 
- Any combustible gas. 
 
• If the oxygen content of the air in the space is be low 19 %, no person should be 
allowed to enter such space unless mechanical venti lation is applied to the space until 
the oxygen content and gas content meet the above l imits for a minimum period of 
fifteen (15) minutes. 
 
  (b) Ventilation during occupancy 
 
• All confined spaces should be mechanically ventilat ed continuously during occupancy 
irrespective of the presence of gas, the depletion of oxygen, or the conduct of 
contaminant-producing work. 
 
--- Page 23 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  18 • Where contaminant-producing operations are to be co nducted, an Industrial Hygiene 
Engineer, Safety Engineer, Marine Chemist, or other  qualified to approve such 
operations, should approve the ventilation scheme. 
 
(c) Air tests during occupancy 
 
• If toxic gas presence or oxygen depletion is detect ed or suspected, air tests similar to 
the Pre-entry air tests should be conducted during occupancy of fifteen (15) minute 
intervals. 
 
• Where contaminant-producing operations are conducte d, air tests should be conducted 
to determine the adequacy of the ventilation scheme . 
--- Page 24 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  19 3. SUPERFICIAL INSPECTIONS 
 
3.1 OBJECTIVES 
 
The main objective of superficial inspections is to  record problems at bridges at the earliest notice and then to report 
these problems to the responsible authorities so th at action may be taken when required.  In doing so,  maintenance 
costs may be saved on by preventing relatively mino r problems to deteriorate to serious problems. 
 
3.2 INSPECTION GUIDELINES 
 
Superficial inspection will mostly be conducted fro m a moving vehicle while driving over or under a br idge.  It may 
even involve an occasional stop at the structure fo r a quick cursory check of the condition of the str ucture.  Because 
of their casual nature, no fixed guidelines can be drawn up for these inspections. 
 
The most important aspect of these inspections is t o create a sense of awareness with the general road  staff of an 
authority.  Some of the more obvious symptoms of pr oblems which may be looked out for are: 
 
* Sight along guardrails, parapets or railings for an y sudden deviations of line, which may be caused by  
differential settlement, tilting or even structural  failure of a subcomponent. 
 
* Take note of the quality (in terms of smoothness) o f the ride over a bridge.  Bumps may be caused by 
settlement of the approach fill, excessive deflecti on or creep of the superstructure or settlement of the 
substructure. 
 
* Take note of any impact damage to the bridge, such as bent or damage traffic barriers, spalls on the 
soffit above the point of smallest vertical clearan ce etc. 
 
* Theft or vandalism, such as the removal of metal ra ilings or bridge warning signs. 
 
* If cracks are observed, these should be reported to  the authority for further investigation into the n ature 
and causes of the cracks. 
 
The reporting of these problems will depend on the time and equipment available at the time of noticin g the 
problem.  Photographs could be very informative, bu t a telephonic or short written report may be all t hat is 
required to initiate a further, more detailed, inve stigation. 
--- Page 25 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  20  
4. PRINCIPAL INSPECTIONS 
 
4.1 OBJECTIVES 
 
To systematically record the condition, identify de fects, and recommend maintenance activities of each  
bridge.  This information is applied on four levels : 
 
- The overall condition of the main components is u sed to form an evaluation of the condition of the 
bridge in general.  This type of information is usu ally used on a network level. 
 
- The condition of each subcomponent (refer to typica l inspection form at the end of Chapter 4) is used 
to provide information on a project level (i.e. spe cific bridge). 
 
- The extent and stage of the defects is used to prio ritise maintenance activities. 
 
- The quantity of required maintenance activities are  used for scheduling of maintenance work, and for 
determining short term budgetary needs. 
 
It is important to keep in mind how the inspection data will be used (Refer to Section 2.1). 
 
4.2 EQUIPMENT REQUIRED 
 
A principal inspection is mainly a visual inspectio n, and therefore only the minimum of equipment is r equired. 
 
The first requirement for all types of bridge inspe ction is a means of access to the components to be 
examined.  Most of the bridges in Uganda are relati vely low-level structures, and access to the compon ents 
above ground level can usually be obtained by means  of ladders or, where available and convenient, by using 
mobile hydraulic lifting equipment.  Plant such as that shown in Plate 4.1, which allows access to the  
underside of the bridge from the roadway above, is particularly useful for structures which cross larg e rivers 
or busy roads.  A fairly experienced bridge inspect or may be able to accomplish a satisfactory visual 
assessment using virtually no tools or equipment bu t his naked eye.  However, such visual assessment w ill 
usually be aided by standard inspection tools, such  as: 
 
- clip boards, chalk, markers 
- measuring tapes, measuring wheel, feeler gauge 
- ladder, level, plumb-line 
- thermometer 
- inspection mirror, binoculars, magnifying glass, camera, flash light  
- wire brush, pick, shovel 
- screwdrivers, pliers, hammers, torque wrench 
- Video camera where video footage is required 
- Existing photo’s/Inventory data to easily identify a specific bridge.
