June 2012  21  
 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 27 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  22  
 
4.3 SEQUENCE OF INSPECTION 
 
The inspection of a bridge should follow a systemat ic and organized procedure that is efficient and wi ll 
minimize the possibility of any item being overlook ed.  Adherence to the standard procedure outlined i n this 
document will ensure that valid comparisons can be made between inspections carried out in different 
locations at different times by different personnel .   
 
The precise sequence of inspection may vary accordi ng to the circumstances, but a logical order would be as 
follows: 
 
- Waterways 
- Foundations 
- Substructure - abutments and piers 
- Bearings 
- Superstructure 
- Non-structural elements - joints, drainage, guard rails, etc. 
- Approaches. 
 
In the case of underwater inspections the alternati ve sequence given in Chapter 5 should be considered . 
 
4.4 INSPECTION GUIDELINES (WHAT TO LOOK FOR) 
 
This section summarizes the components of bridge ty pes commonly found in Southern Africa, with guidanc e 
about those aspects that require examination.  The major bridge construction materials used locally ar e 
concrete, followed by steel and, trailing a long wa y behind, timber.  Because these are prone to diffe rent types 
of failure, alternative inspection strategies are u sually necessary. 
 
The guide does not cover components or aspects uniq ue to aluminium, timber (above the waterline), cast  iron 
or wrought iron bridges, suspension or cable-stayed  bridges or moveable bridges.  Such structures are rare in 
most countries, and information is available elsewh ere if these structures are encountered. 
 
As well as checking the current condition of each i ndividual component, the inspector should note whet her 
repairs or maintenance recommended at previous insp ections have been completed and are performing 
satisfactorily. 
 
The items that follow correspond with those in the model inspection report form.  (Refer to typical in spection 
form at the end of Chapter 4). 
 
The inspector must assess the current condition of each individual component, identify defects, recomm end 
repairs, and state whether a special inspection is required. 
--- Page 28 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  23 4.4.1 APPROACHES  
 
• Note the type of approach; whether concrete slab or  built-up pavement.  Check the condition of the 
approach for unevenness or settlement (Plate 4.2), using a straight-edge if necessary.  Ride over in a  
vehicle to check smoothness.  The existence of thes e defects may cause vehicles approaching the bridge  
to impart excessive impact loading on the structure . 
 
• Check for cracks in the approach slab or pavement. 
 
• Note the approach width if significant loss of edge  is observed. 
 
• Ensure that the fill of the approach embankments is  in sound condition, with no side erosion or voids.  
 
• Check that the drainage from the approaches is sati sfactory. 
 
• Check the shoulders for ruts and holes. 
 
• Check the approach guardrails as described in 4.4.1 4. 
 
4.4.2 WATERWAY  
 
The aquatic environment imposes its own set of dest ructive forces on bridges.  Here, tidal movements, wave 
action, rapid currents, heavy storms and major floo ds can be particularly damaging.  Special attention  should 
be given to channel shifting, aggradation, degradat ion, undermining and scour.  Of these, scour is pro bably the 
most important and the scour-susceptible locations shown in Figure 4.1 should be carefully monitored.  The 
foundations of piers, abutments, retaining, walls a nd training works should receive close attention be cause 
scour at these points can be particularly harmful.  Paving should also be assessed. 
 
Occasionally, underwater inspection is required in order to assess the true extent of scour and the 
effectiveness of scour prevention measures.  Guidan ce on various aspects of underwater inspection is g iven in 
Chapter 5. 
--- Page 29 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  24 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 30 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  25 
 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 31 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  26 • Determine if there have been any upstream or downst ream changes in the river's regime due to man-made 
or natural causes. 
 
- Generally, these could result from dam constructi on, property development, channel formation, 
collapse of a nearby structure, earth slippage and so on. 
 
- On inland basins, such as dams and lakes, regime changes may follow alterations to the rates of 
inflow or outflow. 
 
- In the vicinity of river mouths, natural processe s, such as littoral drift, may be altered by coasta l 
works. 
 
• Observe the adequacy of the waterway at the bridge opening with due regard to the amount of debris 
likely to be carried during floods. 
 
• Check the freeboard. 
 
- Normally the freeboard should be sufficient durin g high water. 
 
- On inland basins the freeboard should accommodate  the maximum static water level, wind set-up 
and wave run-up. 
 
- Combinations of flood debris, high tides and wave  run-up should be considered at the coast.  
 
• Check for debris and plant growth which may preclud e the free flow of water.  However, because natural  
vegetation prevents erosion, its indiscriminate rem oval should be avoided. 
 
• If the bridge opening constricts flow during floods , the resulting backwater effects should be assesse d.  
The backwater raises the level of the flood-plain a nd consequent flooding of adjacent, productive land  
could result in large claims for damages.  Besides this, constrictions can alter the natural processes  of 
scour and deposition and may lead to channel shifti ng. 
 
• Observe the channel cross-section.  Significant cha nges involving meandering, channel shifting, scour 
and deposition should be noted.  If necessary, soun dings should be taken and recorded.  Changes in the  
direction and path of flow during floods are partic ularly important. 
 
• Guide banks or spur dikes are embankments construct ed more or less parallel to the stream in order to 
direct the flow smoothly through openings.  Check t hat such banks are in a sound condition and are 
fulfilling their intended purpose which may be to: 
 
- restrict flow to a single channel 
- improve the flow distribution across the waterway  
- control the angle of attack on piers 
- stop meandering 
- prevent erosion of approach road embankments. 
 
• Spurs or groynes consist of embankments or walls co nstructed more or less normal to the stream bank or  
shore.  They are generally used in groups because, when isolated, they cause severe disturbances and 
--- Page 32 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  27 scouring near the ends.  However, there are excepti ons.  Check their soundness and ability to either: 
 
- prevent erosion of river banks, approach road emb ankments and dikes or 
- act in lieu of guide banks in directing flow into  a constricted waterway opening. 
 
• Dykes comprising embankments or walls are normally constructed on a flood plain to: 
 
- prevent flood waters from bypassing the bridge op ening 
- prevent backwater effects flooding adjacent prope rty 
- control drastic shifts in alluvial fans. 
 
Verify their ability to meet these requirements and  that they are impermeable and will not be 
overtopped by floods. 
 
• Observe the effects of wave action on the bridge, i ts approach road embankments and any associated 
training works.  The forces exerted by large waves breaking fully on a structure are impressive and ca n 
dislodge large rocks and pieces of protective armou r.  Moreover, the undertow occurring during storms 
can remove large quantities of beach. 
 
• Check the soundness and adequacy of revetments.  Ve rify the condition of wire cages and the size, 
distribution, texture and depth of rip-rap.  Ensure  the toe is secure and that it is not being undermi ned. 
 
• Check for local scour around piers and abutments th at intrude into the river.  The nosing of guide ban ks, 
piers and approach road embankments should also be inspected, as should the stream bed alongside 
channel banks or guide banks which run parallel to the direction of flow.  Local scour may also occur in 
the vicinity of river banks upon which the flow imp inges directly.  Note the location, lateral extent and 
depth of scour and obtain photographs where possibl e. 
 
The adequacy and condition of paving in the vicinit y of the bridge should also be checked. 
 
4.4.3 FOUNDATIONS  
 
Most foundation troubles manifest themselves in unf oreseen movements.  All foundations undergo small 
movements which, if they remain small and uniform, cause no distress to the structure.  Movements of l arge 
magnitudes, especially when they are differential m ovements, cause distress to nearly all structures u nless 
specific provisions have been made during design.  Although such movements may result from unpredicted  
settlement or failure of the underlying soil, other  causes include:  mining, subsidence, cavities in r ocks, scour, 
frost action and changes in the water table. 
 
Problems can also result from a lack of structural integrity and deterioration of the construction mat erials.  In 
this respect, the aquatic environment may impose ad ditional destructive forces.  Thus, besides those l isted in 
4.4.2 polluted water in rivers carrying industrial effluent as well as saline water and marine borers in coastal 
areas can be harmful. 
 
• Check for foundation movements.  Although this is d ifficult to detect without the use of periodic surv eys, 
any of the following defects may suggest a problem and should be noted:   
 
--- Page 33 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  28 - A departure from the proper geometry of the struc ture.  In particular, note the alignment, grade 
or slope of the deck surface. 
 
- Severe distortions, bumps, or dips in the bridge deck.  These may be evidenced by pools of 
rainwater on the deck. 
 
- Excessive or unusual movements at bearings and jo ints. 
 
- Cracking of abutments, wing walls, and end of bea ms. 
 
- A particularly effective check is to sight along railings; noting both the vertical and horizontal 
alignment. 
 
• Assess the general structural integrity of the foun dation as evidenced by deformations, tilting and 
displacements. 
 
• Check for scour along the whole perimeter of expose d piles, pile caps and pier footings (Plate 4.3).  Also 
check for scour or undermining along the faces of a butment foundations and training walls that are 
exposed to the stream flow (Plate 4.4).  The locati on, lateral dimensions and depth of scour holes sho uld 
be noted and, if possible, recorded photographicall y. 
 
• Verify that scour protection measures comply with t he specifications.  Check their adequacy and 
soundness and note the location and dimensions of a ny defects.  Obtain a photographic record, if 
possible. 
 
• If the foundations are keyed into rock check for se paration of the base of the footing from the underl ying 
rock formation.  Note if the resulting gap contains  sediment. 
 
• Check the soundness and appearance of all concrete components.  Inspect all concrete faces and note th e 
location and extent of abrasion, corrosion, honeyco mbing and pitting.  Note particularly any cracks an d 
spalls; determine their dimensions and obtain photo graphs where possible. 
 
• Note the presence of steel left protruding from the  finished concrete surface.  This includes 
reinforcement, prestressing strands, fixtures to ho ld the reinforcement and formwork in place, as well  as 
lifting rings for precast piles or other components . 
 
• In the case of poor quality concrete in cooler wate rs, check for the presence of marine rock borers, s uch 
as Pholas. 
 
• Assess the soundness of steel elements and note any  buckling of flanges and loss of section.  Inspect the 
condition of the protective coatings and note the p osition and extent of defective areas as well as an y 
associated loss of steel section.   
 
• Examine timber components for abrasion, cracking, s plintering, splitting and the condition of preserva tive 
treatments.  Obtain samples where necessary. 
 
• Examine timber components below the high-water leve l for the presence of marine borers.  Besides 
attacking poorly impregnated timber, borers initial ly invade along cracks and splits;  around improper ly 
--- Page 34 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  29 covered bolt holes;  and at the cut ends of members . 
 
• Check all exposed piles for buckling, tilt, lateral  movement and distress due to overdriving.  Record the 
presence of any debris lodged between piles. 
 
• Verify the condition of bracing and cross-members.  Assess the condition of bolts and bolt holes. 
 
4.4.5 SUBSTRUCTURE  
 
Examine all items of substructure - abutment breast  walls, wing walls, retaining walls, piers and colu mns - for 
excessive movements.  Alignment should be measured with sophisticated surveying equipment when 
available.  Alternatively, measurements may be obta ined using a plumb bob and rule. 
 
• Check any tendency for separation between abutment and wingwalls, especially where the two are 
separated by a construction joint.  (Plate 4.5). 
 
• Check the clearance between the deck beams and the abutment back wall.  Lack of, or excessive 
clearance may indicate a foundation problem. 
 
• Thoroughly check concrete for cracks, spalling and corrosion of reinforcement.  (Plates 4.6, 4.7 and 4 .8).  
Prepare a sketch if necessary.  Also see figures 5. 2, 5.3 and 5.4. 
 
• Verify that any relevant items, listed under FOUNDA TIONS, have been included. 
 
• Check foundations for evidence of abrasion, erosion  or scour. 
 
• Probe drains and weep holes with a rod to determine  whether they are clear or clogged.

UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual

--- Page 38 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  33 4.4.6 BEARINGS  
 
The geometry and condition of the bearings and the bearing seatings are important indicators not only of the 
condition of the bearing itself, but sometimes of t he general health of the structure as a hole.  Bear ings are 
usually at points where articulation is intended to  take place, so that the magnitude of movements at bearings, 
as well as at joints, is usually significant and ca n be measured with relatively simple equipment. 
 
If necessary, any debris, corrosion or rust should be removed to facilitate inspection. 
 
The particular factors to be looked at are: 
 
• The capability of the bearing to permit movement.  This will be inhibited by the factors below: 
 
- The type and extent of deterioration such as pits  and corrosion on the bearings and bearing 
plates. 
 
- Accumulation of debris (Plate 4.11). 
 
- Missing or broken components such as anchor bolts  and nuts. 
 
- Lack of lubrication, if possible.  Where provisio n is made for lubrication, check the adequacy of 
the provision and the renewal of lubricating materi als. 
 
- Evidence of drainage water falling on bearings. 
 
• The positioning and alignment of the bearing provid es complete contact across the bearing surface, and  
that keyways and thrust plates are properly engaged  but not binding. 
 
• The setting is correct in relation to the prevailin g temperature in order to ensure the necessary free dom of 
motion.  Note the clearance between the end of the main beams and abutment back wall. 
 
• The uniformity of the distribution of load between bearings.  In particular check if there is any upli ft.  In 
extreme cases this might be indicated by a clear sp ace between the bearing and the superstructure. 
 
• The tightness of anchor bolts and nuts and their fr eedom from excessive vibration. 
 
• Bearings and lateral shear keys (on curved or skew bridges) to determine if either are binding or if t hey 
have suffered damage from unforeseen movement in th e bridge. 
 
• Bearing seatings for any signs of relative movement  between the seating and the supporting structure.  
This is particularly important when no dowels or an chor bolts have been used in locating elastomeric 
bearings. 
 
• The seatings of superstructures on bearings and of bearings on substructures for any signs of cracking  or 
spalling (Plate 4.9 and 4.10).  Such deterioration,  which is usually caused by a concentration of load s 
along edges, can lead to a serious reduction in the  security of the seating in the worst case and to 
deterioration of reinforcement in concrete in milde r cases. 
--- Page 39 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  34  
Different types of bearings are prone to different potential problems.  Special attention should be gi ven to the 
following aspects: 
 
• Elastomeric bearings should be examined for splitti ng, tearing or cracking of the outer casing, and fo r 
bulging and distortion caused by excessive compress ive forces or shear movements or both. The first 
signs of distress in elastomeric bearings are usual ly in the form of horizontal cracks near the juncti on of 
the rubber pad and the steel shim (Plate 4.9). 
 
• Elastomeric bearings should also be checked with re gard to excessive rotation which is usually indicat ed 
by differences in thickness between the back and fr ont of the bearing.  Elastomeric pads are often loc ated 
inside the superstructure and cannot be inspected.  In such cases check the roadway for riding quality  
across the adjacent expansion joint.  Unevenness ma y be indicative of deterioration. 
 
• Excessive rotation in rocker bearings should be inv estigated since continued movement may cause the 
bearing to become unstable and the span to drop ont o the seating. 
 
• With bearings using a low friction sliding material , such as "Teflon" (P.T.F.E.) on stainless steel, 
establish that movement is taking place at the slid ing surface.  Cases have been reported where the hi gh 
initial friction of the bearing has not been overco me and large forces have been transmitted to the 
substructure.  To preserve a durable and uniform sl iding surface, measures are usually taken to exclud e 
dirt from the bearing and these should be checked f or their effectiveness.  Also, check for excessive 
movements as this may indicate that the initial pre set was applied in the wrong direction. 
 
• The Freyssinet or concrete hinge type bearing shoul d be checked for spalling in the neck of the hinge.  
 
• Simple sliding bearings used in many of the older b ridges tend to become more resistant to movement 
with age.  In the case of certain materials such as  lead, bitumen and roofing felt, they are probably not 
functioning at all.  In such cases there may be cra cking or spalling of the concrete in the area of th e 
bearing seating.  (Plate 4.10). 
 
--- Page 40 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  35 
 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 41 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual

UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual

--- Page 43 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  38 4.4.7 CONCRETE BEAMS AND GIRDERS  
 
Check the following: 
 
Alignment along longitudinal axis for vertical cant  or lack of parallelism with bridge axis and among members. 
 
Noticeable vertical deflection or excessive vibrati on under live loading.  Also check for independent movement of 
beams under load. 
 
• Spalling concrete, giving special attention to poin ts of bearing where friction from movements and hig h 
edge pressures can cause damage (Plate 4.10). 
 
• Outermost beams in an overbridge of beam and slab c onstruction.  These are particularly vulnerable to 
damage from high vehicles (Plate 4.12).  In many ca ses such damage is only superficial but can lead to  
corrosion of reinforcing steel in the long term. 
 
• Cracking in a variety of forms.  Ideally, a map of the cracking of the main members should be produced , 
so that the implications in terms of strength and d urability can be carefully examined.  If cracking i s 
severe, note the size and distribution of the crack s and try to determine their penetration (Plates 4. 13, 4.14 
and 4.15).  Also see figure 5.5, 5.6 and 5.7. 
 
• Cover to the reinforcement.  This is usually checke d with a "Covermeter", but sometimes indications of  
inadequacy of cover appear as rust stains on the su rface of the concrete due to the rusting of the 
reinforcing steel.  In extreme cases spalling may o ccur, exposing the reinforcement.  In milder cases,  the 
rusting may be due to thin wire used for securing s huttering and left in the concrete.  It may be that  
surface stains occurred during construction, but ea ch manifestation should be carefully inspected. 
 
• Examine the interior faces of flanges and webs insi de box girders for signs of cracking.  Interior 
diaphragms must also be examined, particularly for any signs of cracking at their junction with the we bs.  
Drainage outlets should be probed to check for clog ging. 
 
• Accumulation of debris along surfaces or between me mbers. 
 
• Loose rendering, particularly on soffits and edge b eams where it may fall off and cause injury to traf fic 
passing underneath. 
 
• Condition of any repairs made previously. 
 
 
 
--- Page 44 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  39 Prestressed concrete bridges should be given specia l attention and the possibility of the following ad ditional 
defects should be noted: 
 
• Longitudinal cracks in the flanges and transverse c racks in beams.  (Plate 4.14) 
 
• Spalling or cracking of concrete near bearings or c able anchorages. 
 
• Unfortunately, the most important factors relating to the condition of a prestressed concrete beam can not 
be assessed by a visual inspection.  These are the position of the cable, the uniformity and density o f 
grouting of the cable ducts, corrosion and fracture  of strand of the cable.  Some of these factors cou ld be 
checked by boring small holes into the concrete, bu t usually radiographic techniques have to be employ ed 
to obtain more reliable information. 
 
• Newly-constructed prestressed bridges should be sub jected to routine checks of time-dependent 
behaviour, particularly long-term deflections of be ams.  This check should start during construction. The 
first recorded levels should be taken immediately a fter prestressing or, in the case of precast beams,  after 
casting the in situ  concrete.  Care must be exercised to locate and pr eserve the bench marks, so they will 
not be affected by progressing bridge and road cons truction.  Such surveys should ideally be continued  
until the structure has been in service for at leas t 12 months.  While checks on the deflections of 
prestressed bridges are of primary importance, cons ideration should be given to conducting similar 
surveys on new bridges of all forms of construction . 
 
4.4.8 CONCRETE DECKS AND SLABS  
 
Check for the following: 
 
• Cracking - ideally a map of cracking patterns shoul d be prepared and information collected on the size , 
distribution and penetration of cracks.  A photogra phic record is often useful, particularly if signif icant 
cracks have been outlined in a distinctive material , such as chalk or paint.  The causes of cracking a re 
numerous.  Sometimes the cause is obvious from the form and nature of the cracks and in such cases, th e 
inspection might well embrace diagnosis.  In other cases, the causes are complex and can only be 
assessed after careful examination of records and d rawings, accompanied by calculation. 
 
• Scaling - this is the gradual and continual loss of  surface mortar and aggregate over irregular areas.  The 
depth and size of the area should be recorded, so a s to monitor the progression of this defect. It is most 
commonly experienced on horizontal surfaces exposed  to the weather and to traffic, but it can occur 
elsewhere and is often an indication of frost damag e. 
 
• Spalling - defined as a loss of concrete between fr acture surfaces.  On decks and slabs it may take th e 
form of circular or oval depressions. 
 
• Corrosion of reinforcement - difficult to detect in  early stages, but gradually leads to surface 
discolouration of the concrete and in extreme cases  to cracking and delamination. 
--- Page 45 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  40 • Leakage - leakage of water takes place through conc rete decks, particularly at construction joints and  
where there is a risk of cracking in thin component  sections of the deck, e.g. at kerbs in in-situ con crete 
topping over precast beams (Plate 4.13). 
 
• Leaching - the leakage or seepage of water through cracks and voids in the hardened concrete may 
dissolve out calcium hydroxide and other constituen t materials in the concrete.  Its effects are usual ly 
most evident on the soffits of decks and take the f orm of staining, efflorescence or encrustation at c racks 
and, in extreme cases, in the formation of stalacti tes).  Apart from its adverse effect on appearance,  it can 
in time present a corrosion threat to reinforcement , because of the gradual loss of alkalinity of the 
concrete (Plate 4.13). 
 
• Porous or pervious concrete - the danger here is th e ingress of water leading to corrosion of steel an d to 
enhancement of the deleterious effects of chemicals  and frost on the concrete.  It should be suspected  
where workmanship is poor, or where good compaction  of concrete is difficult, as in the case of densel y 
reinforced slabs and in the corners of formwork (Pl ates 4.16 and 4.17). 
 
• Wear of deck surface - where the deck forms the run ning surface of traffic, it will be subject to wear  and 
polishing.  The extent of wear resistance to skiddi ng should be checked periodically, but this should not 
necessarily be done by the bridge inspectors.  In m any cases, this problem is avoided by surfacing the  
deck with a bitumen-based wearing surface. 
 
Deterioration of the deck can sometimes lead to ref lection cracking or deformation of the surfacing ma terial, but it may be difficult to distinguish 
between this type of failure and the failure in the  surfacing material itself.  Removal of small areas  of the surfacing may be necessary to verify 
the precise state of affairs.  Bumps or irregularit ies in the deck surface can be assessed by riding o ver the bridge in a vehicle. 
 
• Excessive deformation, deflection or vibration - th ese factors may be indicators of the condition of t he 
deck supports as well as of the deck itself.  Surve y and document the deck profile along the centrelin e and 
kerb lines, if required for long-span decks. 
 
• Accidental damage - this can take a variety of form s, but the most common is from damage to 
overbridges by high vehicles. 
 
• Chemical attack - in certain aggressive industrial environments or in coastal regions concrete may be 
subject to chemical attack which causes surface cru mbling of material.  Similar forms of deterioration  can 
occur in less aggressive environments due to defect ive materials or workmanship.  The phenomenon of 
alkali-aggregate reaction occurs widely in the West ern Cape and to a lesser extent in the Eastern Cape  
and on the Witwatersrand.  It can usually be identi fied by the type of pattern cracking illustrated in  Plates 
4.18 and 4.19.  Sulphate attack on concrete has als o been known to occur in various parts of the Repub lic. 
 
--- Page 46 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  41 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 47 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  42 
 
 
 
--- Page 48 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  43 • If service ducts are present verify that they have been correctly detailed and that they are in a soun d 
condition. 
 
• Check the condition of any repairs made previously.  
 
4.4.9 STEEL BEAMS, GIRDERS AND TRUSSES  
 
Among the factors that should be checked during the  inspection are the following: 
 
• Condition of the protective system - paint systems suffer various forms of deterioration.  Early detec tion 
of this deterioration will reduce maintenance subst antially.  Inspection of the protective system can be 
coupled with a search for debris accumulations and an assessment of the general cleanliness of the 
structure.  Debris between beams and on flange area s is particularly likely at river crossings after p eriods 
of flooding.  An inspection procedure for protectiv e systems is given in 4.4.16. 
 
• Corrosion - probably the most important factor affe cting the well-being of a steel structure.  It is 
important to assess its magnitude, its location and  the form that it is taking.  Further, some attempt  should 
be made to assess the loss of effective structural section caused by corrosion and to identify the cau se of 
the corrosion.  Junctions of steelwork with masonry , concrete and other structural materials should be  
given particular attention.  Mating and rubbing sur faces require special attention because of the adve rse 
conditions to which they are sometimes subject and because the critical areas are out of sight.  On 
weathering steel the cohesion and impermeability of  the rust film are qualities to be examined. 
 
• Fracture - apparent as breaks in members and elemen ts of the structure.  The ease of detection is dire ctly 
related to the scale and size of the defect.  It ma y be due to a variety of causes such as overloading , brittle 
fracture, stress corrosion or fatigue. 
 
• Cracking - usually occurs in welds and adjacent met al and is caused by stress fluctuation and stress 
concentration.  Members and connections subject to high stress fluctuations and stress reversals in se rvice 
are the most suspect.  Indifferent quality of weldi ng and sudden changes in the cross-section of the 
member are indications of potential problems from t his cause.  Attention should also be paid to welded  
attachments to load-carrying members (such as those  carrying ancillary equipment). 
 
• Excessive vibration and noise - these factors in th emselves may not be structurally damaging, unless t he 
vibrations are setting up resonance and overstressi ng certain members.  They can, however, be general 
indicators of the condition of the structure, parti cularly if vibration and noise have become more evi dent 
since a previous inspection. 
 
• Deformation and deflection - excessive deformation or deflection is one of the best visual indicators of 
the state of the structure.  Small movements due to  heavy traffic loading can usually be detected visu ally, 
but longer-term movements, due to the structure's s elf-weight, will require detection by levelling 
instruments. 
 
• Alignment and geometry - permanent deformations, wh ich are detectable only in terms of the expected 
form of structure, come into this category.  They m ay be due to a degree of overstressing such as migh t be 
caused by impact. 
 
--- Page 49 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  44 • Buckling, kinking, warping and waviness - these ter ms usually refer to a form of deformation associate d 
with members in compression.  If present to a signi ficant degree, they can considerably reduce resista nce 
to compressive forces. 
 
• Loose bolts and rivets - detection of these defects  requires close inspection, unless there are some 
accompanying movements or noises.  Occasionally tel l-tale cracks appear in the paint films between 
coverplates and main members.  This can be fairly m eaningful in the case of friction grip-bolted 
connections. 
 
• Excessive wear - check for this in the case of memb ers accommodating movement, such as pins in joints 
of trusses. 
 
• Conditions in closed members - if sealing of these members has been attempted then the degree to which  
it is successful should be checked.  Accumulations of water due to leakage and to condensation are not  
uncommon occurrences leading to potential corrosion  problems in certain parts of a box.  In extreme 
cases, mould and fungus growth can develop to the d etriment of the protective paint system.  If 
ventilation is provided, verify that this is effect ive.  Inspection in closed and confined places shou ld be 
carried out with good lighting and with suitable pr ecautions to ensure that the atmosphere does not 
contain toxic or explosive substances. 
 
4.4.10 SURFACING 
 
Check for cracking in the surfacing material.  The most commonly used materials, such as bitumen-based  
ones, are thermoplastic and are therefore prone to cracking in cold weather.  If possible, establish i f the 
cracking is due to failure of the surfacing materia l or due to excessive movement or deterioration of the 
underlying deck. 
 
• Check for excessive deformation.  This is normally caused by the combined effects of traffic and warm 
weather.  Excessive deformation impairs riding qual ity and in turn can substantially increase the dyna mic 
loading and vibration from moving vehicles. 
 
• Check for loss of skid resistance.  As a result of polishing under traffic, the surfacing may become m ore 
slippery with time.  Treatment of the surface may b e required to restore the skid resistance. 
 
4.4.11 EXPANSION JOINTS  
 
Expansion joints are weak points in bridge construc tion and maintenance costs are high.  Statistically , more 
than half of the problems reported on concrete deck  bridges are related to expansion joints.  Conseque ntly, it 
is most important that these items be thoroughly ch ecked.  The following procedure is recommended. 
 
• Check for loose joints or joint components.  This f ault manifests itself by rattling under traffic and  by 
cracks developing between the joint and the adjoini ng surfacing.  Adhesion or the tie-down of the join t to 
the seating can usually be checked by the ringing n ote when the joint is tapped with a hammer. 
 
• Check freedom of movement, clearance and alignment.   There should be adequate space for the joint to 
function under the prevailing temperatures.  Loss o f clearance can be caused by relative movement, 
--- Page 50 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  45 wrong setting of the joint, accumulated debris (par ticularly in urban areas)(Plate 4.20) and expansion  due 
to alkali-aggregate reaction. 
 
• Check the vertical profile.  Excessive displacement  may present a hazard to small and two-wheeled 
vehicles. 
 
• Check for displaced joint filler.  Premoulded joint  fillers frequently deteriorate or become loose and  fall 
out of the joint (Plate 4.21). 
 
• Check for horizontal movements.  Horizontal offsett ing of a skew deck relative to the abutment may be 
indicative of alkali-aggregate reaction.  Horizonta l movement can also take place during high floods o r in 
combination with rotation where insufficient provis ion for thermal movements and creep has been made. 
 
• Check for leakage of water through joints.  Water s tains on abutment walls and on piers may assist in 
locating faults.  Where joints are designed to allo w the free passage of water and debris, the adequac y of 
the drainage system should be checked. 
 
• Check for cracking of the surfacing at buried joint s.  This type of cracking tends to develop with tim e and 
is not necessarily indicative of a faulty joint.  I n the event of excessive cracking, local repair to the 
surfacing may be justified.  However, the joint may  only be inspected after the surfacing has been 
removed. 
 
• Check the type and extent of any deterioration of t he nosing (Plate 4.21). 
 
• Check that gland-type seals are continuous and have  a uniform width. 
 
• Check that nosing are adequately bonded to the stru cture and that the legs of steel angles are continu ous 
across the full width of the deck. 
 
• Check that seals are continuous across kerbs; espec ially at low points. 
 
• Check the behaviour of any service ducts that may b e present. 
 
--- Page 51 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  46 
 
 
--- Page 52 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  47 4.4.12 DRAINAGE 
 
Drainage is an important inspection item since trap ped, ponded, flowing or splashing water can cause 
considerable damage to a bridge over a period of ti me and represents a safety hazard to traffic.  The main 
defects to be checked include the following: 
 
• Ponding on the deck (Plate 4.22) 
 
• Water stains.  Water stains on beams, slabs, piers,  columns and abutments may indicate leaky pipes, fi lled 
gutters, inadequate drainage systems or insufficien t consideration of the drainage problem. 
 
• Clogged or inadequate drainage gulleys and pipes.  Look for evidence of debris on, or around, the inle t 
after a storm, and for scaling and staining of conc rete. 
 
• Drain outlets.  Drain outlets (scuppers) should be checked to ensure that water is not discharged wher e it 
may be detrimental to other components of the struc ture, or where it may cause erosion of fill and 
embankment material or spill onto the roadway below . 
 
• Damaged pipes.  Check the run of pipes for damage a nd leakage. 
 
• The accumulation of debris.  A substantial amount o f debris can find its way into the drainage system and 
there may be traps for it at strategic points.  Bot h these and rodding points should be checked for th eir 
cleanliness and operational efficiency. 
 
• Blowing of water on the structure.  Water can be bl own onto the soffit of beams and slabs by the wind,  
aided by the funnelling effect of the abutments.  T he effectiveness of any downstands or grooves along  
the edge to prevent the spread of water in this way  should be checked. 
 
4.4.13 KERBS AND SIDEWALKS 
 
• Examine kerbs and sidewalks for vegetation, cracks,  spalling and deterioration (Plates 4.23). 
 
• Check joints for differential movements which could  later result in a step or a gap which would be a 
hazard to pedestrians. 
 
• Check the condition of manhole covers and service d ucts. 
--- Page 53 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  48 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 54 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  49  
4.4.14 PARAPETS, RAILINGS AND GUARDRAILS  
 
• Check for damage due to traffic impact.  Assess the  nature and extent of the damage and any possible l oss 
of strength.  (Plate 4.24) 
 
• Check for cracks, spalling and scaling of concrete,  and corrosion of reinforcement, in concrete parape ts 
(Plate 4.25). 
 
• Examine metal railings for corrosion and check the condition of the protective system (Plate 4.28).  
Special attention should be paid to the region arou nd the base of the supporting posts (Plate 4.26).  An 
inspection procedure for protective coatings is giv en in 4.4.16. 
 
• Check the tightness of holding down and connecting bolts, taking into account in the latter case the 
provision for movement in the parapet, or between t he parapet and the guardrails on approaches (Plate 
4.26). 
 
• Check for missing bolts, nuts and washers. 
 
• Examine the quality of welding.  Welds should be ca pable of transferring the necessary forces between 
members so that they can effectively contain errant  vehicles. 
 
• Check the alignment of the parapet and guardrails ( Plate 4.27).  The overlaps between guardrails on 
approaches and parapets on bridge decks should be c apable of preventing collision between a vehicle an d 
the end post of the parapet.  At the end of the bri dge, check that the guardrail does not protrude in front of 
the face of the parapet, thereby presenting a chise l edge to traffic.  Note any feature of the parapet  that 
may bring a colliding vehicle to rest abruptly.  Ch eck the movement of handrails across expansion join ts. 
 
• Check the adequacy and soundness of fencing in the vicinity of the bridge. 
 
4.4.15 SIGNS AND ANCILLARY EQUIPMENT  
 
• Check that all signs required to show a weight restriction , reduced speed limit  or reduced vertical 
clearance  are in their proper location.  The check should in clude signs at or on the structure, including 
bridge number plates , as well as any necessary advance warning signs  and hazard plates . 
 
• Check the signs to ensure that the lettering is cle ar and legible, the colours have not faded, the ret ro-
reflective material is effective, and that they are  generally in good condition.  Check the position a nd 
clearance of signs. 
 
• In the case of navigable waters, check fendering, n avigation aids, warning devices and clearances that  are 
intended to protect the bridge from waterborne traf fic.

--- Page 58 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  53 4.5 CONDITION RATING PROCEDURE 
 
Whatever type of organizational structure is adopte d by an inspecting authority, an essential requirem ent of a 
principal inspection is a written report of the con dition of the structure.  Ease, uniformity and comp leteness of 
reporting can be increased by the use of a prepared  checklist or standard form, completed at the time of 
inspection. 
 
The diversity of materials, structural configuratio ns and construction techniques for bridges in south ern Africa 
is not high, and the range within one authority may  be quite limited.  In most cases, therefore, it sh ould be 
possible to make use of a checklist, which, while r emaining relatively simple and generalized, covers the 
important items and aspects of all but a few of the  bridges to be inspected.  The use of such a checkl ist will 
inevitably facilitate matters for the inspector, an d will reduce the possibility of items being overlo oked.  
Perhaps the greatest advantage of standard forms, h owever, is that uniformity of inspection is increas ed, with 
the result that inspections conducted by different personnel at different times and on different struc tures can 
be compared.  This is an essential requirement for an efficient system of bridge management. 
 
The typical report form (for a specific bridge) is shown on pages 4/37 to 4/48.  It has been developed  for this 
document and reflects current practice.  It is offe red as a model form, aided specifically at Southern  African 
conditions, for use in principal inspections.  The form can be adapted to suit the individual requirem ents of an 
inspecting authority. 
 
It is important to define exactly what is meant by the condition rating  of a component as opposed to the 
appraisal rating  of a component.  Condition rating is a comparison of the current condition with that of what 
it should have been when new, irrespective of possi ble substandard design parameters.  Appraisal on th e other 
hand is a comparison of the actual design parameter s at a bridge with the current standards e.g. a bri dge might 
have been designed for a road width of 10 m, but th e current geometrical standards might require a wid th of 
13 m for that specific road class on which the brid ge is located.  If there is nothing wrong with the bridge, it 
will still be rated as being in a good condition. 
 
The main objectives were put forward in Section 4.1 .  For this reason the inspection form has been div ided 
into two levels , namely main components  (bold print) and subcomponents  (normal print). There is also a 
difference in approach when evaluating the conditio n of main- and subcomponents. 
 
The following considerations should apply when rati ng the main components: 
 
- Approaches:  Smooth transition onto the bridge an d stability of the fill and the probable 
effects on the bridge. 
 
- Waterway:   Free flow of water under the bridge, stability of the waterway and the probable 
effect on the bridge. 
 
- Superstructure:  Structural integrity 
 
- Substructure:  Structural integrity 
 
- Roadway:   Smooth and safe passage over the bridg e. 
 
--- Page 59 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  54 A situation may therefore arise where, for example,  the seal of an expansion joint is lost (which cons titutes a 
poor (3) condition for the expansion joint) but the  roadway over the bridge still provides smooth and safe 
passage over the bridge.  The main component (Roadw ay) will therefore still be considered to be in a 
satisfactory to good condition (providing there are  no other defects).  On the other hand, if a main b eam of a 
bridge is in a poor condition, the structural integ rity of the superstructure in general is most likel y to be 
affected, and therefore it should be rated as margi nal or poor.  The significance of a defect on the c riteria set 
out above should be considered when rating the main  components. 
 
When rating the subcomponents, the emphasis is on r ecording the condition of each subcomponent, 
irrespective of its relative importance.  Explanato ry tables for different components are given in Tab les 4.1 to 
4.10.  The inspector must simply compare the actual  condition against the descriptions in the table an d read of 
the corresponding rating. 
 
A numerical scale (0-9) is used to rate the conditi on of both the main components and subcomponents.  The 
corresponding condition for each number is clearly defined in the explanatory tables. 
 
The ratings should be based on the prevailing (gene ral) condition of a subcomponent.  Keep in mind tha t the 
condition rating is used to obtain an idea of the g eneral condition of a bridge for management level p lanning.  
Special provision is made in the Maintenance report  (discussed in next section) to report on specific defects or 
problems.  For example: if the guardrails over a br idge has some localized accident damage, but are ot herwise 
in a good condition, then it’s condition would be c onsidered to be satisfactory to good.  However, if the local 
damage had been severe that the integrity of the gu ardrail is in question, then its condition would be  marginal 
to poor. 
 
All the main components must be rated .  Where subcomponents such as foundations are not visible, a rating 
for the main component (substructure) must then be based on the condition of the sighted subcomponents  and 
symptoms (or the lack thereof) of potential problem s at the unsighted subcomponent.  However, when rat ing 
the individual subcomponents, no rating must be giv en to the unsighted subcomponent.  A remark to that  
effect must then be written into the remarks column . 
 
The condition rating must be based on the current c ondition, and not on the potential condition if not hing is 
done about the problem.

UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  61 4.6 IDENTIFICATION OF DEFECTS AND RECOMMENDATION OF  REPAIRS 
 
The standard reporting form lists typical defects a nd maintenance activities that could be expected at  
bridges.  This serves as a guideline for identifica tion of defects and recommending remedial action at  a 
bridge.  Special provision has been made for activi ties not on the list. 
 
The stage and extent of the defect (or deterioratio n of the component) must be described using the 
following options: 
 
Stage   : B = Beginning   Extent  : L = Localised  
   E = Established     D = Distributed 
   A = Advanced      E = Extensive 
 
Where the defect will be repaired a reference to th e recommended maintenance activity must be given. 
The inspector must select the appropriate activity for each defect, estimate the quantity of work invo lved 
(measured in the indicated units). The above inform ation is then used to prioritise maintenance activi ties, 
estimate Maintenance costs at each bridge, and also  used to plan short and medium term budgets. 
--- Page 67 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  62 5. SPECIAL INSPECTIONS AND RELATED TOPICS 
 
5.1 OBJECTIVES 
 
Special inspections will usually be made in connect ion with specific problems, which in turn often rel ate to specific 
site conditions, structural behaviour, material def ects or loading patterns. 
 
The objectives for special inspections will general ly be to identify and diagnose specific problems an d propose 
remedial actions.  These types of inspections usual ly require the involvement of experienced bridge en gineers or 
related specialists and the use of more sophisticat ed assessment techniques.  The art in conducting th ese inspections 
successfully, often lies in the ability to choose t he appropriate assessment techniques. 
 
This chapter therefore discusses a wide range of to pics that might be applicable to special inspection s.  It is left 
over to the bridge engineer to decide which of the information is applicable to a specific condition u nder 
investigation. 
 
5.2 ASSESSMENT TECHNIQUES 
 
A number of techniques are available to the bridge inspector to assist in the assessment of the qualit y of 
materials, the condition of components and the beha viour of the structure as a whole.  They range from  cheap, 
well-tried methods that require minimal expertise t o expensive, highly specialized techniques that are  virtually 
restricted to research tools.  It has to be admitte d that the accuracy, under field conditions, of man y of the 
available techniques is not high.  For this reason they should only be considered to supplement, and n ot replace, 
the critical assessment of a diligent, experienced inspector. 
 
5.2.1 ASSESSMENT OF CONCRETE 
 
5.2.1.1 Monitoring of cracks 
 
The width of a crack can be measured with an optica l gauge.  Changes in width can be measured using a Demec 
gauge or similar and fixed studs placed either side  of the crack.  A very useful indication of movemen t can be 
obtained by watching for a fracture in a small stri p of glass or ceramic plate stuck permanently over the crack. 
 
5.2.1.2 Reinforcement cover 
 
The amount and location of reinforcing steel in con crete members can usually be ascertained by means o f a 
“Covermeter”, a portable and convenient instrument which employs the principle of magnetic induction.  In some 
cases, however, the only really reliable method may  be to expose the steel for visual inspection. 
 
5.2.1.3 Concrete strength 
 
The following basic methods are commonly used in ev aluating the in situ strength of concrete: 
--- Page 68 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  63  
(i) Hardness tests 
 
These types of tests involve hitting the surface of  the concrete in a standard manner and either 
measuring the rebound of the object hitting the con crete or measuring the size of the indentation.  
For a non-destructive, cursory assessment of concre te strength the rebound-hammer (also known as 
the Schmidt-hammer) is often used.  It consists of a plunger which is held against the concrete and 
hit with a spring loaded hammer, the rebound of whi ch is recorded.  It is essential that the 
instrument is calibrated before use and that the te sted surface is smooth.  The scatter of results is 
appreciable and a number of measurements are usuall y taken and averaged.  The method gives 
indicative rather than exact results, but is useful  in determining the uniformity of concrete 
throughout a structure. 
 
(ii) Vibration methods 
 
The ultrasonic pulse velocity test is a method of a ssessing the quality of concrete by measuring the 
velocity with which a longitudinal pulse of vibrati ons passes through it.  The technique is accurate 
and versatile, but requires care in operation and a  certain amount of experience in interpreting the 
results.  It can be used to assess the homogeneity of the in situ concrete, and to locate cracks and 
voids in the material.  The strength and elastic mo dulus of the concrete can also be determined but, 
with strength especially, the instrument must be ca librated against concrete of similar mix 
proportions, and this is not without its problems.  One major disadvantage of the technique is that 
the results can be significantly affected by the pr esence of reinforcing steel, and in bridge 
structures, especially superstructures, the percent age of reinforcing steel is usually high.  
Mechanical sonic pulse methods use the measured vel ocity of a wave transmitted through concrete 
to calculate the dynamic modulus of elasticity.  A longitudinal or compression wave is initiated by 
a single impact or hammer blow and the time taken f or it to travel between two pick-ups is 
measured.  Formulae are used to determine the wave velocity and then the modulus of elasticity 
which in turn gives an indication of compressive an d flexural strengths. 
 
(iii) Radiometric and Radiographic methods 
 
Concrete will absorb X-rays or gamma rays which pas s through it and the degree of absorption is 
an indication of the density of the concrete.  The technique can be useful for assessing concrete 
structures, especially pre-stressed concrete decks.   It can provide a permanent record of the 
homogeneity and quality of the concrete, of defects  in reinforcing bars or in pre-stressing cables 
(errors in positioning, corrosion, lack of bond), a nd of the quality of grouting.  Although the 
equipment is portable, its use presents a health ha zard.  The methods are expensive and normally 
confined to defining faults rather than searching f or them. 
 
(iv) Core drilling 
 
Because of the many variables affecting the correla tion of concrete properties in the non-
destructive test methods outlined above, concrete s trength, when critical, should be determined by 
laboratory tests on cores.  However, the relative v alues of properties indicated by non-destructive 
field tests are a useful aid in selecting the locat ions from which cores are obtained.  Cores provide 
a sample of the interior of the concrete member for  visual inspection or chemical analyses.  The 
density of the concrete may be determined from a sa mple core, and this may be important in some 
cases.  Usually, however, cores are drilled and tes ted to assess the strength of the concrete, and 
although this can be a very accurate approach, cert ain procedures should be adhered to, and the 
results interpreted with care. 
--- Page 69 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  64  
5.2.1.4  Delamination of concrete decks 
 
The early detection of delamination in concrete dec ks is important as it may indicate serious corrosio n of 
reinforcement.  Unsurfaced concrete decks can often  be assessed by a visual examination.  Determinatio n of the 
deterioration of asphalt-covered decks requires spe cialized testing methods.  Not only can the asphalt  hide defects 
until they are well advanced, but it is often diffi cult to distinguish between deterioration in the co ncrete slab and 
debonding of the overlay.  Some of the more commonl y used methods are: 
 
(i) Chain-drag survey 
 
A simple but efficient test for delamination in con crete decks.  This is a sonic technique based on mo nitoring the audible sound generated by 
a chain dragged across the deck surface.  The metho d is particularly effective for decks with less tha n 25 mm of asphalt, and is useful for 
identifying anomalous areas for further investigati on.   
 
(ii) Ground-penetration radar 
 
This technique has proven in many cases to be the m ost reliable of the available methods, but is 
expensive to perform.  Specialized knowledge is req uired to interpret the reflection profiles. 
 
(iii) Thermography 
 
This is one of the quickest and the least expensive  test methods.  It is better suited to the rapid as sessment of the overall condition of a large 
number of decks than to defining exact areas of con crete deterioration for contract purposes.  Success ful results are weather-dependent, and 
thermography is best used when the air temperature is not less than 18 degrees, the wind no greater th an 40 km/h and the relative humidity 
less than 50%. 
 
5.2.2 ASSESSMENT OF STEEL 
 
5.2.2.1 Identification of material 
 
A visual distinction between wrought iron and steel  bridges can usually be made on the basis of tensio n members; 
eye bars and pin-connected links are used for the f ormer as opposed to connection plates and rivets in  the latter.  
Spark tests can also be used to distinguish between  steel and wrought iron, and high and low carbon st eels. 
 
5.2.2.2 Material strength 
 
In the case of metals, identification of the metal in rather general terms, e.g. steel, wrought iron, aluminium, will 
define the modulus of elasticity.  Estimates of str ength are possible if hardness tests are combined w ith the 
identification.  Hardness tests should if possible be made on a smooth surface at some distance from a ny edge and 
paint, rust and millscale should be removed.  When tests are made on pitted surfaces, the location of the point of the 
hardness tester relative to even minor pits can hav e a sizeable effect on the hardness reading. 
 
5.2.2.3 Evaluation of cracks 
 
The evaluation of cracks in metals may be accomplis hed by one or more of the following techniques.  
 
(i) Dye penetration 
 
Penetrant tests detect discontinuities that are ope n to the surface and can be effectively used for 
both ferrous and non-ferrous materials.  Surface di scontinuities such as cracks, seams, laps and 
laminations are indicated by these methods. 
 
(ii) Magnetic field disturbance techniques 
 
--- Page 70 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  65 The Magnetic Crack Definer identifies cracks in mag netic materials by setting up a magnetic field 
in the test material.  A crack causes a localized d isturbance in the magnetic field and the crack size  
is proportional to the severity of the disturbance.   The equipment is portable and battery-operated.  
The Magnetic-particle method is an inspection proce dure used to determine the presence of defects 
at or near the surface of ferromagnetic objects. 
 
Interference with the lines of magnetic flux caused  by irregularities in the material such as cracks 
or non-metallic inclusions are detected by the appl ication of a fine powder of magnetic material 
which tends to pile up and bridge over such discont inuities.  A surface crack is indicated by a line 
of the fine particles following the outline of the crack, and a subsurface flaw by a fuzzy collection 
of the fine particles on the surface near the defec t. 
 
(iii) Ultrasonic techniques 
 
Ultrasonic vibrations are frequently and successful ly used to detect minute internal defects in 
ferrous metals.  They normally require specific sur face preparation, the use of a coupler, a cathode 
ray tube display and interpretation by a skilled op erator. 
 
Radiometric and radiographic techniques 
 
Radiographic examination involves the passage of X- rays or gamma rays through the metal into a 
light-sensitive film.  Areas containing defects suc h as cavities or cracks absorb less radiation than 
the denser areas, and show up as darker areas on th e developed picture.  During bridge inspection, 
the method is normally confined to defining cracks rather than searching for them. 
5.2.2.4 Corrosion 
 
The degree of corrosion may vary between light rust ing on previously protected surfaces to pronounced rusting on 
unprotected or badly protected surfaces.  The follo wing techniques may be used to assess various types  of corrosion. 
 
total corrosion  : electrical resistance 
corrosion rate  : linear polarisation 
stress corrosion  : potential monitoring 
pits and flaw  : potential monitoring, acoustic emi ssion.  Eddy current test.  
 
5.2.3 ASSESSMENT OF OVERALL BEHAVIOUR 
 
The use of surveying techniques can be very valuabl e for monitoring the various types of horizontal an d vertical 
movement, absolute or differential, to which bridge  structures are susceptible, in areas or situations  where long-term 
movements may be expected, regular surveys of the s tructure are recommended. 
 
For general non-destructive testing of a bridge str ucture, the classical load test with deflection mea surements and 
detailed visual inspection before and after testing  provides a wealth of reliable information.  The ne ed for the load 
testing may occur when there are indications that t he structure may have become unserviceable of that there may 
have become unserviceable or that there may be an i nadequate margin of safety.  The conventional appro ach, using 
dial gauges, may be suitable where a fixed referenc e point is available, but may often require expensi ve and 
cumbersome scaffolding.  An apparatus employing lin ear variable differential transformers (LVDTs) and an 
electronic recording system has been developed and used successfully to monitor the short-term deflect ions of both 
bridge decks and abutments.  
 
An alternative to full-scale load testing of bridge s to assess their working capacity is the technique  of dynamic load 
testing.  While this method may have its advantages , much research is needed to confirm its validity. 
 
For the measurement of strain, forces and pressure a wide variety of instruments and gauges is availab le, ranging 
from strain gauges and extensometers to load cells and hydraulic pressure devices. 
 
--- Page 71 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  66 5.2.4 ASSESSMENT OF UNDERWATER CONCRETE 
 
Visual examination of underwater components may be augmented by photographic, video and closed circuit  TV 
equipment.  However, such examination is limited by  the turbidity of the water and, under conditions o f zero-
visibility, or dark water, divers must touch and fe el in order to detect flaws, damage and deteriorati on.  Although 
artificial lighting can be helpful in some situatio ns, it is of no use in turbid waters which reflect light.  These 
limitations may be offset by various non-destructiv e tests and other techniques described below. 
--- Page 72 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  67 5.2.4.1 Surface defects 
 
Surface defects below the waterline are best determ ined by visual examination using measuring tapes, c allipers, 
feeler gauge and so on.  Surfaces should be cleaned  for detailed inspections.  Optical gauges are not suited to 
underwater observations and reliance must be placed  on tactile examination in conditions of zero-visib ility. 
 
5.2.4.2 Reinforcement cover 
 
A “Covermeter” may be used to measure concrete cove r provided that the concrete surface has been thoro ughly 
cleaned.  Accuracy is improved if the bar size is k nown. 
 
5.2.4.3 Concrete strength 
 
(i) Hardness tests 
 
See 5.2.1.3(i) for rebound-hammer test procedures. 
 
(ii) Vibration methods 
 
Ultrasonic methods, such as those in 5.2.1.3(ii) ca n be used to assess the homogeneity of concrete 
and to locate cracks, laminations, voids and lack o f bond.  The technique is sensitive and fast but 
requires a skilful operator.  Surfaces should be th oroughly cleaned and reasonably smooth since 
roughness can affect the results.  Complex shapes a re difficult to assess. 
 
(iii) Acoustic holography 
 
Acoustic holography provides information similar to  that obtained from ultrasonic methods.  The 
resulting three-dimensional view permits precise lo cation and measurement of internal defects.  
Surfaces must be thoroughly cleaned.   
 
(iv) Radiography  
 
This technique was discussed in 5.2.1.3(iii).  Unde rwater surfaces should be thoroughly cleaned 
and the water between the source and the subject mu st be displaced.  Access to both sides of the 
component is necessary and the method is unsuitable  for complex shapes. 
 
(v) Core drilling 
 
Core drilling procedures were described in 5.2.1.3( iv).  Unfortunately, commercial drilling 
equipment is not readily suited to underwater opera tion and modifications are necessary.  These 
involve replacement of electric-power with pneumati c or hydraulic power; provision for bolting 
the base plates to the structure; and the incorpora tion of a pressure-regulated ram to provide the 
thrust to the bit. 
 
 
(vi) Computerized topography (CT) 
 
Once the procedure is perfected for underwater use,  this method should be able to detect voids and 
steel reinforcement.  It uses a gamma ray source to  form a flat fan of rays that are attenuated as 
they pass through the component to a set of detecto rs.  The apparatus is rotated to produce a series 
of projections through the same cross section.  
 
5.2.5 ASSESSMENT OF UNDERWATER STEEL 
 
The comments at the beginning of 5.2.4 apply equall y well here. 
--- Page 73 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  68  
5.2.5.1 Surface defects 
 
Surface defects involving corrosion, loss of sectio n, cracks, damage and distortion can best be detect ed using the 
procedures outlined in 5.2.4.1. 
 
5.2.5.2 Material strength 
 
Although samples are rarely taken from the underwat er components of bridge substructures, they can be obtained by 
torch cutting or coring (see 5.2.4.3). 
 
5.2.5.3 Evaluation of cracks 
 
The detection of cracks in steel components near or  below the waterline may be accomplished using one or more of 
the following techniques. 
 
(i) Magnetic field disturbance techniques 
 
These methods were described in 5.2.2.3(ii).  Thoro ugh cleaning of steel surfaces is necessary.  
Use of the magnetic particle technique within the s plash zone may prove difficult. 
 
(ii) Vibration methods, acoustic holography and rad iography 
 
Application of these three methods to the examinati on of underwater concrete was discussed in 
5.2.4.3.  They may be used for the examination of s teel elements below the waterline. 
 
5.2.6 ASSESSMENT OF UNDERWATER TIMBER  
 
The basic approach adopted for the examination of t imber substructures located below the waterline is similar to that 
described for concrete in 5.2.4. 
 
Many of the techniques given in 5.2.4 and 5.2.5 can  be used to assess timber.  However, the most commo n method 
for testing wood involves boring into the surface w ith drills or removing samples by means of core dri lling.  Ease of 
penetration may indicate low density or voids cause d by fungus, bacterial, or borer action.  The holes  should be filled 
with wooden plugs upon completion of the examinatio n. 
Test boards are sometimes used to detect borer atta ck.  These consist of wooden blocks which are immer sed in the 
water and periodically retrieved for laboratory stu dy and borer identification.  
 
5.2.7 ASSESSMENT OF DEPTH OF SCOUR 
 
All practical methods, including the latest detecti on equipment should be used to assess the extent an d depth of 
scour.  A variety of possible approaches to submerg ed foundations is currently available. 
 
5.2.7.1 Visual surface assessment 
 
The general pattern of scour may indicate its cause  and the most critical locations to check.  The ext ent of scour, rip-
rap and other protective measures should be sketche d and photographed to determine if changes have occ urred 
between inspections.  Soundings should be taken to measure the depth of scour.  However, these observa tions may 
not indicate the maximum depth of scour because the  holes tend to refill with sediment as the flood re cedes. 
 
--- Page 74 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  69 5.2.7.2 Visual underwater assessment 
 
Divers should be used if undermining or undercuttin g of pier, abutments and other works is suspected b ecause these 
cannot be adequately detected from the channel surf ace. 
 
Where possible, samples of stone should be recovere d from the stream bed stone in order to establish i f rip-rap or 
other deposits are present.  Again, divers cannot d etermine the maximum depth of scour because the hol es tend to 
refill with sediment. 
 
5.2.7.3 Echo sounders 
 
Echo sounders (specifically fathometers) are partia lly effective in checking for scour.  However, fath ometers are 
ineffective when used very close to the structure, because substructural components or debris accumula tions can lead 
to erroneous readings.  
 
5.2.7.4 Determination of maximum depth of scour 
 
As stated previously, scour holes tend to refill wi th sediment as the flood recedes.  The following te chniques, not in 
order of importance, are used to establish the maxi mum depth of scour: 
• boring 
• penetrometers 
• test pit excavations 
• sonar 
• ground radar 
• lead line sounding during floods 
• installed meter systems 
 
--- Page 75 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  70 (i) Boring  
 
Boring is only feasible if the bed consists of fine  material, such as gravel, sand, silt and clay.  
However, the drilling process may disturb the soil sufficiently to obscure differences between the 
original stream bed material and fresh sediment.  L arge stones are sometimes cored in order to 
examine material underlying rip-rap.  One or two bo rings are usually required to calibrate sonar 
and ground radar profiles. 
 
(ii) Penetrometers 
 
This method, which consists of driving solid rods i nto the stream bed, is only feasible if the scour 
holes have been refilled with loose, fine-grained s ediment.  Moreover, it is difficult to distinguish 
between original and new material if they are of si milar densities.  The technique is insensitive and 
is considered to be inferior to sonar and ground ra dar. 
 
(iii) Test pit excavations 
 
Besides being difficult, time-consuming and costly,  these excavations may not reveal the depth of 
scour in deep alluvial river bed strata unless mark ers have previously been placed in backfill 
layers.  Because, the backfilled test pits may form  potential weak spots during subsequent floods, 
they are not recommended for normal bridge inspecti on.   
 
(iv) Sonar 
 
Sonar for soils cannot penetrate a cover of concret e cobbles or rip-rap and requires at least 0,6 to 
1,6 m of water over the stream bed.  Apart from the se limitations, it can produce continuous 
profiles or soil strata below the stream bed in whi ch filled scour holes show up as changes in soil 
density.  The frequency of the “pings” lies in the 3 kHz to 14 kHz range and can be varied to 
develop the best possible profile at each site.  Bo reholes are sometimes required for calibrating the 
soil profiles. 
 
(v) Ground radar 
 
Use of this technique to disclose filled scour hole s is still being investigated.  The equipment 
consists of a portable transceiver which emits a si gnal of about 120 MHz.  This signal can 
penetrate fresh water and soil to depths of about 2 5 m.  Strata are detected by soil conductivity 
differences.  Unfortunately, the signal cannot pene trate salt water, conductive clays and mats of 
steel reinforcement.  Some boreholes may be require d to assist in the interpretation of results. 
 
(vi) Lead line sounding during floods 
 
The use of weighted line can give direct measuremen ts of scour holes near their maximum depth.  
However, because bounding points are generally limi ted to positions more or less directly below 
the observer, the method is restricted to waterway cross sections located beneath the parapets.  
Moreover, the number of bridges that can be checked  in this way, and the number of locations that 
can be measured at each bridge are limited by the f requency and duration of the floods. 
(vii) Installed meter systems 
 
These systems, which are installed during construct ion, can only check scour at preselected points.  
Various sophisticated devices, such as resistance-g auges, tiltmeters and crack indicators, have been 
tried but are generally considered to be too delica te for construction and use conditions.  
Alternative systems are comparatively simple.  One consists of a column of distinctive bricks or 
plastic balls which are laid in sand-filled shafts.   After a flood, excavation reveals how far down 
the markers have been disturbed.  Another possibili ty is the layering of pyrite, fools gold, and 
back-fill.  Besides its distinctive appearance, its  presence, or absence would show up well on radar. 
 
5.3 DIAGNOSIS 
 
--- Page 76 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  71 5.3.1 CAUSES OF DEFECTS AND DETERIORATION IN BRIDGE  STRUCTURES 
 
Before carrying out any remedial work it is importa nt to identify the basic causes that have made the repair 
necessary.  Otherwise an inappropriate and conseque ntly ineffective repair technique may be selected.  This chapter 
provides a brief introduction to assist inspectors and those responsible for bridge maintenance in ide ntifying the 
likely causes of defects or deterioration.  It deal s both with defects apparent shortly after construc tion and also with 
deterioration that occurs after many years of use. 
 
In some cases, for example damage due to impact or fire, the cause is obvious; in other cases the caus e or causes may 
only be established by means of a detailed programm e of testing and examination. 
 
The reasons for deterioration are either physical o r chemical.  The types of damage and their possible  cause are 
outlined below. 
 
5.3.2 CONCRETE STRUCTURES 
 
In most cases, deterioration of a concrete structur e can be attributable to one or more of the followi ng causes: 
 
5.3.2.1 Spalling 
 
Depending on the cause, the amount of concrete invo lved may vary from very small particles to sizeable  pieces.  If 
not already detached from the main body of concrete , the affected zone can usually be removed with lit tle effort.  
Possible causes are: 
 
(i) Accidental mechanical damage 
 
This type of damage should be obvious and is usuall y attributable to vehicle and marine vessel 
collision.  It can take a variety of forms, but the  most common is damage to the spans of 
overbridges by high vehicles.  Incorrect handling a nd rough treatment during construction can 
damage precast components.  Damage to bridge member s by debris may also be apparent after 
severe flooding. 
(ii) Corrosion of steel reinforcement 
 
The products of steel corrosion occupy considerably  more volume than the original steel and the 
resulting expansive forces cause spalling of the ov erlying concrete.  Characteristic of this type of 
damage is spalling or cracking which follows the li ne of the reinforcement.  This is particularly 
noticeable at the corners of beams and piers, altho ugh a pattern of links and stirrups can often be 
seen.  Reinforcement corrosion may be difficult to detect in the early stages, although rust stains on  
the surface of the concrete may indicate a problem.   Rust stains alone, however, may not always 
indicate corrosion of the reinforcement.  In milder  cases, the rusting may be due to tying wire.  It 
may be that surface stains occurred during construc tion, but each manifestation should be carefully 
inspected.  The causes of reinforcement corrosion a re discussed in the section dealing with 
cracking (5.3.2.3). 
 
(iii) Freezing or cracked or porous concrete 
 
This type of damage is usually limited to members w hich are subject to freezing while saturated 
with water.  Freezing of the moisture in the concre te causes expansive forces which lead to 
spalling.  The cause of the cracking should be esta blished, and consideration should be given to the 
repair of porous concrete in such cases. 
 
(iv) Poor quality concrete 
 
A result of poor compaction or curing or inadequate  mix design. 
 
(v) Chemical attack 
 
--- Page 77 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  72 In such cases, spalling will normally be accompanie d by cracking of the concrete.  Refer to the 
section on cracking (5.3.2.3). 
 
(vi) Thermal shock 
 
Although not normally a problem with bridge structu res, it is possible that bridge members may be 
subjected to high temperatures due to a vehicle acc ident on or below the bridge.  In such cases, the 
evidence of fire should be obvious. 
 
(vii) Insufficient or choked joints 
 
Where one concrete member abuts or bears on another , the restraint of movement due to 
inadequate or choked joints may cause concrete spal ling. 
 
(viii) Inadequate bearing area 
 
Spalling may occur where the bearing area is too cl ose to the edge or end of a concrete member. 
 
--- Page 78 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  73 5.3.2.2 Scaling and abrasion 
 
Scaling is the gradual and continuous loss of surfa ce mortar and aggregate over irregular areas.  It i s most common 
on horizontal surfaces exposed to the weather and t o traffic, but it can occur elsewhere and is often an indication of 
frost damage.  Abrasive damage in aqueous environme nts is discussed in Section 5.3.4.1. 
 
5.3.2.3 Cracking 
 
Cracks in concrete do not always jeopardize the saf ety or bearing capacity of a structure.  The possib le effects of 
cracks must be considered in the context of cause, location, statical system and environment and utili zation of the 
structure.  Cracks may be the cause or effect of a fault or both. 
 
The design and structural analysis of a reinforced concrete structure are based on the hypothesis that  the concrete 
will crack in the tension zone before the reinforce ment bars can take up the tension.  Cracks in this part of the 
member will therefore not directly affect the immed iate loadbearing capacity of the member even if the  cracks are of 
considerable width.  However, cracks in the concret e cover will permit the entry of corrosion-accelera ting agents to 
the steel.  Consideration should therefore be given  to the possible need to seal by injection those cr acks that are 
wider than acceptable.  What is considered an accep table crack width is, however, a highly subjective matter.  For 
the layman, it is often the aesthetic aspects of cr acks that are unacceptable.  The professional’s gre atest concern is the 
decrease in the durability, water tightness and str ength of cracked concrete.  Maximum permissible cra ck widths 
specified in design codes are provided as an aid to  the designer in selecting and placing reinforcemen t but bear little 
resemblance to the actual size and distribution of cracks that will appear in the final structure.  Li ke most physical 
phenomena, crack size is subject to the normal laws  of distribution. 
 
Although most cracks will normally be smaller than allowable, some cracks will be larger.  The effect of concrete 
cracking upon durability is not cut and dried, and guidelines are therefore difficult to establish.  T he classification 
given in Table 5.1 is suggested.

Possible causes that may lead to cracking and that may be of major significance in bridge structures a re: 
 
(i) Accidental mechanical damage 
 
This type of damage can be attributed to a variety of causes and can take a number of forms.  The 
most common is probably damage to the external beam s of overhead bridges by high vehicles.  
Collision damage due to impact of marine vessels ca n sometimes be sufficiently extensive to reach 
the foundations and cause cracking and fracturing o f piles.  Incorrect or rough handling during 
construction can damage precast elements.  Overdriv ing can crack and even buckle precast piles.  
Cracks can occasionally be caused by pile driving a nd blasting.  Damage to bridge members by 
debris may also be apparent after severe flooding. 
--- Page 79 ---
