(ii) Corrosion of reinforcement 
 
Concrete normally provides a protective environment  to embedded reinforcing steel because the 
highly alkaline hydration products ensure passivati on of the steel surface due to the formation of a 
protective layer of iron oxide.  The passivity of t he protective film can be destroyed by the 
reduction of the pH of the pore solution in the cem ent paste by leaching, carbonation, sulphation 
and by high rates of oxygen diffusion in the presen ce of enough salt to cause depassivation.  If the 
optimum levels of oxygen and water are present, ele ctrochemical “corrosion cells” are formed and 
the steel is corroded.

--- Page 81 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  76 The resulting corrosion products usually consist of  a mixture of black and reddish brown oxides of 
iron. 
 
The former, known as black rust or magnetite (Fe 3 o4), is produced where the oxygen supply is 
restricted whilst the latter, known as red rust or haematite (Fe 2O3), is the higher oxidation state.  
Magnetite is denser than the red rust and dos not p roduce the expansive forces which can fracture 
concrete. 
 
An insufficiency of cover to the reinforcement is t he most general cause of corrosion in concrete.  
Cracking, often accompanied by spalling, occurs in a direction parallel to the main reinforcement, 
particularly at the corners of beams and piers, alt hough a pattern of links and stirrups can often als o 
be seen.  There are usually rust stains on vertical  faces and soffits.  The conditions of carbonation,  
sulphation, and salt penetration that can lead to d epassivation, and hence corrosion of the steel, are  
outlined in the section on chemical attack. 
 
(iii) Chemical attack 
 
In countries where de-icing salts are used, and in maritime environments, the interrelationship 
between cracking and reinforcing steel corrosion is  one of the major factors causing the 
deterioration of concrete.  In Southern Africa, the  highest incidence of corrosion is found in coastal  
regions and aggressive industrial environments.  Ch emical attack may consist of one or more of the 
following: 
 
a) Carbonation 
 
Carbonation is a natural process whereby atmospheri c carbon dioxide diffuses into concrete, 
dissolves in the pore water to form carbonic acid w hich in turn reacts strongly with hydration 
products.  If this process proceeds so that the neu tralized (or carbonated) layer extends down 
to the depth of the steel reinforcement then the pa ssivating layer is broken down and 
corrosion of the steel can take place.  Well design ed, good quality, properly compacted and 
cured concrete with adequate cover to the reinforce ment will not normally carbonate to the 
extent that reinforcement corrosion can occur durin g its design life.  Unfortunately, concrete 
construction does not always meet this high standar d and some concretes may not be capable 
of proving corrosion protection to embedded steel o ver the lifetime of the structure.  The 
single most important factor affecting the rate and  extent of carbonation is the permeability of 
the concrete.  Humidity is also a significant facto r.  Carbonation occurs most rapidly in the 
range 50 to 75 % relative humidity.  For a structur e situated in a non-coastal environment and 
greater than twenty years old, carbonation could be  a likely cause of deterioration, although 
this is by no means a certainty.  Simple on-site te sts are available.  
--- Page 82 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  77 (b) Sulphation 
 
A process similar to carbonation occurs with sulphu r dioxide in the atmosphere.  However, 
the concentration of sulphur dioxide is between 3 0 00 to 7 000 times smaller than carbon 
dioxide and the effects of sulphation are usually n egligible.  Only in very polluted 
atmospheres will sulphation be a problem.  Structur es such as the reinforced concrete colling 
towers of coal-burning power stations and sewage pi pes are particularly prone to sulphation.  
Sulphates are also found in sea water, sulphate-bea ring soils and suphate-bearing ground 
water, but they can also be present in the coarse o r fine aggregate of the concrete itself.  
Concretes made with ordinary Portland cement have a  certain resistance to sulphate attack, 
but, for higher concentrations of sulphate, a riche r mix, sulphate-resisting cement, or possibly 
a pozzolanic cement would normally be recommended f or new construction.  The reactions 
between sulphates and the hydrated cement compounds  double the solid volume of the 
original reactants and the subsequent expansion lea ds to disruption and cracking of the 
concrete.  Positive identification of sulphate atta ck is made difficult by the fact that concrete 
damaged by sulphate expansion can resemble concrete  damaged by either alkali-aggregate 
reaction or drying shrinkage.  However, drying shri nkage would normally be accompanied by 
evident signs of contraction.  Unrestrained concret e shows a typical map-pattern type of 
cracking.  Under conditions of restraint, or loadin g, the predominant cracks form parallel to 
the direction of the major principal stress.  Sulph ate attack can be positively diagnosed only 
by examination in the laboratory. 
 
(c) Salt penetration 
 
The mechanism by which chloride ions destroy the pa ssivity of steel is not clearly understood.  
It is thought that the chloride ion promotes corros ion of reinforcing steel in concrete through 
the conversion of insoluble iron oxide to soluble f errous chloride.  Also, chloride salts 
increase the conductivity of the ground path, enhan cing the corrosion current.  Chloride ions 
also introduce a source of variation in galvanic al ong the steel, thus forming concentration 
cells.  In the USA and Britain, the continued use o f de-icing salts on bridge decks has led to 
the wholesale deterioration of bridge structures th rough corrosion of the reinforcement. 
 
Fortunately, this is not a problem in Southern Afri ca, but in the coastal regions, particularly 
Natal, salt-laden moisture is responsible for the d eterioration of many concrete structures.  
Although chlorides are most likely to be the cause of reinforcement corrosion in coastal areas, 
the previous use of calcium chloride to accelerate the early strength gain of concrete, by no 
means confines it to these regions.  Damage due to chloride is characterized by deep pitting of 
the reinforcement bar, the result of intense locali zed attack.  Testing usually involves the 
extraction of core samples and laboratory testing. 
--- Page 83 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  78 d) Alkali-aggregate reaction 
 
Two types of alkali-aggregate reaction are known.  Alkali-carbonate rock reaction is the 
reaction between alkalis and argillaceous dolomitic  limestones or argillaceous calcitic 
dolostones.  Alkali-silica reaction, involves a rea ction between the alkalis in the cement and 
certain forms of silica such as opal, chert, flint,  chalcedony, microcrystalline and 
cryptocrystalline quartz and volcanic glasses, in t he aggregate. 
 
When the water soluble alkalis in the concrete reac t with the reactive forms of silica present 
in the fine or coarse aggregates, an alkali silicat e gel is formed.  This gel can form around and 
within the aggregate particles and can also migrate  into the surrounding paste matrix.  As the 
gel absorbs water by osmosis and swells, it causes the concrete to expand.  When the osmotic 
pressure within the gel exceeds the tensile strengt h of the concrete, cracking occurs.  
Furthermore, when the gel loses water it shrinks, s o that differential expansion occurs 
between the external faces which are exposed to reg ular cycles of wetting and drying, and the 
expanded internal core.  This results in map or pat tern cracking of the external surface of the 
concrete.  The cause of cracking in structures shou ld be evaluated carefully as concrete 
damaged by drying shrinkage or sulphate attack can be similar in appearance to that caused 
by AAR. 
 
Alkali aggregate reaction may cause closure of expa nsion joints, spalling or offsetting and 
warping of structural members and pavements.  Concr ete which is unrestrained in all 
directions develops map or pattern cracking on expa nsion.  The direction of both 
reinforcement and imposed loads influence the orien tation of cracks in the concrete.  Cracks 
are usually aligned parallel to the direction of th e major principal stress or reinforcement.  
Streaks of gel, similar to the occurrence of calciu m carbonate efflorescence, may sometimes 
be seen on vertical surfaces.  Grey discolouration of concrete surfaces, due to impregnation 
of the surface by gel, is often observed along edge s of cracks.  Damp patches on the surfaces 
are sometimes seen.  The presence of AAR can be con firmed by the macroscopic or 
microscopic examination of flat polished surfaces c ut from concrete cores.  The expansion 
mechanism, which is illustrated in Figure 5.1 may b e seen in medium to large particles of 
reactive aggregate.  Reactivity within the reaction  rim or expansion shell results in one or 
more of the following diagnostic features: 
 
• a darkened reaction rim impregnated with viscous ge l 
 
• peripheral cracks separating the expansion shell fr om the unaffected interior 
 
• one or more transverse tension cracks across the co re 
 
• radial tension cracks in the surrounding matrix whi ch generally connect with similar 
cracks form other reactive particles 
 
• gels which migrate into the fracture system and voi ds in the cement paste.  The 
abundance of these gels depends on several factors and their presence or absence is 
not indicative of the severity of the reaction.  Th eir composition and appearance can 
also vary from soft and viscous through white and w axy to hard and porcelaineous. 
 
--- Page 84 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  79 
 
 
--- Page 85 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  80 Three conditions must be satisfied for AAR to occur : 
 
• Sufficient alkali must be present in the concrete 
• The presence of a reactive constituent in the aggre gate is necessary 
• Appropriate moisture conditions must exist. 
 
With the absence of any one of the above, the react ion will not occur.  For example, if 
concrete undergoing AAR is allowed to dry out, the reaction will stop.  Furthermore, once 
the reactive silica or alkali has been depleted by the reaction, it will also stop.  However, 
even if the reaction has stopped, the continued exi stence of the gel, with its hygroscopic 
nature, will still cause expansion and shrinkage to  occur in the concrete on absorption and 
loss of moisture respectively.  With the resulting deleterious effects.  The gel does, 
however, crystallize in time, producing a fairly in ert white reaction product, but by this 
stage, the damage has inevitably been done. 
 
e) Restrained thermal contraction and expansion 
 
Concrete, in common with other materials, contracts  and expands with variations of 
temperature and when the movement is restrained, da mage can result.  Temperature 
variations can occur as a result of externally appl ied heat or cold, or due to the quite 
considerable quantities of heat generated by concre te during setting and hardening.  If 
thermal expansion of a bridge deck is restrained by  a choked or incorrectly set expansion 
joint, the large local compressive stresses which a re set up can result in spalling.  
Temperature variations between opposite faces of a bridge member result in differential 
internal stresses which not only cause cracking, bu t which may also be responsible for the 
opening and closing of existing cracks along with v ariations in ambient temperature.  
Damage caused by the heat of hydration released dur ing setting of concrete is characterized 
by surface cracking, or in extreme cases cracking a cross the whole section. 
 
f) Restrained shrinkage 
 
As concrete initially sets and hardens, it shrinks slightly.  Subsequently, additional 
shrinkage occurs as it dries out.  If this shrinkag e is restrained, cracking may result.  
Restrained initial shrinkage resembles in many ways  restrained thermal contraction, and the 
situations in which damage occurs and the type of d amage are similar.  Usually such 
damage is a combination of the two effects.  Cracks  due to restrained shrinkage are often 
noticed soon after construction, but where slow dry ing is a major factor they may not be 
apparent till much later.  Restraint against shrink age of the concrete in a structure is 
provided by the embedded steel, by the foundation a nd by adjacent structural elements.  
Also he interior concrete, because of differential shrinkage, can impose a restraint on 
surface shrinkage, causing surface cracks which do not penetrate deeply onto the concrete.  
Rapid surface shrinkage is the primary cause of sur face crazing, although carbonation of the 
surface may enhance the tendency to crack during dr ying and wetting cycles.  Concrete 
cracking due to drying shrinkage can resemble that due to either alkali-aggregate reaction or 
sulphate attack. 
 
g) Structural cracking 
 
Cracks resulting from loading can take a variety of  forms depending on the stress regime.  
Cracking could be a result of bending; tension; she ar; torsion; bond failure; concentrated 
--- Page 86 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  81 load; or any combination of these.  Substructure an d superstructure elements are dealt with 
separately. 
 
h) Cracking of substructure elements 
 
Cracks in abutments often occur as a result of disc repancies between design assumptions 
and actual behaviour.  Vertical cracks in wing wall s, often initiated at the top edge, are a 
common occurrence (Figure 5.2).  In this case the a butment and wing walls may be acting 
compositely although this was not the intention.  V ertical cracks in breast walls similar to 
those shown in Figure 5.2 are not uncommon and are usually a result of one or more of the 
following:  non-uniform shrinkage; a discrepancy be tween design assumptions and actual 
behaviour; and the earth pressure on the wing walls .  The relative effects of these 
phenomena are uncertain, but all of them act to cre ate tension in the front face of the breast 
wall.  Where vertical cracking extends throughout t he breast wall section, the cause is more 
likely to be differential settlement (Figure 5.3).  Separation of the breast and wing walls is a 
possible further indication of differential settlem ent. 
 
Horizontal cracks other than at construction joints , on the other hand, may be more serious.  
If a breast wall designed to act as a cantilever ex periences propping action from the deck, 
then unforeseen tensile strains may develop in the front face under the earth pressure from 
the backfill (Figure 5.4).  This fault generally oc curs when the superstructure is constructed 
before placing fill material behind the abutment.  If the designer has not allowed for elastic 
deflection of the abutment stem, or, in some cases,  rotation of the foundation slab, the 
expansion joint and bearings will close up. 
--- Page 87 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  82 
 
--- Page 88 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  83 i) Cracking of superstructure elements 
 
The more commonly encountered types of cracking tha t occur in bridge superstructures are 
shown in Figure 5.5.  They may be categorized as fo llows: 
 
• Separating cracks:   cracking of the whole concrete area of a member u nder axial 
tension.  This occurs infrequently as there are nor mally other forces present in most 
practical instances. 
 
• Bending or flexural cracks:   cracks usually begin at the tensile face and exte nd 
vertically, or near vertically, to near the neutral  axis of the section (Figure 5.6).  This 
type of cracking is normally a result of insufficie nt design strength or overloading.  In 
prestressed or post-tensioned beams this may be an indication of a loss of tension in 
the cables.  Vertical cracking in a prestressed or post-tensioned beam over an 
intermediate support is usually caused by inadequat e anchorage, a common design 
inadequacy (Figure 5.7).  At end supports, excessiv e splitting forces at cable 
anchorages, usually the result of a design error, a re evidenced by horizontal cracks 
(Figure 5.6). 
 
• Forking cracks:  strongly reinforced deep members under an applied m oment may 
develop closely spaced bending cracks which tend to  join together to form larger web 
cracks.  This type of cracking can also develop in thick walls under tension if there is a 
large amount of surface reinforcement. 
 
• Secondary or bond cracks :  short cracks between main flexural cracks are of ten an 
indication that the bond between the reinforcement and the concrete has broken down. 
 
• Shear or torsion cracks:   shear cracks are found in the webs of members sub ject to 
large shear forces (Figure 5.6).  Excessive crackin g suggests insufficient shear 
reinforcement.  The cracks are normally at an incli nation of between 25 and 50 
degrees to the axis of the beam and frequently star t at the tensile face as flexural 
cracks.  Twisting causes similar cracks but they ar e found on all faces of the member 
(in spiral formation). 
 
5.3.3 STEEL STRUCTURES  
 
Deterioration of steel members can normally be attr ibutable to one or more of the following causes. 
 
5.3.3.1 Cracking and fracture 
 
The types of cracking that may occur in metal compo nents may be categorized as follows: 
 
 
--- Page 89 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  84 
 
--- Page 90 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  85 
 
--- Page 91 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  86 (i) Fatigue fracture 
 
The surface appearance of fractured material may va ry from smooth to crystalline.  This type of 
cracking is normally caused by cyclic loading that has reached the fatigue life of the structure or 
structural member. 
 
(ii) Brittle fracture 
 
Brittle fracture can appear as cracks on the surfac e of welds.  In the parent metal fracture often 
occurs along cleavage planes and is evidenced by cr acks that are induced at stress raisers such as 
holes and sheared or flame-cut edges that are not p roperly machined or ground.  Brittle fracture 
normally occurs at low temperatures combined with t ensile stresses and is characterized by a lack 
of plastic deformation. 
 
(iii) Tearing of metal 
 
Tears in a metal may be caused by overloading, inco rrect size or grade of fasteners, inadequately 
fastened bolts, flame-cut or poorly drilled holes, and hole drifting during erection. 
 
(iv) Laminations 
 
Laminations are generally critical only for members  or parts of members in compression.  They 
show up as hairline cracks or gaps on the edges of members and are usually aggravated by 
corrosion. 
 
(v) Stress corrosion cracks 
 
The presence of tensile stresses is a prerequisite for the occurrence of this type of crack.  The 
tensile force tends to expose more metal at the gra in boundaries, permitting corrosive substances to 
penetrate between the grains.  As the corrosion con tinues, cracks develop along grain boundaries 
and eventually through the centre of the grains. 
 
5.3.3.2 Excessive deformation and buckling 
 
Deformation and buckling often occur as a result of  local yielding near points of support or concentra ted loads 
caused by high shears or inadequate bearing length.   Deformation of members may also result from inade quate or 
missing lateral bracing or from a collision.  Overl oading can cause buckling of compression members. 
 
5.3.3.3 Corrosion 
 
Most structural steel components tend to rust unles s adequately protected.  Exceptions include certain  high strength 
low allow “weathering steels”, such as Cor-Ten, whi ch use moisture and certain airborne contaminants t o form a 
hard protective oxide coating or patina.  However, under the wrong conditions even these steels will c orrode. 
--- Page 92 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  87 (i) Structural steels 
 
The corrosion process, whereby metallic iron is con verted into rust compounds through chemical 
or electrochemical processes, is outlined in Sectio n 5.3.2.3.  This rust flakes off easily from the 
parent metal and reduces the cross-sectional area o f the component.  Corrosion can be classified as 
atmospheric, underwater, soil, chemical and electro lytic (caused by electric currents). 
 
Atmospheric corrosion occurs in the parts of steel members located above the splash zone.  Here, 
there is an abundance of oxygen and the predominant  corrosion factor is moisture. 
Within the splash zone, corrosion is accelerated by  salt water and the effects of wet/dry cycles. 
 
In the case of submerged portions of steel members the predominant factor is the concentration of 
dissolved oxygen.  The higher the velocity or turbu lence of the water, the higher the corrosion rate 
because more oxygen is brought into contact with th e metal.  Below the mud line, where portions 
of members are buried in the soil, the predominant causes of corrosion are electrical conductivity, 
acidity, water content and dissimilar metals.  Stra y electrical currents may cause severe corrosion 
through electrolysis. 
 
Chemical corrosion is caused by chemicals in pollut ed waters.  Corrosion is readily identified by 
its typical reddish-brown, rust-coloured appearance , its pitted oxidised surface and loose oxide 
flakes.  Although limited growths of barnacles in t he sea increase pitting, heavy growth tends to 
prevent corrosion.  Consequently, the unnecessary r emoval of marine growth from steel 
components should be avoided. 
 
(ii) Weathering steels 
 
These steels perform satisfactorily in environments  with strong wet/dry cycles, where there is 
sufficient rainwater to wash the surface and dilute  contaminants, and also enough air to promote 
drying.   
 
However, they will corrode if used incorrectly in c onditions involving excesses of either water of 
airborne contaminants which lead to rusting and fla king.  Harmful contaminants include corrosive 
industrial fumes, salt laden spray and de-icing sal ts.  Excesses of water can arise from: 
 
• Poor design details which retain water on horizonta l surfaces 
 
• High annual precipitation – Cor Ten is best suited to climates with an annual rainfall of about 
750 mm 
 
• Constant submergence in soil or water 
 
• Condensation in damp climates or localities 
 
• Vegetation which inhibits air circulation. 
 
The use of weathering steels should therefore be av oided along coastlines exposed to salt spray.  
Moreover, it is preferable to provide additional pr otection, such as paint or galvanising, in the 
vicinity of bolted connections, leaky expansion joi nts and down spouts. 
 
5.3.4 TIMBER STRUCTURES 
 
Where used, timber structures contain a large numbe r of components, such as piles and cross-members, w hich 
require considerable time for inspection.  The life  of timber structures depends on the environment.  Deterioration 
can result from fungal attack, which causes rotting , and marine borers, which consume the wood.  The p rinciple 
marine borers are the Limnoria and the Teredo. 
 
--- Page 93 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  88 Generally speaking, all untreated wood is susceptib le to attack by marine borers and even the heart-wo od of some 
special species, such as teak, is suspect.  Althoug h creosote is the most widely used wood preservativ e, recent 
developments favour the use of dual treatments cont aining toxic additives; one harmful to Limnoria and  the other to 
Teredo.  However, the use of dual treatment in fend er systems is questionable because the wood may bec ome 
embrittled. 
 
5.3.4.1 Abrasion 
 
Abrasive damage is caused by waves, currents and th e action of suspended particles of sand and silt in  moving 
waters.  Deterioration results in worn, smooth surf aces and general depressions, or even necking, of t he affected 
area. 
 
5.3.4.2 Accidental damage 
 
Timber can be damaged before installation by unsuit able preservation methods and incorrect or rough ha ndling.  
Over-driving piles during construction causes split s, checks and shakes which can be identified visual ly.  Buckling 
may also occur. 
 
Incorrect preservative treatment and hardware place ment can leave piles unprotected against marine bor ers.  
Similarly, surface gouges and tears due to incorrec t handling also invite borer attack.  Although thes e defects are 
visually identifiable, marine growth may obscure bo lt-holes that permit borer penetration. 
 
5.3.4.3 Collision damage 
 
Collision damage to timber components usually resul ts from impact by marine vessels and, to a lesser e xtent, large 
pieces of debris.  Besides the immediate surroundin gs, collision impact can damage adjacent or even re mote 
structural components.  The extent of damage depend s on the nature of the structural frame and its con nections.  The 
degree of damage can range from superficial to comp lete destruction of all or part of the structural s ystem.  
Examples include: 
 
• severe abrasion or splintering of timber piles 
• lateral displacements of piles varying from slight to complete fracture below the mud line. 
5.3.4.4 Limnoria 
 
The Limnoria is a small, lobster-like crustacean wh ich bores into the exposed outer shell of wood.  Th e initial attack 
by small colonies is generally small, not limited t o specific points and, apart from small tunnels and  the absence of 
fouling organisms on external surfaces, is difficul t to detect in the early stages.  However, once ent renched, the 
population increases, mechanical erosion of the tun nels occurs and the timber section is rapidly reduc ed.  This makes 
both visual and tactile detection easier.  Figure 5 .8(a) shows that these “surface” borers are most ac tive in the 
intertidal zone. 
 
5.3.4.5 Teredo 
 
The marine borer, known as Teredo, or shipworm, is a mollusc whose larvae begins tunnelling on the ext ernal 
surfaces but rapidly grows and moves into the inter ior of the wood as shown in Figure 5.8(b).  It is a lso referred to as 
an “internal” borer and is difficult to detect beca use only the tails, or siphons, remain at the surfa ce.  Stealth and 
familiarity with the physical appearance of these s iphons is required if the borers are to be seen pro truding from the 
surface.  Attack, which can occur anywhere below th e tidal zone, may produce tunnels as large as 12 mm  in diameter 
and 1 m long. 
 
Combined attack by both borers results in the surfa ce borers exploring the calcareous-lined tubes of t he internal 
borers; making visual identification possible.  Bot h non-destructive and destructive tests are used to  determine the 
presence of Teredo. 
--- Page 94 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  89  
 
 
 
5.3.5 PROTECTIVE COATINGS 
 
Coatings are expendable materials used to protect a nd decorate substrates which are costly to replace.   All coatings 
will eventually fail, but rapid deterioration is un desirable and can be prevented by careful selection  of the correct 
coating system and the observance of the sound pain ting practice. 
 
The paint film on a metal component should never be  allowed to deteriorate to the stage where apprecia ble rusting 
occurs underneath it.  Should this happen, extensiv e cleaning down to the bare metal will be necessary .  In all 
maintenance painting the aim should be to repaint b efore the old paint has deteriorated to such an ext ent that it has to 
be removed completely.  This is done by periodicall y applying another coat of paint over the cleaned, old paint.  In 
general, this will be less costly than removing the  old coating and repainting with a complete coating  system. 
 
Coatings break down in a multitude of ways related to the nature of the coating system, condition of e xposure and 
the nature of the substrate.  Deterioration can usu ally be attributable to one or more of the followin g causes. 
 
5.3.5.1 Loss of gloss 
 
On paints exposed to the weather, the first sign of  deterioration is a loss of gloss.  Gloss can be at tributed to an 
excess of binder in a paint film, forming a smooth,  glass-like film on the surface.  Weathering causes  the breakdown 
of the organic material forming this film, resultin g in a loss of gloss. 
 
5.3.5.2 Chalking 
 
Chalking usually follows loss of gloss in a paint f ilm.  It is caused by the formation of loose powder  at or just below 
the surface as a result of decomposition of the coa ting through the effects of the weather.  Certain p igments and 
--- Page 95 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  90 binders cause more chalking than others and chalkin g may be controlled by their correct choice.  Only excessive 
chalking may be considered to be a failure.  In som e instances, chalking is a desirable property as it  lends itself to 
self-cleaning and reduces the likelihood of checkin g occurring – by reducing the thickness of the pain t coating (see 
5.3.5.4).  Surfaces that exhibit chalking are thoug ht to be the best type of surface for repainting. 
 
5.3.5.3 Discolouration 
 
Most paints change colour on exposure to the weathe r.  This is usually as a result of either a chemica l change in the 
pigment or excessive chalking.  For example, rapid colour changes have been observed where hydrogen su lphide in 
the atmosphere has reacted with lead pigments in a paint.  Changes in colour may also be where hot, hu mid 
conditions prevail.  Fungal growth causing black sp lotches on a paint film may also lead to colour cha nge. 
--- Page 96 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  91 5.3.5.4 Checking 
 
Checking is a slight V-shaped break in the coating which does not penetrate to the substrate.  It is c aused by relieving 
of mechanical stress in the paint film.  This pheno menon may be the first sign of breakdown, but often  checking will 
remain constant over long periods of film life.  Th e result is merely a loss of decorative effect.  Sh ould paint not 
check as a result of a lack of high tensile strengt h, it may crack with the expansion and contraction of the surface to 
which it is applied, and flaking may result? 
 
5.3.5.5 Cracking 
 
Cracking is that phenomenon in coatings that become s visible as extensive breaks through the paint fil m.  It is a very 
serious type of failure and necessitates complete r emoval of the failed coating, before repainting can  commence.  
Cracking is caused by embrittlement of the paint fi lm through oxidation, polymerization or by loss of plasticizer 
during service. 
 
5.3.5.6 Alligatoring 
 
Alligatoring consists of breaks that are wide and e xtensive but do not penetrate to the substrate.  Al ligatoring may 
begin as checking or cracking but instead of the br eak remaining narrow at the bottom, it grows wider through 
contraction of the topcoat.  In extreme cases wrink ling may occur in the “islands” of topcoat. 
 
5.3.5.7 Flaking, peeling and delamination 
 
Flaking and peeling are caused by loss of adhesion to the substrate.  When the loss of adhesion is bet ween coatings, 
the defect is delamination or undercoat adhesion fa ilure.  Flaking is the loosening of small brittle p ieces (less than 10 
mm diameter), whilst peeling is the extreme form, f or example, whole sheets of paint peeling from the underlying 
surface.  Usually the latter is associated with mor e flexible coatings.  Failures of this type may be attributed to the 
application of paints to incorrectly cleaned, prime d, over cured or glossy surfaces. 
 
5.3.5.8 Blistering 
 
Blistering is the appearance of pockets under the p aint film containing gas, solid or liquid material.   It is caused by 
gases or liquids, in or underneath the coating, whi ch exert pressure stronger than the adhesion or coh esion of the 
paint area under stress.  Solvents entrapped in the  coating may cause blistering on increase in temper ature, if the rate 
of diffusion of the solvent through the paint film is too slow.  Another common cause of blistering is  through the 
absorption of hydrogen in the surface of steel duri ng pickling to remove millscale.   
 
5.3.5.9 Wrinkling 
 
Wrinkling is a defect that occurs on the surface of  a dried paint film.  It has the appearance of fine  corrugations in 
certain areas of the surface.  Invariably it is cau sed by the application of too thick a layer of pain t.  The surface dries 
and expands more rapidly than the body of the paint , causing wrinkles.  Wrinkling is more common with oil-based 
paints and may be accentuated by the application of  heat during drying.  This defect usually has littl e effect on the 
durability of the paint system although the appeara nce of the coating may be affected. 
5.3.5.10 Corrosion 
 
One of the most important functions of a coating sy stem is to protect substrate metals from the corros ive effects of 
the environment.  To be effective the coating shoul d act as a barrier between the substrate and the su rrounding 
media, it should be continuous initially and should  remain continuous throughout its service life.  Co rrosion is 
observed as local or general corrosion and is cause d by damage to the coating or where the coating was  applied too 
--- Page 97 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  92 thinly.  This is a very serious type of failure and  the only remedy is to remove all of the old coatin gs and as much of 
the corrosion products and other contaminants as po ssible before repainting commences. 
 
5.3.6 TRAFFIC LOADING 
 
Traffic loads, specifically increased traffic volum es and illegally overloaded vehicles, can contribut e significantly to 
bridge wear.  This usually occurs on older bridges located on secondary roads where lower design loads  were used 
and manifests itself in the form of stress cracks i n components of the superstructure.  It may also co ntribute to stress 
cracking in concrete piles or splintering in timber  piles. 
 
 
5.3.7 DEFERRED MAINTENANCE (NEGLECT) 
 
Deferred maintenance or neglect can aggravate most forms of distress.  This is particularly true of un derwater 
components where failure to expedite repairs may le ad to partial or complete collapse.  To this end: 
 
• cracks, spalls and voids in concrete should be repa ired 
• scour and the undermining of foundations should be minimised 
• timber piles should be protected from marine borers  
• accumulations of debris should be removed 
 
5.4 SCOUR AND TRAINING WORKS 
 
It has been claimed that more bridges collapse beca use of scour than any other single cause.  Reasons contributing to 
these failures include the relatively tranquil cond itions that generally exist when such crossings are  designed and 
built.  These can be deceptive in that they provide  little indication of the tremendous forces that ca n be unleashed by 
heavy and persistent rain storms which occur very i nfrequently and extend over vast areas of the catch ment.  The 
situation is often aggravated by changes in the riv er’s regime due to natural and man-made causes, suc h as earth 
slippage and property development.  These can upset  predictions regarding peak discharge, maximum floo d levels, 
flow paths and the erodibility of stream beds.  Mor eover, general guidance on the selection and layout  of training 
works has not always been readily available and unl ess based on model studies, their design was, to a large extent, an 
art.  For these reasons, the waterway should be ins pected regularly after construction in order to ver ify behaviour and 
rectify the original design where necessary.   
 
Scour lets loose erosive forces which lower the cha nnel bed below its natural level or other appropria te datum.  This 
tends to expose or undermine foundations that would  otherwise remain buried.  The depth of material re moved 
below the stated datum is known as the depth of sco ur while the depth of water above a scoured bed, un der stated 
flow conditions is called the soured depth.  Figure  5.9 illustrates the meaning of these and other ter ms. 
 
5.4.1 CATEGORIES OF BRIDGE SCOUR 
 
The scour at bridges may be divided into the four f ollowing categories, which do not necessarily appea r in order to 
relative importance: 
 
• General scour across bridge waterway openings assoc iated with the restricted flow of flood waters, 
See Figure 5.9. 
 
• Local scour around piers, abutments, the noses of g uide bank and groynes and various other 
locations; which results from vortex systems induce d by obstructions to the flow.  See Figure 5.10. 
 
--- Page 98 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  93 • Natural scour in alluvial and tidal channels associ ated with variations in flow conditions.  This may 
involve the transportation of bed-material, migrati on of the bed-form and channel shifting.  See 
Figures 5.11 and 5.12. 
 
• Progressive channel degradation associated with geo logical processes or man-made regime changes, 
either upstream or downstream.  See Figure 5.13. 
 
Occurrences of scour may involve combinations of th ese categories as well as other, less common effect s.  
Aggradation is discussed in Section 5.5. 
 
5.4.2 FACTORS AFFECTING SCOUR 
 
The depth and extent of scour at any given time may  be affected by any or all of the following factors  which do not 
appear in order of relative importance: 
 
• slope, natural alignment and shifting of the channe l 
• type and amount of bed-material in transit 
• history of previous floods 
• accumulation of debris 
• construction and/or realignment of flow due to brid ge works 
• layout and geometry of training works 
• geometry and alignment of piers 
• composition of the bed and underlying materials 
• placement or loss of rip-rap and other protective m easures 
• natural or man-made changes to flow or sedimentatio n regimes 
• accidents, such as the collapse of a near-by struct ure 
 
--- Page 99 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  94 
 
 
--- Page 100 ---

--- Page 101 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  96 5.4.3 EROSION AND SEDIMENTATION  
 
Water flowing in natural or artificial channels oft en has the ability to scour sand, gravel and even l arge boulders from 
the bed or banks.  The resulting sediment is swept downstream and subsequently deposited in the lower reaches of 
the channel.  The sediment generally consists of a bed load, in which the larger particles roll along the bed, and a 
suspended load, in which the finer material is held  in suspension by turbulence. 
 
5.4.3.1 The process of erosion 
 
Figure 5.14 (a) shows the variation with depth of v elocity, V, and shear, τ in an open channel in which the bed 
material is at rest.  In this case, water can perco late through the pores of the underlying material a nd the velocity 
does not reduce to zero at the bottom. 
 
As the velocity is increased, the fluctuating lift and drag forces of the water move particles at the boundaries of the 
stream and start the erosion process.  This may occ ur even when the surface flow is fairly steady.  Ve locity and shear 
gradients at an advanced stage of sediment transpor tation are illustrated in Figure 5.14 (b).   
 
The erosion process can be influenced by macro turb ulence in the form of eddies, helicoidal flow, roll ers and surges.  
Figure 5.16 illustrates the complicated flow patter n and consequent scouring action at a bridge pier.  Both this and 
the complex relationship between local and average velocities lead to difficulties in predicting the e rosive power of 
rivers. 
5.4.3.2 Resistance to erosion 
 
(i) Non-cohesive materials 
 
The resistance to erosion of cohesionless materials  depends primarily on grain size, size 
distribution and particle density; grain shape, ori entation and packing arrangement being less 
important.  Although not necessarily justified, it is generally assumed that a mixture of sand and 
stone can be represented by a single size, such as the median, D50, size by weight.  Resistance to 
erosion increases with the median diameter of the b ed-material, lower flow rates and shallower 
depths. 
 
(ii) Cohesive materials 
 
Apart from the fact that a breakdown of the bond be tween particles must occur before erosion can 
begin, little is known about the erosion resistance  of cohesive materials. 
 
(iii) Bedrock formations 
 
Weak sandstones and poorly cemented sands and grave ls will erode if the cementing medium is 
dissolved.  Laminated materials, such as hard shale s may tend to peel off during major floods. 
--- Page 102 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  97 (iv) Rates of scour 
 
Rates of scour in different materials and situation s vary considerably.  The rate depends not only 
on the relationship between the erosive power and t he resistance to erosion, but on the balance 
between material eroded and material deposited.  It  may also be affected by variations in the 
relative resistance of successive stratified layers .  Under conditions of steady flow, most scour 
situations eventually reach equilibrium.  However, when the flow is unsteady, the scour 
topography will develop over a period or series of events. 
 
5.4.4 LOCAL SCOUR AT PIERS 
 
Generally speaking, piers located in waterway openi ngs are a cause of local scour; a situation which i s aggravated by 
significant restrictions to the flow and accumulati ons of debris (see Plate 5.1).  During large floods , scour can 
penetrate to considerable depths.  The depth of sco ur usually depends on: 
 
• the pier width and length 
• its shape and alignment 
• the footing details 
• the velocities and depth of flow 
• the type and size of bed-material 
• the rate of bed transport 
• accumulations of debris 
 
5.4.4.1 Pier shape 
 
The influence of pier shape on scour in cohesionles s soils can be seen in Figure 5.15.  This and simil ar types of 
information are normally used for design purposes b ut have been included here in order to illustrate t he relative 
influence of various factors. 
 
These scour depths may increase by as much as 50 pe r cent if the depth of flow exceeds five times the minimum 
horizontal dimension, W, of the pier.  Debris accum ulations can drastically increase these allowances.  
 
5.4.4.2 Pier skew 
 
The alignment of piers elongated in a direction ske w to that of the natural flow can significantly alt er the scour 
pattern.  Figure 5.16 shows that, with increasing s kew, the point of maximum scour tends to move downs tream, away 
from the pier nose, on the exposed side of the pier .  It may even occur downstream of the pier tail if  the skew is 
particularly severe.  An indication of the relative  increases in the depth of scour associated with pi er length and 
alignment may be obtained from Figure 5.17.  Becaus e skew can cause deep scour, any observed changes i n river 
alignment and flow direction relative to the piers should always be included in bridge inspection repo rts.

The information in Figures 5.15 to 5.17 basically a pplies to piers with footings located below the dep th of scour.  
However, if the footing or enlargement is exposed t o scour, as in Figure 7.18(b), then its effect is s imilar to that of a 
large pier for which the width, w, used to evaluate  the scour factors, should reflect the dimensions o f the footing. 
 
The conical transition shown in Figure 5.18© is som etimes effective in minimising local scour where th e general 
scour bed level is uncertain. 
 
On the other hand, Figures 5.18(d) and (e) demonstr ate how footings, caissons and other enlargements t end to reduce 
scour provided that the projection lies below the g eneral scour bed level and is of sufficient horizon tal extent to 
cover the area of local vortex action.  In order to  prevent scour these projections should be greater or equal to the 
minimum horizontal dimension of the pier.  Figure 5 .18(f) shows an example of extreme scour leading to  pile 
exposure. 
 
5.4.4.4 Accumulations of debris 
 
Debris accumulations restrict the waterway area and  raise the potential for general scour in the chann els between the 
piers.  See Figure 5.19.  Because, such accumulatio ns increase the effective width of piers and hence the depth of 
scour, debris should always be removed after floods .  Debris is less likely to accumulate on piers wit h semi-circular 
cutwaters that are vertical or slightly raked in pr ofile.  See Figure 5.15. 
 
5.4.5 PREVENTION OF LOCAL SCOUR AT PIERS 
 
Beside the measures given in 5.4.4.3 four other tec hniques are used to cater for scour at piers.  Thes e are also 
illustrated in Figure 5.20 viz: 
 
• rip-rap apron or backfill 
• sheet piling 
• pile bents designed to withstand exposure to scour 
• spread footing founded below the worst local scour level. 
 
The first methods are also used to protect piled fo undations. 
 
5.4.5.1 Protective rip-rap aprons 
 
Local scour is frequently reduced or eliminated at piers through the use of rip-rap either as backfill  to the foundation 
excavations or in the form of an apron similar to t hat in Figure 5.20(b). 
 
The surface of these aprons should be located below  the anticipated general scour level.  They should extend beyond 
the face of the pier for a distance equal to at lea st 1,5 times, but preferably twice the minimum hori zontal pier 
dimension.  The thickness of the apron should be fa irly uniform and not less than twice the D 50  size of the stone. 
 
 
--- Page 107 ---

--- Page 109 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  104 The stones should have been placed in at least two overlapping layers in order to prevent massive fail ure in the event 
of a slight loss of material.  They should also hav e a reasonable size distribution and be properly in terlocked so as to 
present an even surface texture.  There should be n o protrusions of isolated large stones that provoke  severe eddies 
leading to failure. 
 
Incorrectly laid rip-rap not only affords little sc our protection, but can even lead to scour at adjoi ning piers or in the 
downstream channel.  See Figure 5.21. 
 
5.4.6 LOCAL SCOUR AT ABUTMENTS 
 
The wide variation in abutment configurations and a pproach flow conditions thwart attempts to provide general 
guidance on scour at abutments.  However, it can be  said that scour will probably occur at the upstrea m corners of 
abutment footings that protrude into the stream flo w.  This is illustrated in Figure 5.22.  Preventati ve measures are 
generally based on the concepts given for piers. 
 
5.4.7 TRAINING WORKS 
 
Various types of training works or bank protection are used at most bridge sites to control the direct ion of flow and 
protect earth fills, piers and abutments from flood  damages.  Their importance has increased during re cent years 
because modern road alignment standards require gre ater use of such works to make unfavourable sites u sable. 
 
5.4.7.1 Purpose of training works  
 
 
The specific function of bank protection and traini ng works in relation to bridges and their approach embankments 
are: 
 
• to stabilise river banks and channel locations in t he case of shifting streams 
 
• to economise on bridge lengths by constricting the waterway opening 
 
• to direct flow parallel to piers so as to reduce lo cal scour 
 
• to improve the hydraulic efficiency of waterway ope nings thereby reducing afflux and scour and 
facilitating the passage of debris 
 
• to protect the bridge approach embankments from str eam attack and prevent meanders from folding 
onto the approaches 
 
• to permit the construction of square bridge crossin gs by diverting the channel flow from a skewed 
alignment 
 
• to secure existing works, or to repair damage and r ectify initial design problems. 
 
Some of these functions are illustrated in Figure 5 .23. 
--- Page 110 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  105 
 
 
 
 
 
--- Page 111 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  106 5.4.7.2 Types of training works 
 
The principle types of bank protection and training  works associated with bridges are listed below: 
 
• Bank and slope revetment:   rip-rap, stone pitching, paving or other type of covering placed on 
stream banks, embankments or slopes to prevent eros ion.  See Figure 5.24(a). 
 
• Guide banks or spur dikes:  embankments, such as th at in Figure 5.24(b), which are  constructed 
more or less parallel to the stream in order to dir ect the flow smoothly through openings.  See also 
Figure 5.23(a) and (b). 
 
• Spurs or groynes:   embankments or walls constructed more or less nor mal to a stream bank or shore 
line in order to redirect the flow.  See Figure 5.2 4(b). 
 
• Dikes:   embankments or walls constructed to prevent flood ing of lands adjacent to rivers and 
bridges. 
 
• Channel diversion:   artificial cuts made in order to improve flow ali gnment through the bridge 
opening, or to facilitate the layout and constructi on of other types of training works. 
 
5.4.7.3 Guide banks or spur dikes 
 
Guide banks are used for a variety of purposes: 
 
• to restrict the flow to a single channel 
• to improve the flow distribution across the waterwa y 
• to control the angle of attack on piers 
• to stop meandering 
• to prevent erosion of approach road embankments. 
 
(i) Length 
 
Opinions differ on the recommended length of guide banks.  However, in unstable, shifting rivers, 
these banks will probably extend upstream of the br idge centre line by approximately 75 per cent of 
the width of the waterway opening, and downstream b y 25 per cent of this width.  See Figure 
5.23(b).  These dimensions may have to be modified in the light of natural features and observed 
channel behaviour.  For example, the channel bend i n Figure 5.25(c) requires a long straight bank 
and a short curved bank combination.   
 
If the stream has a tendency to meander, the upstre am portion should direct the flow straight through 
to the bridge and prevent the formation of a loop b ehind the bank which could breach the approach 
road embankment.  See Figure 5.23(a). 
 
--- Page 112 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  107 
 
 
--- Page 113 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  108 (ii) Shape in plan 
 
Guide banks are generally curved in plan with a bel l-mouth entry to the waterway opening.  This is 
illustrated in Figure 5.24(b).  Although straight p arallel guide banks are used in certain situations,  
they tend to cause bar formation alongside one bank  which in turn diverts the flow to the opposite 
side. 
 
(iii) Cross-section and height 
 
A typical guide bank cross-section is shown in Figu re 5.25(a).  The angle of slope is usually selected  
to suit the subsoil conditions, the embankment mate rial, and the type of slope protection provided:  
The steeper the slope, the smaller the area requiri ng protection, but the heavier the actual revetment .  
The ends of the guide banks should be rounded as sh own in Figure 5.25(b).  Protection against 
undermining is described in 5.4.8. 
 
The tops of guide banks are generally wide enough t o accommodate construction and maintenance 
vehicles and are normally located above the design high-water level, with an appropriate freeboard 
allowance.  In this regard, the level of the dead-w ater pond in Figure 5.23(a) will be higher than tha t 
at the actual bridge opening.  The difference in le vel depends on the normal fall along the channel 
and the velocity head in the contracted waterway op ening. 
 
Lower guide banks that can be overtopped by high fl oods are sometimes used if the backwater 
induced by a higher bank is unacceptable.  However,  in such cases, the top should be protected 
against erosion. 
 
5.4.7.4 Spurs or groynes 
 
Spurs or groynes may be used singly or in groups to : 
 
• prevent erosion of the approach road embankments, d ikes or natural river banks, or 
• in lieu of guide banks to direct the flow into a co nstricted waterway opening. 
 
Spurs may consist of embankments, similar in cross- section and height to guide banks.  Alternatively, they may be 
built of timber, steel or concrete walls or even ta ke the form of permeable structures, such as a doub le row of piles 
filled with cut trees. 
 
Spurs or groynes are normally used in groups since,  when used singly, they cause severe flow disturban ces which 
result in scour at the ends.  However, there are ex ceptions. 
 
(i) Orientation 
 
Spurs in the form of earth embankments are generall y pointed upstream so as to create a dead-water 
pond which acts as a “cushion” against erosion of t he upstream face.  See Figure 5.24(c).  In such 
cases, only the nose of the spur requires protectio n.  If pointed downstream, it may be necessary to 
protect the full length of the upstream faces again st erosion.   
 
(ii) Spacing and length  
 
Figure 5.24(c) shows that the extent of embankment protection on either side of the spur is roughly 
equal to twice the project length of the spur norma l to the flow.  For this reason, spurs are seldom 
more closely spaced than twice their projected leng th and may be even further apart when several are 
present.  The nature of resulting disturbances on t he adjacent river usually dictates the choice 
between a few long spurs and several short spurs.  Alternatively, continuous slope protection of the 
bank may be both better and cheaper. 
 
5.4.7.5 Dikes 
 
--- Page 114 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  109 Dikes are normally constructed on a flood-plain to:  
 
• prevent flood waters from bypassing the bridge open ing 
• prevent undesirable backwater effects and possible damage to adjacent property 
• control drastic shifts in alluvial fans. 
 
Dikes should be impermeable, high enough to prevent  overtopping and resistant to erosion.  They are us ually wide 
enough to accommodate construction and maintenance vehicles.   
 
During construction care is normally taken to minim ise disturbance of the natural vegetation and to av oid the 
development of adjacent ditches which might subsequ ently alter to erosion channels.   
 
5.4.8 PROTECTION AGAINST UNDERMINING 
 
Undermining, which is frequent cause of revetment f ailure, may be prevented by the four methods shown in Figure 
5.26. 
 
a) The revetment is continued down to non-erodible material or to a point below the anticipated scour 
level.  Although this method is the most permanent,  it may be impractical and uneconomical if deep 
scour is anticipated. 
 
b) A flexible launching apron is placed at the foot  of the revetment.  This settles at a natural slope  and 
covers one side of scour holes.  Whereas this metho d is recommended for cohesionless channel beds 
where deep scour is expected, it does not perform w ell on cohesive channel beds where scour takes 
the form of slumps with steep slip planes. 
 
Launching aprons are constructed from a variety of materials including rip-rap, articulated concrete 
matting, concrete blocks, gabions and mattresses of  wire mesh filled with stone.  Stone rip-rap is 
most commonly used.  The aprons should extend below  the limits of the worst possible scour 
conditions. 
 
 
 
--- Page 115 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  110 
 
 
--- Page 116 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  111 c) Use of a cut-off wall or sheet piling from the t oe of the revetment down to a non-erodible material  or 
to a point below the anticipated scour level.  Such  walls tend to cause deeper scour than paved 
slopes and should be tied back to prevent failure d ue to embankment pressure if the channel material 
is eroded. 
 
d) Paving across the entire bed of the waterway ope ning.  This is only economical for small streams.  
Scour tends to occur at the downstream edge of the paved area unless this is tied into a natural non-
erodible formation or an artificial stilling basin is provided.  The size of paving blocks or stones 
should be consistent with the water velocity throug h the opening. 
 
5.4.9 SCOUR DEPTHS ALONGSIDE BANKS AND TRAINING WOR KS 
 
Besides local scour at the piers, Figure 5.10 shows  five other locations along the banks which are sus ceptible to 
scour: 
 
• alongside channel banks or guide banks which are pa rallel to the flow 
• at guide bank nosing where parallel stream flow is intermixed with strong spiral currents 
• around the noses of spurs where the main flow is mo re or less normal to the end of the spur 
• around the ends of approach road embankments that a re unprotected by guide banks 
• under a bank, dike or guide bank on which the flow impinges directly. 
 
There is little documentary information regarding s cour depths at the above locations and it is recomm ended that, 
wherever possible, such depths be estimated from ob servations at similar locations in the river.  Tabl e 5.3 gives 
some approximate relative scour depths. 
 
TABLE 5.3:  RELATIVE SCOUR DEPTHS AT BANKS AND TRAI NING WORKS 
 
LOCATION AND DIRECTION OF FLOW POINT RELATIVE SCOUR  DEPTH  
Flow parallel to bank 2 1,5 to 2,0 
Flow impinging normal to bank 6 2,25 
Nose of spur or guide bank 3 and 5 2,0 to 2,75 
 
 
5.5 RIVER FLOW AND ASSOCIATED TOPICS 
 
5.5.1 THE NATURAL RIVER 
 
Left to their own devices most alluvial rivers will  develop irregular courses which offer high resista nce to floods and 
raise flood levels.  This contrasts with the requir ements of bridge builders who, in order to effect e conomical 
crossings, often find it necessary to confide the r iver to some extent and also stabilise portions of the adjacent 
channel.  Satisfactory control of scour, aggradatio n and so on requires some appreciation of the river ’s natural 
inclinations. 
--- Page 117 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  112 5.5.1.1 Flow distribution in straight channels 
 
A typical relationship between flow velocity and de pth may be seen in Figure 5.27(a).  This stream has  a reasonable 
uniform depth, y, and a rough bed and it can be see n that the average flow velocity, V m, occurs at a depth of 0,4  y 
above the bed.  Just below the surface the velocity  attains a maximum equal to some 10 to 20 per cent more than the 
mean value.  However, along the bottom, friction an d strong turbulence combine to produce a steep velo city gradient 
and an ill-defined flow rate of between 0,3 V m and 0,7 V m. 
 
Friction along the sides also influences the flow v elocity and results in the type of flow distributio n defined by the 
constant velocity contours in Figure 5.27(b).  The tendency of floating objects to move away from the bank towards 
the middle of a stream is caused by secondary trans verse currents.  These currents, which are shown in  Figure 
5.27(c), have a threefold effect: 
 
• they cause the main flow to spiral 
• they raise the midstream level fractionally relativ e to the sides 
• they depress the point of maximum velocity at midst ream. 
 
Whereas twin cells are normally present in most str aight channels, multiple cells of alternating rotat ion may occur in 
wide rivers. 
 
5.5.1.2 Braiding 
 
Multi-cell secondary flow probably accounts for the  strong tendency of straight channels to form centr al bars which 
split the flow into separate channels.  These chann els may divide and merge endlessly and develop the braided 
appearance.  Braiding commonly occurs in the steep upper reaches of rivers, where the transporting ene rgy is high. 
 
5.5.1.3 The thalweg 
 
In straight channels, the line joining points of ma ximum depth tends to swing from side to side.  This  line, or 
thalweg, is shown in Figure 5.28(a).  At the crosso ver points, such as A-A, the mean depth is less and  the flow 
velocity greater than at the loops and gives rise t o the pool and riffle effects shown in Figure 5.28( b).  Because of 
this, apparently straight channels conceal meander patterns in which the thalweg traces a sinuous path  between 
straight banks. 
 
5.5.1.4 Meanders 
 
At some stage, instability, arising either from cha nges in strata or braiding or the thalweg, will cau se an alluvial river 
to scour along one of its banks and develop a singl e meander loop.  With time this solitary loop grows  into a 
complete meander system similar to those in Figure 5.28(c).  The secondary transverse flow at a meande r loop only 
contains the single cell shown in Figure 5.29(b).  This scours the outer banks of the loops and deposi ts material on 
the inner banks causing the meanders to wander and migrate in the manner shown in Figure 5.29(d). 
 
Although the relatively gentle flow associated with  meanders permits easy bank stabilization, they are  often cut-off 
by training works in order to streamline the flow a nd reduce flood levels.  Training works, such as th ose shown in 
Figure 5.29(e), normally follow a sinuous course wh ich preserves and fits in with the natural meander pattern.  
Straight alignments are deliberately avoided becaus e they tend to revert to a meandered form. 
 
5.5.1.5 The flood-plain 
 
Normal river flow will eventually produce a reasona ble stable channel within a flood-plain.  When this  channel is 
overtopped by floods, the flood-plain on either sid e of the main channel carries the additional flow a s shown in 
Figure 5.30.  The relatively shallow waters in this  overbank flow move at a lower velocity than those in the main 
channel. 
--- Page 118 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  113  
Degradation or aggradation of the bed will depend o n the magnitude of the flood and flow velocity.  If  the overbank 
flow is sufficiently slow to permit the deposition of silt, aggradation will gradually raise the level  of the flood-plain.  
On the other hand, heavy floods can degrade the flo od-plain and even the whole waterway; sometimes exp osing 
underlying bedrock formations. 
 
5.5.2 OPEN CHANNEL FLOW 
 
The water flowing in an open channel contains speci fic energy.  This energy has two components:  the p otential 
energy of the depth of flow and the kinetic energy of the velocity of flow.  These three respective fo rms of energy are 
related by Bernoulli’s equation for open channel fl ow: 
 
E = y + V 2/2g (5.1) 
 
Where  E = specific energy 
y = depth of flow 
V = velocity of flow 
g = gravitational constant. 
 
Figure 5.31(a) shows the relationship between depth  and specific energy for a constant discharge.  Thi s curve is 
asymptotic to the horizontal axis and a line at 45º  to this axis.  The 45º line is a plot of the poten tial energy and the 
difference between it and the specific energy curve  represents the kinetic energy. 
 
 
--- Page 119 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  114 
 
 
--- Page 120 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  115 
 
--- Page 121 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  116 
 
--- Page 122 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  117 
 
 
 
--- Page 123 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  118 5.5.2.1 Critical flow 
 
Under normal flow conditions a river seeks a state of equilibrium in which the specific energy is a mi nimum.  The 
depth and rate of flow at this stage are called the  critical depth and critical velocity respectively.   The critical depth, 
yc, and corresponding velocity, Vc shown in Figure 5.31(a), have the following values: 
 
yc = (q 2/g) 1/3 (5.2) 
 
and 
 
Vc = √g y c (5.3) 
 
where 
 
q = rate of discharge or volume rate of flow per un it time. 
 
In 5.5.6.3 it is shown that this critical velocity is equal to that of the solitary wave given by equa tion 8.8. 
 
5.5.2.2 Supercritical and subcritical flow 
 
Reference to Figure 5.31(a) shows that two low cond itions may exist when the specific energy exceeds t he 
minimum critical value: 
 
• Supercritical or rapid flow, V > √g y c;  high velocity in shallow water 
• Subcritical or tranquil flow, V < √g y c; low velocity in deep water. 
 
Examples of supercritical and subcritical flow may be seen in Figure 5.32.  The rapid flow conditions 
immediately downstream of the sluice gate and in th e tail-race of the spillway can cause severe scour.   In 
both cases subsequent transformation to the tranqui l flow condition is preceded by a hydraulic jump. 
 
Supercritical flow is not common in natural rivers,  but occurs in some mountain streams and artificial  
channels. 
 
 
 
5.5.2.3 The hydraulic jump 
 
Streams in a state of supercritical flow contain an  excess of energy which they seek to reduce by chan ging to a 
subcritical condition.  Such reductions occur at hy draulic jumps in which energy is dissipated through  turbulence 
at the resulting wave front. 
--- Page 124 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  119 The supercritical and subcritical flow depths in Fi gure 5.31 are related as follows. 
 
y2  = y 1 ((1 + 8F 12)½  - 1)/2 (5.4(a)) 
 
y1 = y 2 ((1 + 8F 22) ½  – 1)/2 (5.4(b)) 
 
Where Froude’s number, F is the ratio of the veloci ty of the stream to that of a gravity wave, or F = V/ √ 
gy. 
 
The energy on either side of the jump may be determ ined from equation 5.1: 
 
E1 = y 1 + V 12/2g 
 
and 
 
E2 = y 2 + V 22/2g 
 
The energy loss, E 1 – E2 is shown in Figure 5.31(b). 
 
Figure 5.33 shows two wave front extremes.  The fir st, known as a shock type surge, is associated with  a 
relatively large height differential and loss of en ergy.  These can occur as tidal bores or surges in estuaries 
which shallow and narrow rapidly and cause the inco ming water to advance in a steep wave front.  The 
other extreme involves smaller jumps where the back  roll is replaced by undular flow in which there is  
less energy loss. 
 
Near conditions of critical flow, alterations in de pth may correspond to very small changes in specifi c 
energy.  Because several depths may exist for pract ically the same specific energy, flow in the vicini ty of 
the critical depth may appear to have an unstable w avy surface.  
 
5.5.2.4 Occurrences of hydraulic jumps 
 
Examples of hydraulic jumps abound.  Besides the pr evious two which are associated with man-made 
obstructions, others, resulting from various natura l causes, are given in Figure 5.34.  Whereas the ju mps 
resulting from the presence of submerged rock outcr ops and abrupt changes in bed slope are self-eviden t, 
the two conditions in Figure 5.34(c), which are ass ociated with changes in channel elevation, depend o n 
the relationship between the depth of flow and the critical depth.  Hydraulic jumps or standing waves can 
also occur in the vicinity of channel contractions,  or chokes, similar to that in Figure 5.34(d). 
 
Less obvious are jumps inside culverts which are ei ther long or have a high tail-water level.  Two 
examples appear in Figure 5.35. 
 
Figure 5.36 shows the constricting effects of a pai r of bridge piers.  The piers tend to choke the flo w and 
under certain conditions cause a jump similar to th at in Figure 5.36(b).  Such constrictions raise the  
upstream water level and are one of the causes of b ackwater curves.  These are discussed below.
