UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  123 5.5.3 BACKWATER CURVES 
 
An example of a backwater curve appears in Figure 5 .37(a).  The height by which the natural water leve l 
is raised at any point is called the afflux. 
 
The parameters controlling the profile of backwater  curves are related by Bernoulli’s theorem for open  
channel flow.  For the short reach or length of cha nnel in Figure 5.37(b), this may be written in the 
following form: 
 
ye1  + V 2
1/2g = y e2  + V 2
2/2g + y f (5.5) 
 
where 
 
ye1  = height of upstream water level above datum 
ye2  = height of downstream water level above datum 
yf = head loss due to friction 
V1 = upstream flow velocity 
V2 = downstream flow velocity 
g = gravitational constant 
 
The downstream water level y e2 , and flow velocity, V 2, adjacent to the obstruction control 
the parameters in the series of reaches shown in Fi gure 5.37(a).  The afflux may extend 
upstream for several kilometres and gradually dimin ishes until it eventually disappears.  
The actual calculation of backwater curves, using c harts or computer programs, is beyond 
the scope of the document. 
 
The importance of the backwater effects depends on the economic significance of the river level.  Beca use the 
flooding of productive land may result in large cla ims for damages, flood protection measures, such as  dikes, should 
be adequate ad maintained in sound condition. 
 
5.5.3.1 Various backwater effects 
 
a) Increase in backwater elevation due to sedimenta tion 
 
If the dam shown in Figure 5.37(a) is constructed o n a silt-laden river, the reduced flow velocity in 
the backwater may increase the rate of sedimentatio n.  Figure 5.37(c) shows how this might 
gradually raise the elevation of the backwater curv e.  This can cause overtopping of bridges by 
floods and even their burial within the sediment. 
 
b) Flooding 
 
The approach embankments of the bridge in Figure 5. 38(a) intrude into the natural waterway and, 
together with the piers, constrict the flow.  Becau se this river is located within an incised channel 
without substantial overspill, the afflux occurring  during floods, although not large, may be 
significant if the areas along the upstream banks a re utilized for any purpose. 
 
--- Page 129 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  124 
 
 
 
--- Page 130 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  125 
 
--- Page 131 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  126 c) Ponding 
 
The river shown in Figure 5.38(b) is fairly steep a nd has a wide flood-plain.  The long approach 
embankment located downstream of the skew bridge ma y cause ponding in the vicinity of point A.  
Such ponds may be large and, if considered undesira ble, are prevented by the provision of a dike 
or, alternatively, a relief culvert.  
 
d) Restricted flood-plain 
 
Figure 5.38(c) shows a river with a wide floodplain .  The long bridge approach embankments 
prevent overbank flow and can lead to a substantial  afflux.  Besides causing upstream flooding, this 
can lead to general scour within the bridge opening . 
 
5.5.3.2 Effects of scour and other factors on backw ater 
 
a) Scour 
 
General scour in the waterway opening reduces the a fflux that might otherwise result from the 
constrictions described in (b) and (d) above.  Wate rways are sometimes artificially enlarged by 
excavation to reduce afflux. 
 
b) Guide banks 
 
Guide banks similar to those shown in Figure 5.38(d ) streamline the flow and tend to reduce 
backwater effects.  However, this may not be true i f the river bed is steep. 
 
c) Submerged superstructures 
 
In unusual circumstances, flood water may reach the  deck soffit and cause the bridge to act as a 
culvert.  Unless the leading edges of the soffits a re rounded to streamline the flow, the afflux will 
rise rapidly once the opening is closed.  This coul d suddenly hasten the spread of flood waters. 
 
5.5.4 TIDAL CROSSINGS 
 
At the coast, changes in water level result from as tronomical tides and storm surges such as cyclones.   Resonant 
oscillations, known as seiches may occur in harbour s and inlets whilst seismic waves are comparatively  rare. 
 
Some of the principal tidal terms are illustrated i n Figure 5.39. 
 
--- Page 132 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  127 
 
--- Page 133 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  128 Whereas tidal fluctuations can generally be predict ed from published tables, the other effects involve  special 
observation.  For example, the tidal range in estua ries and inlets is often greater than along the adj acent coast, owing 
to resonance and funnelling effects.  Mathematical and hydraulic models are used to analyse the behavi our of some 
of the more important cases. 
 
Because tidal waters are often highly valued for th eir biological productivity and recreational benefi ts, the ecological 
and environmental effects of new bridges and associ ated works are normally evaluated during the design  stage.  
Nevertheless, their importance requires that be kep t under constant surveillance. 
 
5.5.4.1 Types of tidal crossings 
 
Road crossings of tidal waters generally fall withi n one of the following three categories. 
 
• Inlets connecting the open sea with an enclosed lag oon or bay, where virtually all the discharge 
results from tidal movements.  See Figure 5.40(a). 
 
• River estuaries where both river flow and tidal flo w contribute to the net discharge.  See Figure 
5.40(b). 
 
• Crossings between islands, or between an island and  the mainland where currents can flow in either 
direction.  See Figure 5.40(c). 
 
5.5.4.2 Hydraulic peculiarities of tidal crossings 
 
Tidal crossings involve the following features, whi ch are not found in most inland rivers: 
 
• The tidal regime and associated discharge may be mo dified by constriction of the natural waterway  
• Currents may flow in either direction 
• Structural components must withstand wave action 
• Scour may be enhanced by periodic current reversals . 
 
5.5.4.3 Tidal inlets 
 
Tidal crossings that restrict the waterway may redu ce the interior wave height and tidal range.  Howev er, they will 
also lead to higher velocities which may increase t he possibility of scour if the bed material is non- cohesive.  Such 
scour will continue until the resulting depths and velocities are compatible with particle size. 
--- Page 134 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  129 5.5.4.4 Estuaries 
 
The hydraulics of estuaries involve combinations of  river flow and tidal flow and the resulting differ ences in salinity 
affect both currents and sediment.  Under certain c onditions stratified fresh water and salt water can  flow 
simultaneously in opposite directions.  Moreover, s uspended sediment and solutions in the river water tend to 
flocculate and precipitate on contact with salt wat er and produce a soft mud bottom which makes founda tion 
construction and inspection difficult.  As little i s known about the scouring characteristics of these  sediments, scour 
predictions are not always reliable. 
 
In order to avoid constricting the natural waterway , the bridge opening should be able to accommodate the design 
river flood acting in conjunction with each of the following:  
 
• high tide plus storm surge where appropriate (for h igh-water level) 
• low tide 
• maximum tidal outflow 
 
Failure to meet the above requirements may give ris e to complex problems involving: 
 
• changes in tide levels 
• backwater effects during river floods 
• changes in current velocities and directions 
• changes in salinity current effects 
• scour. 
 
5.5.4.5 Open passages 
 
In the case of open passages, flow may result from differences between the tides at the two open ends.   In contrast to 
tidal inlets, the volume of water passing through t he opening during tidal changes is not fixed but de pends on 
differences in water level at the ends.  Partial cl osure using a bridge and causeway may lead to scour . 
 
5.5.4.6 Littoral drift 
 
Waves approaching a coast obliquely carry beach mat erial forward and up the shore as they advance and break.  The 
backwash drags this material back along a path whic h is nearly normal to the shore line.  The cumulati ve effect, 
known as littoral drift or long-shore drifting, is illustrated in Figure 5.41.   
 
Interference with the normal process of littoral dr ift by engineering works can have undesirable resul ts.  It is 
therefore necessary to check that bridge works are not affecting the natural process and that new coas tal works have 
not altered drift behaviour in a manner likely to p romote scour. 
 
--- Page 135 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  130 
 
--- Page 136 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  131 5.5.5 INLAND BASIN CROSSINGS 
 
Inland basins include lakes, reservoirs, bays, lago ons and swamps.  The following special problems ass ociated with 
crossings over these basins indicate that they are not inert bodies of water. 
 
• fluctuations in water level due to changes in the s tatic water level, wind set-up (see 5.5.5.3) and 
wave action 
 
• currents resulting from wind set-up, wave action, b asin outflow and through-flow.  These may be 
accompanied by sedimentary movements 
 
• constrictions due to causeway-bridge combinations w hich may inhibit natural flow and raise flood 
levels.  This may increase the flow velocity and le ad to scour. 
 
5.5.5.1 Hydraulic peculiarities of inland basin cro ssings 
 
Apart from flows that are generally nonperiodic, in land basin crossings have similar hydraulic feature s to those of 
the tidal crossings discussed in 5.5.4.  Where wind  set-up or set-down is a factor the flow can move i n either 
direction. 
 
Normally, the main hydraulic factors to be consider ed are: 
 
• superstructure clearance 
• local scour at piers 
• movements of debris 
• wave action at piers, abutments and approach embank ments. 
 
The freeboard on approach embankments should be suf ficient to accommodate the maximum static water lev el, wind 
set-up and wave uprush. 
 
5.5.5.2 Water-level fluctuations 
 
The static water level in a basin is affected by: 
 
• inflow and outflow 
• evaporation of the basin 
• precipitation on the basin 
 
Static water levels may be subject to seasonal and annual fluctuations, or changes in the controls gov erning inflow 
and outflow.  These may change with time. 
 
--- Page 137 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  132 5.5.5.3 Wind set-up 
 
Wind set-up is a rise in water level above the stat ic level.  This occurs downwind and is accompanied by a fall in 
level, or set-down, at the windward side of the bas in. 
 
As the name implies, it is caused by wind which she ars the water surface and produces waves and curren ts.  The 
water either returns along the bottom or at the sur face in the form of rip currents.  See Figure 5.42( a).  Wind set-up 
may occur separately in each of several interconnec ted basins as shown in Figure 5.42(b).  The resulti ng differences 
in water level can cause rapid flow through constri ctions. 
 
The height of wind set-up is influenced by the velo city, direction and duration of sustained wind as w ell as the fetch 
distance, depth of water and the shape of the basin .  Detailed procedures for its evaluation are given  in reference 
5.5.6. 
 
5.5.6 WAVES 
 
Although waves are generally considered to be gener ated by wind and by boats, they can also be created  by the 
sudden opening of gates at dams or locks.  In certa in coastal regions they may result from seismic act ivity.  At 
bridges, protection against wave damage is generall y concerned with those produced by wind. 
 
Because of the damage that waves can do to bridge p iers, abutments, causeways and embankments, attenti on should 
be given to the height, run-up forces and waves. 
 
Terms associated with waves are illustrated in Figu re 5.43. 
 
5.5.6.1 Oscillatory waves 
 
Oscillatory waves are produced when water is swept by wind.  In deep water the particles involved are not 
transported in the direction of wave propagation, b ut oscillate with uniform angular velocity about a mean position, 
in a vertical plane parallel to that direction.  Ne ar the surface, this orbit is virtually circular an d the resulting wave 
form is trochoidal.  This is illustrated in Figure 5.44(a) which also shows that although particles be low the surface 
move in phase with those at the surface, the size o f the orbits decreases with depth.  When the depth is more or less 
equal to the wavelength movement ceases altogether.   Along the coast this occurs at depths of about 18 0 m which 
corresponds to the continental shelf. 
 
As the waters shallow, the orbits acquire an ellipt ical shape and the resulting wave form becomes sinu soidal.  See 
Figure 5.44(b).  Although both the vertical and hor izontal amplitudes decrease with depth, the horizon tal amplitude 
does not normally disappear. 
Waves located within the area in which they are gen erated by wind are known as forced waves.  On enter ing waters 
in which there is no wind, they are called free wav es or swells.  In the ocean the wavelength of force d waves can 
vary between 60 and 180 m whilst that of swells can  be greater.  
 
--- Page 138 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  133 
 
--- Page 139 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  134 5.5.6.2 Translatory waves 
 
In a wave of translation, the wave not only advance s, but each particle of water over which it passes is moved 
forward during its passage and comes to rest ahead of its original position. 
 
 
 
Figure 5.44(c) shows that the motion of each partic le follows the path of a semi-ellipse with its majo r axis horizontal.  
The horizontal displacements are uniform whereas th e vertical displacements are proportional to the di stance above 
the bottom. 
 
Perfect waves of this kind are solitary and may be formed by the sudden opening of a sluice gate admit ting water into 
a long more or less level stretch of water such as a canal. 
 
5.5.6.3 The speed, length and height of waves 
 
i) Oscillatory waves 
 
The relationship between the various factors govern ing the speed, c w, of an oscillating wave is given 
by: 
 
cw = ((gL/2 π) x tanh (2 π d/L)) ½ (5.6) 
 
where 
 
L = wavelength (See Figure 5.43) 
d = depth from the still water level to the bottom.  
 
Provided that the depth of water exceeds half the w avelength, the velocity is 
more or less independent of the wavelength. 
 
ii) Translatory waves 
 
The speed of a wave of translation in still water i s approximately: 
 
cw = (g(d + 2a)) ½ (5.7) 
 
where 
 
a = wave amplitude (See Figure 5.44(c)) 
 
When the amplitude is comparatively small, this app roximates to: 
 
cw = √gd (5.8) 
 
These waves tend to break when their height is near ly equal to the depth of water. 
 
iii) Height 
 
The height, from trough to crest, of oscillatory wa ves normally varies up to about 9 m but can be 
much more in heavy seas. 
 
There is no definite relationship between wavelengt h and wave height because both are largely 
dependent on the wind speed and the distance travel led under its influence.  Molitor has proposed 
the following formula which includes the wind speed : 
 
Equations 5.9 to 5.12 have been metricated. 
 
--- Page 140 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  135 hw = 0,2706 (2,8097 + 0,1190 √ Wf-(f) 1/4 ) (5.9) 
 
where 
 
hw = wave height in m 
f = fetch or straight length of water subject to wi nd action in km 
W = wind speed in km/h 
 
For fetches of 32 km or more this simplifies 
 
hw = 0,0323 √Wf (5.10) 
 
Equations 5.9 and 5.10 are purely empirical and onl y apply to moderately deep water and gale force 
winds. 
 
5.5.6.4 Wave forces 
 
When oscillatory waves enter shallow waters where t he depth becomes less than half the wavelength, fri ction retards 
movement along the bottom while the top of the wave  runs on.  The front of the resulting wave of trans lation 
becomes increasingly steeper and eventually the cre st topples and breaks in the manner shown in Figure  5.45(a).  It 
is this forward movement followed by backwash which  causes erosion.  The undertow results partly from backwash 
from the spent waves and, in some cases, partly fro m wind set-up; previously described in 5.5.5.3. 
 
Undertow can transport finer sediment out to sea an d during storms, strong undertows can remove large quantities of 
beach. 
 
On exposed coasts, where deep water lies immediatel y offshore, even large waves do not break until the y reach the 
shoreline.  These non-breaking waves, which are sho wn in Figure 5.45(b), generally occur when the dept h of water 
in front of the cliff exceeds 1,5 times the wave he ight. 
 
Simplified functions, showing the behaviour of osci llatory waves at vertical walls, are given in Figur e 5.46. 
--- Page 141 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  136 

--- Page 142 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  137 It can be seen that the obstructed crest can reach a height equal to 1,33hW above the still water leve l and that the 
distribution of wave pressure is approximately tria ngular with the maximum unit pressure occurring at a height of 
0,125h w above the still water level.  The centre of applic ation of the wave force acts at a height of 0,375h w above the 
still water level. 
 
The maximum unit pressure is approximately: 
 
Pw = 7,182 h w kN/m2 (5.11) 
 
The area of the triangle represents the total force : 
 
Pw = (5 h w/3 x 7,182 h w )/2 
 = 5,9850 h w2kN/m (5.12) 
 
The forces exerted by the mass of water in large wa ves are impressive.  For example, 2,5 and 5,0 m hig h waves 
produce forces of 3,8 and 15,3 tons/m width respect ively.  These are sufficient to dislodge very large  rock fragments 
which are subsequently moved about and, in batterin g the shoreline, enhance the erosion process. 
 
5.5.6.5 Run-up and armour protection 
 
Wave run-up depends on the slope, porosity and roug hness of the embankment.  A surging wave on an 
impermeable slope can reach about three times the w ave height above still water level.  On the other h and, 
nonbreaking waves may reach up about 1,33 times the  wave height above still water level on contact wit h vertical 
walls. 
 
Run-up is reduced by using composite slopes, permea ble slopes and roughened slopes. 
 
Armour used to protect engineering works from wave action has evolved over a considerable period of ti me.  
Although the selection of armour units generally de pends on the wave forces, the steepness of the slop e and 
construction considerations; historical development s in materials and technology have influenced this choice in the 
past.  In the early stages, difficulties in placing  fitted stone blocks in deep water led to the use o f stone protection 
involving several layers of randomly placed rocks o r rip-rap.  The advent of Portland cement permitted  the 
fabrication of precast concrete units and, besides solid blocks, there emerged a variety of patented u nits, such as 
tribars, tetrapod and dolos.  These units are place d randomly and rely on their mass and interlock to resist wave 
forces and backwash.  Some wave energy is dissipate d within the interstitial voids.  More recently, a number of light 
armour units, including Sheds.  Cobs and Diodes, ha ve been developed.  These must be carefully placed in single 
interlocking layers and have very porous internal s tructures which serve to reduce their mass and diss ipate wave 
energy more efficiently.   
 
Guidance on the ability of various types of armour to meet specific situations is beyond the scope of this document.  
Further information may be obtained from references  5.5.6, 5.5.11 & 5.5.13. 
--- Page 143 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  138 5.6 UNDERWATER INSPECTIONS 
 
5.6.1 THE NEED OF UNDERWATER INSPECTION 
 
Because the life of a bridge depends on the preserv ation of the physical integrity of both the superst ructure and the 
substructure, all bridges with components in water too deep to allow adequate visual evaluation during  periods of 
low flow require underwater inspection.  This inclu des inconspicuous secondary structures because, no matter how 
unimportant they might be, a bridge that collapses becomes very conspicuous.  Moreover, the aging of s tructures, 
pollution, increased traffic loads and recent unusu al weather combine to make underwater inspection es sential. 
 
Historically, underwater inspection programmes have  been hindered by a number of misconceptions.  Cont rary to 
previous beliefs: 
 
• surface inspections are inadequate for assessing un derwater conditions 
• many bridge substructures are inaccessible at low w ater by wading 
• bridges do not have a guaranteed engineering life s pan 
• underwater inspections should control, not increase  costs. 
 
The purpose of inspection and preventive maintenanc e is to protect investments by detecting weaknesses  prior to 
imminent failure while the less expensive rehabilit ation measures are still feasible.  Repairs to an e xisting 
structure are nearly always cheaper than total repl acement. 
 
5.6.2 FREQUENCY OF UNDERWATER INSPECTIONS 
 
Most underwater inspections are performed separatel y from surface assessments.  However, because the u nderwater 
inspectors have a good vantage point, an examinatio n of the deck soffit and its junction with the subs tructure is often 
included.  The integration of information from surf ace and sub-surface inspections is an important fin al step in the 
assessment procedure.  It is advantageous to separa te the funding of surface and underwater inspection  programmes 
because the latter involve higher costs and time.  The personnel, procedures and equipment also differ .  
Nevertheless, the superstructure and substructure f unction as a unit and maintenance plans should be b ased on a 
combined evaluation of these two components. 
Underwater inspections should preferably be schedul ed for the most favourable period of the year.  Tha t is when the 
water and pollution levels are low and visibility i s good.  Although probably less favourable, inspect ions 
immediately after floods can yield information on s cour. 
 
5.6.2.1 Priorities 
 
Because road authorities may be responsible for man y bridges that cannot be inspected concurrently, a system of 
priorities is necessary in order to establish the s equence of underwater inspections.  The priorities reflect the level of 
concern regarding the safety of a bridge.  They inc lude factors such as age, construction, history, ma intenance 
history, significance of failure and deficiencies n oted on previous inspections.  Once an underwater i nspection 
programme has been initiated, bridges with a high p riority should be inspected within the first year a nd all bridges 
within five years. 
5.6.2.2 Inspection intervals 
 
The current state-of the-art has insufficient data on the pattern of underwater deterioration to permi t rigid 
recommendations on inspection intervals.  However, the necessary information becomes more apparent as inspection 
data are accumulated and a sufficiency should be av ailable after two or three inspection cycles.  At p resent, the best 
--- Page 144 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  139 approach is to complete a baseline evaluation of al l bridges, noting any damage.  Structures suffering  deterioration 
that does not require repair should be closely moni tored at intervals of less than five years.  The in itial first level of 
underwater inspection may be followed by a more tho rough inspection to evaluate problem areas. 
 
Supplementary inspections are required under specia l circumstances: 
 
• Following boat collisions 
• After severe floods 
• Before alterations to or reconstruction of the stru cture 
• Evaluation of contracted construction and/or mainte nance work 
 
5.6.2.3 Collisions 
 
Structural damage to submerged components of a brid ge can be either collision-related or the direct re sult of 
collision.  The inspection of collision damage shou ld be carried out as soon as possible while all phy sical evidence is 
still in optimal condition for discovery, identific ation, analysis and recording.  Delays may result i n some damage 
being obscured by fresh marine growth. 
 
The identity of the vessel involved should be deter mined if damages are to be requested.  However, the  owner of the 
structure may not be notified or may not be aware o f the collision.  Consequently, more frequent inspe ctions are 
required in the case of structures prone to collisi on damage. 
 
5.6.2.4 Storms 
 
Storms may inflict severe damage on bridge substruc tures.  The velocity of flow and depth of water inc rease with the 
intensity and duration of the storm.  Higher veloci ties increase a river’s ability to transport debris  which may 
accumulate on bridges.  Besides adding to the horiz ontal forces on structures, such accumulations conc entrate the 
flow and raise the scour potential.  Wind induced w aves and standing waves may also be harmful.   
 
An inspection of the waterway should therefore be c arried out after a major storm or flood.  Evidence of damage to 
the structural components, fenders and underwater i nstallations, such as submarine cables and cathodic  protection for 
steel piles, should be reported. 
--- Page 145 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  140 5.6.3 LEVELS OF INSPECTION 
 
Underwater inspections are classified into three le vels which are defined by the extent of the survey and 
measurements obtained.  Baseline inspections are mo st efficiently and economically performed using Lev el I 
techniques. 
 
5.6.3.1 Level I inspections 
 
Level I inspections consist of a “swim-by” or “swim -through” overview, with minimal cleaning of marine  growth.  
Although considerable use is made of visual examina tion, tactile examination may be necessary in turbi d water.  
Attention should be concentrated on the mean low wa ter level, the mud line, one or more intermediate d epths and 
damaged areas.  The quantity and type of any debris  attached to the structure should be noted.  This t ype of 
inspection is useful for verifying adherence to con struction plans or to detect obvious damage.  Probl ems identified 
by a level I assessment can be further evaluated by  a Level II inspection. 
 
5.6.3.2 Level II inspections 
 
Level II inspections are more detailed and are inte nded to obtain limited measurements of damaged or d eteriorated 
areas.  These may be obscured by marine growth whic h must be removed by cleaning.  Because this is tim e 
consuming, it is usually restricted to sample areas  of the entire structure.  These areas generally co nsist of 250 mm 
wide bands at designated levels. 
 
Instrumentation is usually simple:  consisting of s cales, callipers and graduated picks.  More precise  measurements 
using ultrasonic devices may sometimes be employed.  
 
Level II inspections are normally necessary for ass essing wooden or steel structures as well as evalua ting problems 
revealed during the course of Level I inspections.  Moreover, a random set of Level II measurements ca n be used to 
verify the results of a Level I inspection. 
 
5.6.3.3 Level III inspections 
 
Level III inspections are highly detailed and are i ntended to detect hidden damage or loss of cross-se ctional area and 
generally assess material homogeneity.  This may in volve non-destructive testing techniques, such as u ltrasound, or 
minimally destructive sampling procedures, such as the extraction of wood or concrete cores or in situ  hardness tests. 
 
5.6.4 ESTABLISHING AN UNDERWATER INSPECTION PROGRAM ME 
 
Two requirements are necessary for the establishmen t of an underwater inspection programme: 
 
• An accurate inventory of bridges with elements in w ater too deep to permit visual evaluation from the 
surface 
• A baseline assessment of the condition of the eleme nts.  
 
--- Page 146 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  141 5.6.4.1 The inventory 
 
The inventory is most conveniently stored within a computer system.  It should include the following i nformation: 
 
• The age of the structure 
• The construction materials, e.g. steel, concrete, t imber 
• The number and type of submerged elements, e.g. pil es and training walls 
• Water conditions, e.g. salt, heavy currents, corros ive pollution 
• Flood and/or collision damage 
• Maintenance history and modifications 
• Approximate traffic volume 
 
Although some of this information may not be availa ble at the outset, it should be acquired as soon as  possible 
because it is relevant to the following: 
 
• Evaluating bridge conditions 
• Planning diving schedules, inspection techniques an d underwater maintenance 
• Setting inspection priorities. 
 
This information should be incorporated into the ge neral bridge inventory. 
 
5.6.4.2 The baseline inspection 
 
The baseline inspection involves the inspection of all the submerged components of all bridges include d in the 
underwater inspection programme.  It provides a bas eline for scheduling future underwater inspections and assessing 
future deterioration. 
 
i) New bridges 
 
In the case of new bridges, the initial underwater assessment should comprise a pre-acceptance 
inspection following construction.  Because it occu rs before the appearance of marine growth, Level 
I inspection techniques are best suited for this pu rpose.  Moreover, this permits identification of 
construction related damage, poor workmanship and g eneral adherence to the drawings and 
specifications.  Inspection should preferably follo w various construction phases, such as pile driving , 
concrete placement and so on in order that defectiv e workmanship can be detected before it is 
obscured by marine growth.  Besides this, recogniti on of an authority’s ability to undertake such 
inspections generally improves the quality of const ruction. 
 
Suggestions for the future underwater inspection pr ogramme should be obtained from the designer; 
cognisance being given to the design concepts and t he local environment. 
 
--- Page 147 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  142 ii) Existing bridges 
 
The baseline inspection of existing bridges that ar e about to be incorporated into an inspection 
programme is concerned with the quality of original  construction as well as subsequent deterioration 
and structural damage.  Because this inspection may  take place some years after construction, it 
could be hampered by the presence of marine growth and a Level I inspection techniques may not be 
sufficient. 
 
iii) Sampling 
 
Although financial constraints suggest sampling tec hniques, this approach should be used with 
caution, because, at this stage, the aim is to eval uate the entire structure.  Besides risking and 
inadequate assessment, random sampling results in p roblems between continuously examining the 
same sample to monitor deteriorations or sampling d ifferent elements in successive inspections in 
order to eventually cover the whole bridge.  Consid eration must also be given to variations in the 
quality of materials and workmanship as well as the  conditions of service.  For instance, the piers 
supporting a particular bridge may have different b ottom conditions, depths, currents or accidental 
damage.  Additionally, variations in the quality of  piles might remain undetected if only sample 
bents are inspected. 
 
If pile sampling is unavoidable, the inspection pro gramme should be sufficiently flexible to permit 
examination of more piles should a problem be detec ted. 
 
5.6.5 THE UNDERWATER INSPECTION TEAM 
 
Bridge authorities should evaluate their underwater  inspection requirements and then select one of the  following 
three options for performing the inspection: 
 
• put the work out to contract 
• use in-house personnel 
• use a combination of these two. 
 
The decision of an authority to use either contract  divers or an in-house team will be influenced by t he number of 
bridges with major underwater components.  In gener al, inland authorities would not develop an underwa ter 
inspection capability. 
 
5.6.5.1 Contract inspections 
 
Contract inspections may be let to diving firms or engineering firms involved in underwater inspection . 
 
i) Engineering firms 
 
Engineering firms may offer engineer divers or engi neer supervision of trained divers functioning as 
technicians or they may subcontract a diving firm.  The ability of these firms to provide detailed 
assessments of structures can be useful where maint enance and repair are found to be necessary.  At 
the same time, if this assessment capability duplic ates expertise already present with the bridge 
authority, it would be less costly to hire a diving  firm that working with agency staff. 
 
ii) Diving contracts 
 
Diving firms engaged in underwater inspection may e ither report directly to agency engineers or 
employ their own engineering consultants.  The curr ent availability of television, video cameras, 
photography and so on, enables organisations to emp loy commercial divers who are not qualified 
professional engineers.  However, such persons shou ld be well trained in structural inspection and 
should be supervised by a professional engineer fam iliar with bridge structures and their capabilities .  
--- Page 148 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  143 In the selection of diving contractors experience i n underwater inspection is more important than 
knowledge of salvage or construction operations. 
 
iii) Advantages 
 
The advantages of contracted inspections include 
 
• elimination of the need to recruit train and mainta in an in-house diving team 
• elimination of the need to purchase and maintain di ving equipment 
• reduction in the idle time of equipment when the te am is not actively engaged in diving operations 
• opportunity to specify the range of work, qualifica tions, equipment and so on in each contract 
• elimination of problems associated with the assignm ent of the in-house team to other work. 
 
iv) Disadvantages 
 
Disadvantages include: 
 
• slow response to emergencies 
• difficulties in bidding for engineering services 
• the letting of annual contracts 
• assignment of responsibility for the engineering co ntent on the inspection report. 
 
v) Diving contracts 
 
The appointment of an in-house representative with the appropriate knowledge and experience will 
help to avoid many of the pitfalls in contracting u nderwater inspections.  This person should play a 
leading role in contractor selection and negotiatio ns.  The requirements and expectations of agencies 
should be clearly defined before negotiations comme nce. 
 
Requests for bids for contract diving should indica te the qualifications required both for diving and 
bridge inspection.  The location and nature of the structure, as well as the water depth should also b e 
given along with the site and amount of special equ ipment.  Bids should contain rates (hourly, daily, 
weekly) for various operations and charges for spec ial tools and equipment.  Some flexibility should 
be included in the inspection contract since renego tiation may be difficult if the inspection findings  
are unexpected and require a change in plan. 
 
5.6.5.2 In-house inspections 
 
The selection of diving personnel and the compositi on of an in-house diving team is important. 
i) Personnel 
 
An in-house diving team should not be formed from m inimally trained personnel who have limited 
diving experience, restricted to recreational setti ngs.  Besides differences in diving conditions such  
as poor visibility, underwater obstacles and unpred ictable currents, diving techniques also differ.  
Commercial divers use heavy weights for negative, n ot neutral buoyancy; depth sounding instead of 
gauge-determined depth; and line tending and safety  divers rather than buddy diving.  Most 
inexperienced divers spend too much time adapting t o the diving conditions to do an effective job 
whereas properly trained divers can perform a good inspection safely. 
 
ii) Composition of the team 
 
The use of employees with other duties as part-time  divers is not altogether satisfactory since they 
may not maintain familiarity with procedures necess ary for effective inspections.  The practice may 
also result in the diving team having to constantly  compete for personnel.  However, continuity may 
be achieved through use of a core of full-time dive rs supplemented by part-time employees.  The 
full-time personnel can also train and supervise pa rt-time staff. 
--- Page 149 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  144  
a) Advantages 
 
Amongst the advantages of using an in-house diving team are: 
 
• total control of the inspection operations 
• opportunity to train inspectors in specific problem s of design, construction and materials 
• use of the diving team for the inspection of new co nstruction 
• accumulation of experience and knowledge from repea ted dives during scheduled 
inspections. 
 
b) Disadvantages  
 
Disadvantages include: 
 
• the capability of the team may sometimes be exceede d 
• the provision of alternative assignments for team m embers not qualified for other tasks. 
 
It is beneficial for an authority to use its own li mited, in-house diving team for routine, 
construction and emergency inspections with backup contract divers for additional work. 
 
5.6.6 DIVING TECHNIQUES 
 
A wide variety of individual capabilities, equipmen t and techniques are used in diving operations.  In  general, 
however, the two techniques illustrated in Figures 5.4.7(a) and (b) are used for bridge inspection: 
 
• SCUBA (self-contained underwater breathing apparatu s) diving 
• surface supplied/tended air or mixed gas diving, co mmonly referred to as SDDE or HOOKAH. 
 
Both these techniques use air as the breathing medi um.  Mixed gas and the deep-sea equipment shown in Figure 5.47 
(c) are seldom required and are not discussed here.   Closed circuit or semi-closed circuit equipment i s not permitted. 
 
5.6.6.1 Scuba diving 
 
The scuba diver carries a portable air supply and h as freedom of movement because there is no surface connection or 
umbilical cord.  The method is ideal for inspection s requiring considerable mobility or where many div es of short 
duration in different locations are required instea d of one sustained dive.  Surface support systems a re minimal. 
Scuba divers should avoid jagged rock and coral as well as conditions of poor visibility.  Limitations  are imposed by 
depth, air supply and lack of protective clothing.  The lack of vocal communication with the surface a lso causes 
problems, as do currents which exceed 0,5 m/sec.  T he diving team should comprise at least four men in cluding a 
buddy and a standby diver. 
 
The maximum sustained working depth for scuba divin g is generally about 18 m although experienced dive rs can 
sustain 37 m for short period.  A single 2 m3 tank will enable a diver to work at a depth of 9 m for a bout one hour.  
However, air consumption varies with each individua l and the depth of water, which increases exertion.  
 
Further information is contained in SATS Diving Ins tructions. 
 
--- Page 150 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  145 
 
--- Page 151 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  146 5.6.6.2 Surface supplied/tended air diving 
 
In this type of operation, air is supplied from the  surface to the diver by a flexible hose.  Besides the air hoses, a 
communication cable, lifeline and a pneumatic fatho meter are usually attached to the diver.  A diver m ay use either 
heavy weight (hard-hat) equipment with a dry suit o r light weight (face sealing mask) equipment with a  wet, dry or 
hot-water suit. 
 
The main disadvantage of this type of diving operat ion is the size and weight of the support system wh ich comprises 
the compressor hoses and lines.  This presents tran sportation problems and a comparatively large suppo rt craft is 
necessary.  Additional disadvantages arise from the  comparative lack of physical protection and limite d vertical 
mobility.  The diving team should include a standby  diver. 
 
On the other hand, the benefits include an unlimite d air supply, good horizontal mobility and good voi ce or line pull 
communications. 
 
The depth and duration of the dive depends on the t ype of equipment.  This varies between a normal wor king depth 
of 18 m for 60 minutes to a maximum of 27 m for 30 minutes.  These figures can be extended to 58 m for  60 minutes 
if special equipment is available.  The current sho uld not exceed 1,3 m/sec. and the diver should norm ally be able to 
free ascend to the surface. 
 
Further information may be found in the SATS Diving  Instructions. 
 
5.6.7 DIVING EQUIPMENT 
 
Diving equipment should have a proven record of rel iability and should comply with the latest edition of the South 
African Bureau of Standards (SABS) “Code of Practic e for the selection, use and maintenance of underwa ter diving 
equipment”.  
 
5.6.7.1 Scuba diving 
 
i) Minimum diver’s equipment 
 
The minimum equipment requirement of a scuba diver comprises:  an open circuit SCUBA, a safety 
vest, a weight belt, knife, face mask and swim fins . 
--- Page 152 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  147 ii) Operational equipment 
 
Routine inspections involving scuba diving typicall y require the following operational equipment: 
 
• a vehicle to transport the equipment 
• a boat, motor and trailer 
• anchors, mooring lines and life preservers 
• diving flag 
• chipping hammers, picks, levers, probes and scraper s 
• underwater lights 
• underwater slates for recording data 
• underwater camera 
• yardstick, tapes, callipers and other measuring dev ices. 
 
5.6.7.2. Surface supplied/tended air diving 
 
i) Minimum diver’s equipment 
 
The minimum equipment requirement consists of diver ’s mask, such as the USN MK1 or the Jack 
Browne mask, a wet suit, a weight belt, knife, swim  fins or shoes and the umbilical system. 
 
ii) Operational equipment 
 
Besides the operational equipment listed in 5.6.1(i i), inspections involving surface supplied/tended 
air diving require: 
 
• A larger transport vehicle 
• A larger boat 
• A compressor and receiver tank 
• Air hose, communications cable, lifeline and pneuma tic fathometer  
• A diver’s radio. 
 
5.6.7.3 Maintenance and care of equipment 
 
Portable diving equipment should be stored under lo ck and key when not in use. 
 
The equipment should be maintained in accordance wi th the latest editions of SABS 0127 and SABS 019, “ Code of 
Practice for portable metal containers for compress ed gases:  Basic design criteria, use and maintenan ce”. 
 
State Regulations require that diving equipment be examined and tested by a competent person. 
 
Further information is contained in the SATS Diving  Instructions. 
 
5.6.8 UNDERWATER SAFETY 
 
5.6.8.1 Government Regulations 
 
All diving operations should comply with regulation s enforced by the Machinery and Occupational Safety  Act, (Act 
No. 6, 1983), namely the Regulations Concerning Div ing Work covered by Chapter 7 of the Factories, Mac hinery 
--- Page 153 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  148 and Building Act, (Act No. 22, 1941), and appearing  in the Government Gazette No. 3215 of July 16 th  1971, as 
amended. 
 
5.6.8.2 Hazards and accidents 
 
Considerable guidance on safety and operational pro cedures may be obtained from the SATS Diving Instru ctions. 
 
Divers are confronted by natural forces and physica l effects which impose definite limits and may caus e serious 
accidents.  Safety requires knowledge of these fact ors and the ability to recognise and respond to the m.  Divers must 
be able to evaluate the environment, specific divin g equipment and their own condition relative to the  task being 
performed.  Many potential dangers are usually comp letely avoidable. 
 
The various hazards and accidents to which divers a re vulnerable include the following (not listed in either 
importance or frequency of occurrence): 
 
• respiratory accidents  
• oxygen poisoning and nitrogen narcosis 
• bleeding 
• overexertion and exhaustion 
• fainting, hypothermia and other medical conditions 
• physical injury 
• squeeze, gas expansion and blow-up 
• loss of communication 
• fouling 
• polluted water and noxious air 
• tides and currents 
• marine life 
• marine traffic and floating debris 
--- Page 154 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  149 5.6.8.3 Precautions  
 
Each type of diving operation requires its own safe ty precautions.  General precautions that apply to all operations 
include the following recommendations: 
 
• thoroughly plan each diving operation in advance an d ensure that it is adequately supervised 
 
• train diving personnel thoroughly in the use of equ ipment and ensure that they are physically fit 
 
• use good quality equipment that has been designed a nd thoroughly tested and inspected before use  
 
• follow all safety precautions during the diving ope ration. 
 
Employees should have manuals of safe practices ava ilable at diving locations.  For each type of divin g operation 
the manual should cover: 
• safety procedures and operational checklists 
• the assignments and responsibilities of the diving team 
• equipment procedures and checklists 
• emergency procedures for equipment failures, advers e conditions and diver illness or injury. 
 
5.6.9 UNDERWATER INSPECTION PROCEDURE 
 
Diving regulations and safety measures should be ad hered to at all times during the course of underwat er inspection. 
 
The inspection of the underwater structural compone nts of a bridge and its waterway should be conducte d in a 
systematic and organised manner that is efficient a nd will minimise the possibility of any item being overlooked.  A 
suggested inspection sequence is given in 5.6.9.4. 
 
5.6.9.1 Preparation 
 
Proper planning and preparation will save considera ble field time and result in a more accurate inspec tion.  Before 
starting, the inspection team should become familia r with the construction of the bridge and the site conditions.  The 
following items should be considered: 
 
• the nature of the inspection (baseline, routine, su pplementary) 
 
• the level of inspection (swim-through, detailed, hi ghly detailed) 
 
• examination of all pertinent information on the bri dge, such as completion drawings, specifications, 
previous inspections and previous repairs 
 
• diving technique (SCUBA, surface supported/tended a ir supply) 
 
• manpower, equipment and materials requirements 
 
• communications system (line pull, voice, closed cir cuit TV) 
 
• recording equipment (slate, notebooks, standard for ms, photographs, video etc.) 
--- Page 155 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  150  
• the inspection procedure. 
 
5.6.9.2 Cleaning 
 
Some cleaning or removal of marine growth from unde rwater components to facilitate inspection is nearl y always 
required.  The extent of cleaning depends on the am ount of growth and the type of inspection.  Unneces sary cleaning 
should be avoided because it is arduous, time consu ming and removes growth that generally affords some  protection 
against deterioration.   
 
Various tools may be used to remove marine growth u nderwater.  These range from a diver’s knife and ha nd tools, 
such as chipping hammers or scrapers, for light cle aning, to power tools, such as pneumatic or hydraul ic-powered 
chippers, grinders and brushes, for removing thick,  hard growth.  Sand or water blasters may also be u sed. 
 
High pressure water blasters with discharge pressur es of 100 MPa are effective for tough cleaning jobs  on steel and 
concrete.  The gun flow is divided into a primary f low for cleaning and a secondary flow to counteract  the jet.  
Because the high-pressure models can cut through so und concrete, they are potentially dangerous.  Cons equently a 
diver must exercise care in directing the nozzle aw ay from him and others as it removes muscle and bon e more easily 
than marine organisms!  For similar reasons, these blasters should not be used on timber. 
 
The hydraulic-power brush systems, developed for cl eaning ship hulls, may also be used to clean large areas of 
underwater components. 
 
5.6.9.3 Detection 
 
Depending on visibility below the water surface div ers may detect flaws, damage and deterioration thro ugh visual 
examination or tactile examination. 
 
i) Visual examination 
 
Visual examination, which is the primary method of underwater inspection, can be limited by water 
clarity.  Although artificial lighting can be used to improve conditions of poor visibility, its use i s 
limited in turbid waters because high turbidity ref lects direct light and reduces visibility. 
--- Page 156 ---
UNRA Asset Management System  BMS Inspection Manual 
June 2012  151 ii) Tactile examination 
 
Under conditions of zero-visibility, or dark water,  the diver must touch and feel in order to detect 
flaws, damage or deterioration.  This can be very d ifficult if the water is cold, or the currents are 
strong or the marine growth is abundant.  It is nor mally difficult, if not impossible to qualitatively  
assess damage using this technique.  Under these co nditions the inspector should have a very sound 
knowledge of the completion drawings as well as goo d communications with the surface.  These 
limitations may be offset by use of the non-destruc tive testing and other techniques described in 
Section 5.2. 
 
5.6.9.4 Inspection sequence 
 
The sequence of inspection will depend on the circu mstances.  However, one logical order might be: 
 
• abutments 
• piers 
• training walls 
• embankments, spurs and dikes.
